No Benefit Before Compensation in Usurpation
States the principle and proves with hadith and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on benefiting from usurped property before compensation and explain why istihsan overrides the qiyas that would allow it.

Core Idea
Usurper cannot benefit before compensating—istihsan overrides the qiyas that would permit it.

This passage addresses a key question in ghasp (usurpation): may the usurper use the property before making good the loss? The original analogy suggests yes, but the Hanafi school adopts a stricter view based on juristic preference (istihsan) supported by a hadith of Ali (ra). Understanding this ruling clarifies the school's approach to balancing analogy and revealed evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The act is benefiting from usurped property (e.g., cooking, grinding, sowing) before compensating the owner. The ruling is that it is not permissible (makruh tahrimi).
This applies to any form of use, even if the usurper intends to pay later.
2
Locate the evidence
Identify the proof cited. The commentator quotes a hadith from Ali (ra): 'Feed it to the prisoners' regarding a sheep slaughtered without permission. This command implies the usurper has no right to benefit.
The hadith is ahad (single narration) but deemed reliable enough for istihsan.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the prohibition? The command to give the meat in charity shows that the usurper's ownership is not established; otherwise he would be allowed to keep it. The prohibition of benefit is immediate, not after compensation.
The underlying principle: the usurper has not acquired ownership and thus cannot benefit until the owner is satisfied.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
What is the opposing view? Zufar, al-Hasan, and a narration from Abu Hanifa hold that benefit is allowed before compensation, based on qiyas (analogy) with other cases of liability. The commentator presents this as the qiyas position.
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, there can be differences based on method.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Why is the istihsan view preferred? Istihsan is a stronger source in this case because it is based on a specific hadith and serves the public interest (preventing misuse). The commentator sides with the istihsan view, which is the mufta bihi position.
Tarjih is based on the authority of the hadith and the principle of preferring explicit evidence over analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بلا حل انتفاع قبل أداء الضمان بشيء، وطبخ وطحن وزرع واتخاذ سيف أو إناء بغير الحجرين، وبناء على ساجة، ولو ذبح شاة، أو خرق ثوبا فاحشيا ضمن القيمة،
بلا حل انتفاع إلخ : أي وقبل الإبراء وتضمين المالك أو الحاكم القيمة، وبعد وجود واحد منها يحل، وكان القياس أن يجوز الانتفاع به قبل أن يوجد واحد منها، وهو قول زفر والحسن، ورواية عن أبي حنيفة، ولكن الاستحسان: أن لا يجوز؛ لقوله عليا في الشاة المذبوحة بغير رضاء صاحبها: "أطعموها الأسارى، أخرجه الدار قطني، ولقد أفاد هذا الأمر بالتصدق وزوال ملك المالك، وحرمة الانتفاع للغاصب قبل الإرضاء.
Without lawful benefit before fulfilling the guarantee in any way, and cooking, grinding, sowing, making a sword or vessel without the two stones [gold and silver], and building on a plank, and if he slaughters a sheep, or tears a garment excessively, he is liable for the value. [Commentary:] 'Without lawful benefit etc.' means before the owner or judge grants release and assesses the value, and after one of these happens it becomes lawful. The analogy was that it is permissible to benefit before any of these occurs, which is the view of Zufar and al-Hasan, and a narration from Abu Hanifa. But istihsan [juristic preference] is that it is not permissible, due to the saying of Ali regarding the sheep slaughtered without the consent of its owner: 'Feed it to the prisoners,' related by al-Daraqutni, and this command to give charity indicates loss of the owner's ownership and prohibition of benefit for the usurper before satisfaction.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage states the general rule that a usurper may not benefit from the usurped property until he has compensated the owner or the owner/appointed judge has assessed the value. The commentator explains the ruling, noting that the original analogy (qiyas) would allow benefit before compensation, which was the view of Zufar, al-Hasan, and a narration from Abu Hanifa. However, the relied-upon ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) that prohibits it. The proof is a hadith from Ali (Allah be pleased with him) concerning a sheep that was slaughtered without the owner's permission: he ordered that it be fed to prisoners, indicating that the owner's ownership has ceased and the usurper may not benefit without the owner's consent.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that sets aside a qiyas (analogy) in favor of a stronger evidence or public interest.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or compensation; here refers to the liability of the usurper to pay the value or return the property.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction; in this lesson, the qiyas would allow benefit before compensation.
غصب (ghasp): Usurpation; taking another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible for a usurper to benefit from the usurped property before compensating the owner based on the hadith mentioned?
A) Because the hadith commands the usurper to destroy the property.
B) Because the hadith commands giving the property in charity, implying the usurper has no right of benefit.
C) Because the owner's ownership remains fully intact until compensation.
D) Because the usurper becomes the owner immediately upon taking.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits a usurper from benefiting from usurped property before compensating the owner, relying on istihsan over qiyas, as proven by the hadith of Ali.

Slaughter or Tear: Owner's Option for Value or Depreciation
Explains the owner's option when usurper slaughters or tears property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the owner's option when a usurper partially destroys property and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding claiming depreciation after taking the slaughtered meat.

Core Idea
When a usurper partially destroys property, the owner may claim full value or take the item and claim depreciation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with a common scenario: a usurper slaughters someone's sheep or makes a large tear in their garment. The law grants the owner a choice: either demand full compensation and leave the damaged item with the usurper, or take the item back and claim the reduction in value (tadmin al-nuqsan). However, there is a difference within the Hanafi school on whether taking the slaughtered meat forfeits the right to claim depreciation. Let's examine the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The usurper's acts (slaughter, excessive tear) are partial destruction because some utility remains (e.g., meat, usable fabric). This gives the owner a choice.
Partial destruction contrasts with total consumption (istiklak).
2
State the owner's two options
The owner may either claim the full value of the property and surrender it to the usurper, or claim the depreciation (nuqsan) and take the damaged item back.
The option is called takhyir.
3
Understand the underlying illa (reason)
The option exists because the damage is not total; some benefit remains. Therefore the law gives the owner flexibility to choose what compensates him best.
This illa supports the majority view.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reports from Abu Hanifa that if the owner takes the slaughtered meat, he cannot claim depreciation because slaughter and skinning add value (increase). The preferred view (zahir al-riwayah) holds that the owner may still claim depreciation.
The dispute centers on whether the meat is considered an 'increase' that offsets the damage.
5
Distinguish from total consumption cases
For inedible animals (e.g., donkey), cutting a limb is total consumption, so full value is due and the owner takes nothing. For a slave, cutting a limb results in half value with the owner retaining the slave.
This shows the principle is consistent: partial destruction gives option; total destruction gives full compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبح شاة إلخ: يعني لو ذبح رجل شاة لآخر، أو خرق ثوبا لغيره خرقا فاحشيا، ضمن المالك الغاصب قيمة الشاة أو قيمة الثوب، وسلم المالك المغصوب إلى الغاصب إن شاء، وإن شاء ضمن الغاصب النقصان، وأخذ الشاة أو الثوب، وكذا إذا قطع يد الشاة أو رجلها يخير المالك؛ لأن هذه الأشياء إتلاف من وجه باعتبار فوت بعض الأغراض من الحمل والدر والنسل، وفوت بعض المنفعة في الثوب، فيخير بين تضمين جميع قيمته وتركه له، وبين تضمين نقصانه، وأخذه، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه ليس له أن يضمنه النقصان إذا أخذ اللحم؛ لأن الذبح والسلخ زيادة فيها، والأول هو الظاهر، ولو كانت الدابة غير مأكولة اللحم، يضمن قاطع الطرف جميع قيمته؛ لأنه استهلاك من كل وجه بخلاف قطع طرف العبد، حيث يضمن نصف قيمته مع أخذه.
And if he slaughters a sheep etc.: meaning if a man slaughters another's sheep, or tears another's garment excessively, the owner can hold the usurper liable for the value of the sheep or garment and hand over the usurped property to the usurper if he wishes, or if he wishes he can hold him liable for the depreciation and take the sheep or garment. Similarly if he cuts off the sheep's hand or leg, the owner has a choice; because these things are partial destruction considering the loss of some purposes like carrying, milking, and offspring, and loss of some utility in the garment, so he may choose between claiming full value and leaving it with him, or claiming depreciation and taking it. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that he may not claim the depreciation if he takes the meat, because slaughter and skinning are an increase. The first is apparent. And if the animal is not of edible meat, the cutter of the limb guarantees the full value, because it is total consumption, unlike cutting the limb of a slave, where he guarantees half its value along with taking it.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage examines the case where a usurper slaughters an edible animal or makes a large tear in a garment. The owner has an option: either take the full value from the usurper and let him keep the damaged item, or claim the depreciation (the reduction in value) and take the item back. The same applies if the usurper cuts a limb of the animal. The reasoning is that such acts are partial destruction—some utility remains, so the owner is given a choice. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reports from Abu Hanifa that if the owner takes the meat (of a slaughtered sheep), he cannot claim depreciation because slaughter and skinning add value (e.g., the meat becomes edible). However, the preferred view (al-Zahir) is that the owner may claim depreciation. If the animal is inedible (e.g., a donkey), cutting a limb results in full compensation because it is total waste, unlike cutting a slave's limb where only half the value is due but the slave is not taken by the usurper.

Key Terms
تضمين النقصان (tadmin al-nuqsan): Claiming compensation for the reduction in value of the damaged property.
فاحش (fahish): Excessive or gross damage that affects the utility of the item.
إتلاف من وجه (itlaf min wajh): Partial destruction; damage that leaves some utility intact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the owner's option when a usurper slaughters his sheep (which is a partial destruction)?
A) The owner must take the full value and forfeit the meat.
B) The owner may either take the full value and leave the meat with the usurper, or take the meat and claim the depreciation.
C) The owner must take the meat and cannot claim any compensation.
D) The owner may only claim the value of the meat, not the depreciation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a usurper partially destroys property, the owner has a choice between taking full value or taking the item plus depreciation, with the majority view allowing this choice even after taking the slaughtered meat of an edible animal.

Building on a Usurped Plank: Ownership Transfer Conditions
Ruling on when building on a usurped plank transfers ownership of the plank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when a usurper's building on a stolen plank transfers ownership, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Harm prevention justifies transferring property rights, with compensation replacing restitution.

This passage examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over a usurper who builds on a stolen plank. The Hanafi school, prioritizing harm prevention and relying on the maxim 'no harm nor mutual harm', rules that the owner's right to reclaim is severed if the building's value exceeds the plank's, while the Shafi'i school insists on the owner's absolute right to reclaim their property. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how Hanafi usul balances property rights against wider social harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two scenarios based on relative values. When the building's value exceeds the plank's, the usurper owns the plank but must compensate; when the plank is more valuable, the owner may take it. In neither case is the building demolished.
Note the condition: ownership transfer is conditioned on building value > plank value.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i holds that the owner's right is never severed; the owner may demolish the building and reclaim the plank in all cases.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i rely on the general principle: if a person finds their specific property, they are more entitled to it, based on textual evidence (e.g., 'The owner of a lost item who finds it is more entitled').
The passage does not quote the exact Shafi'i proof but notes 'by text'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response applies the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' and istihsan: demolishing the building would cause harm to the usurper, and the owner's harm is compensable via payment of value. Analogies: sewing a stolen thread into a human belly or inserting a plank into a ship at sea, where removal causes greater harm.
This is based on preference for preventing harm over strict property right.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, the usurper's building cannot be demolished; in the Shafi'i school, the owner can always demolish and reclaim the plank.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبناء على ساجة [مفرد ساج شجر عظيم قوي ينبت ببلاد الهند (عيني)] أي ملك الغاصب بلا حل انتفاع قبل اداء الضمان ببناء الغاصب على ساجة ويزول ملك مالكها ويلزم الغاصب قيمتها قال في "الذخيرة" هذا فيما إذا كان قيمة البناء أكثر من قيمة الساجة، وأما إذا كان قيمة الساجة أكثر من قيمة البناء، فلا يملكها، وله أخذها. وذكر الكرخي: أن وضع المسألة فيما إذا أدخل الساجة في بنائه بأن بني حولها لا عليها، وأما إذا بنى على الساجة فيهدم للرد ولكن هذا ضعيف كذا في "الكافي"، وعند الشافعي: لا ينقطع حق المالك كيفما كان، فيهدم البناء ويأخذ ساجته؛ لأنه وجد عين ماله فكان أحق به بالنص، وعندنا انقطع حقه مطلقا في الصحيح؛ لأن في قلعه ضررا بالغاصب، قال علي: "لا ضرر ولا ضرار في الإسلام، وضرر المالك محبور بضمان القيمة، فلا يعد ضررا، فصار كما إذا خاط بالخيط المغصوب بطن آدمي أو أدخل لوحا مغصوبا في السفينة، وكان في لحج البحر.
And building on a plank [plank: a large strong tree that grows in India (Ayni)] i.e. the usurper owns [the plank] without lawful benefit before fulfilling the guarantee by the usurper's building on the plank, and the owner's ownership ceases, and the usurper is liable for its value. It is said in al-Dhakhira: This is when the value of the building is more than the value of the plank. But if the value of the plank is more than the value of the building, he does not own it, and the owner may take it. Al-Karkhi mentioned that the scenario applies when he inserts the plank into his building by building around it not on it. But if he builds on top of the plank, it is demolished for return, but this is weak, as in al-Kafi. According to al-Shafi'i: the owner's right is not severed in any case; he demolishes the building and takes his plank, because he finds his property and is more entitled to it by text. According to us: his right is severed absolutely in the correct view, because demolishing it would harm the usurper. Ali said: 'There is no harm nor mutual harm in Islam.' The owner's harm is compensated by guaranteeing the value, so it is not considered harm. Thus it becomes like sewing a stolen thread into a human belly or inserting a stolen plank into a ship that is in the middle of the sea.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a usurper builds on a stolen plank (sajjah). The commentator explains that if the value of the building (the constructed part) exceeds the value of the plank, the usurper becomes owner of the plank, but he must pay its value. If the plank is more valuable, the owner may take it back. There is a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds that the owner always retains the right to demolish and reclaim. The Hanafi position, based on the hadith 'No harm nor mutual harm' and on istihsan, is that the owner's right is severed because forcing demolition would cause harm to the usurper, and the owner's loss is compensated by the value. This is analogous to sewing a stolen thread into a person's wound or inserting a plank into a ship at sea, where removal would cause greater harm.

Key Terms
ساجة (sajjah): A plank of wood from the Indian sāj tree, used for building.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): Prophetic hadith: 'There is no harm nor mutual harm.' Used as a legal maxim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, when does the usurper become owner of the stolen plank used in his building?
A) Always, regardless of the plank's value.
B) Only if the building's value is greater than the plank's value.
C) Only if the plank is of an Indian tree.
D) Never, the owner always retains the right to demolish.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes preventing harm over strict property rights, allowing the usurper to keep the building in exchange for compensation when the building's value exceeds the stolen material's value.

Planting or Building in Another's Land: Removal and Compensation
Ruling on trees or buildings placed on another's land without permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on plants/buildings placed on another's land without permission, and explain the compensation rule when removal damages the land.

Core Idea
A wrongful root has no right: the encroacher must remove and compensate for devaluation.

In Hanafi fiqh, the principle 'no right for a wrongful root' (laysa li 'irqin zalimin haqq) governs encroachments on property. This lesson examines the specific ruling when someone plants trees or builds on another's land without permission. The remedy is removal of the encroachment and return of the land; but what if removal damages the land? The Hanafi school provides a fair compensation mechanism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act (planting/building on another's land)? What is the hukm (obligation to remove)?
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith of Ali: 'There is no right for a wrongful root.' Understand its linguistic form.
The hadith is a zanni text but clear in meaning.
3
Understand the compensation logic
Why is compensation only for the value as removed (mamlu'a) and not as standing? Because the encroacher has no right to retain the items.
The landowner may claim for land devaluation separately.
4
Note the analogy
The text analogizes to filling another's container with food: the container is returned and the food is removed.
This clarifies that land is not consumed; it remains the owner's.
5
Apply to similar cases
How does this ruling apply to modern situations like unauthorized construction or planting?
The same principle applies to any encroachment that can be removed without destroying the land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غرس أو بنى في أرض الغير، قلعا وردت وإن نقصت الأرض بالقلع، ضمن له البناء والغرس مقلوعا، فيكون له، أي الغرس والبناء لصاحب الأرض القيمة. [رمز الحقائق: 328/2] قلعا وردت أي قلع البناء والغرس وردت الأرض إلى صاحبها؛ لقوله علي: "ليس لعرق ظالم حق"، أي لذي عرق ظالم، وصف العرق بصفة صاحبه، وهو الظلم مجازا، كما يقال صام نهاره، وقام ليله. ولأن الأرض باقية على ملكه إذا لم تكن مستهلكة، ولا مغصوبة حقيقة، ولم يوجد فيها شيء يوجب الملك للغاصب، فيؤمر بتفريغها، وردها إلى مالكها كما إذا شغل ظرف غيره بالطعام. [تكملة البحر الرائق: 232/8] ضمن له إلخ : أي إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع كان لصاحب الأرض أن يضمن للغاصب قيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعا، ويكونان له؛ لأن فيه دفع الضرر عنهما، فتعين فيه النظر لهما، وإنما يضمن قيمتها مقلوعا؛ لأنه مستحق للقلع، وليس له أن يستديم فيها، فتعتبر قيمته في ذلك الوقت مقلوعا.
And if he plants or builds in another's land, they [the plants and building] are removed and the land is returned, even if the land is diminished by the removal, the owner is compensated for the building and plants as removed items, so they belong to the landowner. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/328] 'Removed and returned' means the building and plants are removed and the land is returned to its owner due to the saying of Ali: 'There is no right for a wrongful root' i.e. for a wrongful root owner, describing the root with the attribute of its owner, which is oppression metaphorically, as it is said 'he fasted his day, he prayed his night.' And because the land remains in his ownership if it is not consumed, nor truly usurped, and nothing exists therein that necessitates ownership for the usurper, so he is ordered to vacate it and return it to its owner, just as if he filled another's container with food. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/232] 'He is compensated etc.' means that if the land is diminished by removal, the landowner may claim from the usurper the value of the building and plants as removed items, and they become his; because this prevents harm from them, so consideration is given to them. He only compensates their value as removed items because they are due for removal and he may not keep them, so their value at the time of removal is considered.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a person plants trees or builds a structure on another's land without permission. The ruling is that the plants and building must be removed and the land returned to its owner. If the removal causes damage to the land (e.g., the value decreases), the landowner can claim compensation from the usurper/planter/builder for the value of the removed items (i.e., what they would be worth if taken out). This compensation is for the items as removed (not as standing), because the planter/builder has no right to keep them in the land. The proof is the hadith of Ali: 'There is no right for a wrongful root'—the root (the tree or building) is described as oppressive. The land remains the property of the owner; since it is not consumed (like food) and the usurper does not gain ownership through the act, the land must be vacated. This is analogous to filling someone's container with food—the container is returned and the food is removed.

Key Terms
عرق ظالم ('irq zalim): Wrongful root; a metaphor for any construction or plantation on another's land without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person builds a house on another's land without permission, what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) The builder owns the land because of his building.
B) The building remains but the builder pays rent.
C) The building must be removed and the land returned; if removal decreases land value, the builder must compensate the owner.
D) The landowner loses his ownership if the building is valuable.

Key Takeaway
Unauthorized construction/plantation on another's land must be removed; if removal damages the land, the landowner receives compensation for the removed items plus any land devaluation.

Dyeing or Mixing Usurped Property: Owner's Option and Shafi'i View
Ruling when a usurper dyes cloth or mixes food with usurped ghee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the owner's option when a usurper dyes cloth or mixes food, and distinguish the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Owner of usurped property may choose: claim original value or take mixed item and pay for addition.

When a usurper alters stolen property (e.g., dyeing a cloth or mixing ghee into food), whose rights prevail? This passage presents a classic Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute. The Hanafi school grants the owner a choice, preserving both parties' property rights, while the Shafi'i school prioritizes restoration of the original item. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of property rights in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the cases: dyeing a stolen cloth, and mixing stolen sattuq (a food) with ghee. The same ruling applies to both.
Both involve adding something valuable (dye or ghee) to the stolen item.
2
State the Hanafi owner's options
The owner may either: (1) claim the full value of his original property (white cloth or unmixed sattuq) and leave the combined item with the usurper, or (2) take the combined item and pay the usurper the increase in value from the addition.
The owner has the choice; he is not forced into one option.
3
Compare the Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that for dyeing, the usurper must try to remove the dye by washing and return the cloth, compensating any loss in value. For mixing ghee (where separation is impossible), his view may align with the Hanafi.
The Shafi'i position emphasizes restoration of the original over preserving the addition.
4
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning
The Hanafi proof: both the original and the addition are valuable property. The owner has the primary right as owner of the original, but the usurper has a right to his added property. Giving the owner a choice preserves both rights as much as possible. This contrasts with building on land where separation is possible, so the owner does not have that option.
The key principle: صون حق كل ما أمكن (preserving each right as much as possible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن صبغ، أو لت السويق بسمن، ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض ومثل السويق، أو أخذهما، وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن. وإن شاء المالك: يعني إذا غصب ثوبا وصبغه أو سويقا فلته بسمن، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض، ومثل السويق، وإن شاء أخذ المصبوغ والملتوت وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن، وقال الإمام الشافعي: يؤمر الغاصب بقلع الصبغ بالغسل بقدر الإمكان، ويسلمه لصاحبه، وإن انتقص قيمة الثوب بذلك، فعليه ضمان النقصان، بخلاف السمن؛ لتعذر التمييز ولنا أن الصبغ مال متقوم كالثوب، وبجنايته لا يسقط تقوم ماله، فيجب صون حقهما ما أمكن، فكان صاحب الثوب أولى بالتخيير؛ لأنه صاحب الأصل، والآخر صاحب وصف، وهو قائم بالأصل، وكذا السويق أصل، والسمن تبع، بخلاف البناء؛ لأن التمييز ممكن بالنقض، وله وجود بعد النقض، فأمكن إيصال.
And if he dyes, or mixes sattuq with ghee, he is liable for the value of a white cloth and the equivalent of sattuq, or he takes them both and pays for the increase from the dye and ghee. And if the owner wishes: meaning if he usurps a cloth and dyes it, or sattuq and mixes it with ghee, the owner has the option: if he wishes, he may hold him liable for the value of a white cloth and the equivalent of sattuq; if he wishes, he may take the dyed and mixed items and pay for the increase from the dye and ghee. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The usurper is ordered to remove the dye by washing as much as possible and hand it over to the owner, and if the cloth's value decreases thereby, he must compensate the loss, unlike the ghee because separation is difficult. Our evidence is that the dye is valuable property like the cloth, and by his wrongdoing his property's value is not lost. So both rights must be preserved as much as possible. Thus the owner of the cloth deserves the choice because he is the owner of the original, and the other is the owner of an attribute which is attached to the original. Similarly, the sattuq is the original and the ghee is subsidiary, unlike building because separation is possible by demolishing and the building has existence after demolition, so it is possible to return it.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage discusses two cases: dyeing a stolen cloth, and mixing stolen sattuq (a type of food) with ghee (samn). The owner has an option: either claim the full value of his pure item (white cloth or unmixed sattuq) from the usurper, or take the mixed/dyed item and pay the usurper the increase in value caused by the dye/ghee. This is the Hanafi view. The Shafi'i view is different: for dyeing, the usurper must try to remove the dye by washing, and if the cloth loses value, he compensates the owner. For mixing ghee, because separation is impossible, the Shafi'i position may align. The Hanafi justification is that both items (the cloth and the dye, or the sattuq and ghee) are valuable property. By the usurper's action, the owner's property is not destroyed but combined. The owner's right is primary (he owns the original), so he should have the choice. This is contrasted with building on land where separation (demolition) is possible, so the owner does not have the same option.

Key Terms
ملتوت (multawt): Mixed; specifically sattuq mixed with ghee.
صبغ (sabgh): Dye; the substance used to color cloth.
سويق (sawīq): Sattuq; a type of roasted barley or wheat flour food.
سمن (samn): Ghee; clarified butter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, if a usurper dyes a stolen white cloth, what options does the owner have?
A) The owner must take the dyed cloth and pay for the dye.
B) The owner may either claim the value of a white cloth and leave the dyed cloth with the usurper, or take the dyed cloth and pay for the increase in value due to dye.
C) The owner must force the usurper to remove the dye by washing.
D) The owner loses his cloth because the dye has changed it completely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the owner of usurped property an option when their property is enhanced by a usurper's addition, balancing the rights of both parties.

Owner's Options When Usurper Improves Property: The Dyeing Dispute
Expansion on Hanafi rulings for usurper's improvements with a cross-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the owner's options when a usurper dyes cloth or mixes sawiq with ghee, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The owner may choose between compensation or taking the improvement and paying the increase.

When a usurper adds value to another's property—by building, planting, dyeing, or mixing—the owner's rights must be balanced against the usurper's contribution. Hanafi law gives the owner an option to either claim compensation for the original item or take the improved item and pay the increase. This lesson examines the ruling and the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over dyeing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage covers four main scenarios: building/planting on land, dyeing cloth, mixing sawiq with ghee, and tearing cloth. Identify each.
Focus on the dyeing scenario for the dispute.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on dyeing
The Hanafi school gives the owner the choice: either claim the value of white cloth (or unmixed sawiq) or take the dyed/mixed item and pay the increase in value caused by the dye/ghee.
This is an option (mubah), not a fixed rule.
3
State the Shafi'i ruling on dyeing
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the usurper must wash out the dye and return the cloth, compensating for any deficiency.
He distinguishes dyeing from mixing ghee because ghee cannot be separated.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument: the dye is valuable property, and washing destroys it. Giving the owner an option preserves both parties' rights—the owner's ownership of the cloth and the usurper's property in the dye.
This is based on the principle that both rights must be considered as much as possible.
5
Determine the practical difference
Under Shafi'i, the owner gets the cloth back possibly with reduced value; under Hanafi, the owner can choose to keep the improved cloth by paying the increase, or get compensated for the original value.
The thamarah al-khilaf affects the outcome for both owner and usurper.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسلم المغصوب إليه أو ضمن النقصان، وفي الخرق اليسير ضمن نقصانه، ولو غرس المالك إلى الغاصب إن شاء ضمن الغاصب أي المالك الغاصب وليس له غيره رجل أو بنى في أرض الغير، قلعا وردت وإن نقصت الأرض بالقلع، ضمن له البناء أي الغرس والبناء الأرض إلى مالكها المالك أي للغاصب والغرس مقلوعا، فيكون له، وإن صبغ، أو لت السويق بسمن، ضمنه قيمة ثوب أي البناء والغرس لصاحب الأرض الغاصب الثوب الذي غصبه الذي غصبه أبيض ومثل السويق، أو أخذهما، وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن. إن شاء المالك في الثوب في السويق وفي الخرق اليسير : أي في الخرق اليسير يضمن النقصان وأخذ الثوب، وليس له غير ذلك؛ لأن العين قائمة من كل وجه، وإنما دخل فيه عيب، فنقص لذلك، فكان له أن يضمنه النقصان واختلفوا في حد الخرق الكبير واليسير والصحيح أن الفاحش ما يفوت به بعض العين وجنس المنفعة، ويبقى بعض العين وبعض المنفعة، واليسير: ما لا يفوت به شيء من المنفعة، وإنما يدخل فيه نقصان في المنفعة. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٧/٢] قلعا وردت أي قلع البناء والغرس وردت الأرض إلى صاحبها؛ لقوله علي: "ليس لعرق ظالم حق"، أي لذي عرق ظالم، وصف العرق بصفة صاحبه، وهو الظلم مجازا، كما يقال صام نهاره، وقام ليله رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۸/۲] ولأن الأرض باقية على ملكه إذا لم تكن مستهلكة، ولا مغصوبة حقيقة، ولم يوجد فيها شيء يوجب الملك للغاصب، فيؤمر بتفريغها، وردها إلى مالكها كما إذا شغل ظرف غيره بالطعام. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٣٢/٨] ضمن له إلخ : أي إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع كان لصاحب الأرض أن يضمن للغاصب قيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعا، ويكونان له؛ لأن فيه دفع الضرر عنهما، فتعين فيه النظر لهما، وإنما يضمن قيمتها مقلوعا؛ لأنه مستحق للقلع، وليس له أن يستديم فيها، فتعتبر قيمته في ذلك الوقت مقلوعا.(تكملة البحر الرائق) مقلوعا إلخ: أي حال كون كل من البناء أو الغرس مقلوعا، وكيفية معرفتها: أن يقوم الأرض وبها بناء أو شجر استحق قلعه، وتقوم وحدها ليس فيها بناء ولا غرس فيضمن فضل ما بينهما كذا قالوا وهذا ليس بضمان لقيمته مقلوعا، بل هو ضمان لقيمته قائما مستحق القلع، وإنما يكون ضمانا لقيمته مقلوعا أن لو قدر البناء أو الغرس مقلوعا موضوعا في الأرض بأن يقدر الغرس حطبا، والبناء آجرا، أو لبنا أو حجارة مكومة على الأرض، فيقوم وحده من غير أن يضم إلى الأرض، فيضمن له قيمة الحطب والحجارة المكومة دون المبنية تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٣٢/٨] (عيني) وإن صبغ إلخ : يعني إذا غصب ثوبا وصبغه أو سويقا فلته بسمن، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب أبيض، ومثل السويق، وإن شاء أخذ المصبوغ والملتوت وغرم ما زاد الصبغ والسمن، وقال الإمام الشافعي: يؤمر الغاصب بقلع الصبغ بالغسل بقدر الإمكان، ويسلمه لصاحبه، وإن انتقص قيمة الثوب بذلك، فعليه ضمان النقصان، بخلاف السمن؛ لتعذر التمييز ولنا أن الصبغ مال متقوم كالثوب، وبجنايته لا يسقط تقوم ماله، فيجب صون حقهما ما أمكن، فكان صاحب الثوب أولى بالتخيير؛ لأنه صاحب الأصل، والآخر صاحب وصف، وهو قائم بالأصل، وكذا السويق أصل، والسمن تبع، بخلاف البناء؛ لأن التمييز ممكن بالنقض، وله وجود بعد النقض، فأمكن إيصال .
And if the usurped item is returned or the deficiency is compensated, and in the case of a small tear, he compensates for the deficiency. If the owner [i.e., the owner of the land] plants or builds on the land of another, it is removed and the land is returned, even if the land decreases by removal, and he is compensated for the building or planting [i.e., the owner of the land] [for the usurper's benefit] and the planting is removed, so it becomes his. And if he dyes, or mixes sawiq with ghee, he compensates the value of the cloth [i.e., the building and planting belong to the landowner, the usurper's cloth which he usurped] white and the like of sawiq, or takes them both and pays the increase of the dye and ghee. The owner has the option regarding the cloth and the sawiq. Regarding the small tear: he compensates for the deficiency and takes the cloth, and he has nothing else; because the item remains intact in every respect, only a defect has entered it, so it has decreased, thus he may claim compensation for the deficiency. They differed on the definition of a large tear and a small one. The correct view is that a large tear is that which destroys some of the item and the type of utility, leaving some of the item and some utility; a small tear is that which does not destroy any utility, only a deficiency in utility enters. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/327] 'Removed and returned': meaning to remove the building and planting and return the land to its owner, based on the saying of 'Ali: 'There is no right for an unjust root,' i.e., for the possessor of an unjust root, describing the root with the attribute of its possessor, which is injustice metaphorically, as it is said 'he fasted his day, and stood his night' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/328]. And because the land remains in his ownership if it is not consumed, nor truly usurped, and there is nothing therein that gives ownership to the usurper, so he is ordered to vacate it and return it to its owner, just as when someone fills another's vessel with food. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/232] 'He is compensated...': i.e., if the land decreases by removal, the landowner may claim from the usurper the value of the building and planting as removed, and they become his; because it removes harm from both, so consideration for them is determined. He is only compensated for its value as removed because it is subject to removal, and he may not keep it there, so its value at that time as removed is considered. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) 'as removed': i.e., the building or planting in the state of being removed. The method of calculation: the land is valued with the building or tree subject to removal, and alone without building or planting, and the difference between them is compensated. This is not compensation for its value as removed, but rather compensation for its value as standing subject to removal. It would be compensation for its value as removed if the building or planting were assumed removed and placed on the ground, such as counting the planting as firewood, and the building as bricks, or adobe, or stones piled on the ground, valued alone without including the land, and he is compensated for the value of the firewood and piled stones, not the constructed ones. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/232] (Ayni) 'And if he dyes...': means if he usurps cloth and dyes it, or sawiq and mixes it with ghee, the owner has the option: if he wishes, he may claim the value of white cloth and the like of sawiq; if he wishes, he may take the dyed and mixed items, and pay the increase of the dye and ghee. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The usurper is ordered to remove the dye by washing as much as possible and return it to its owner; if the value of the cloth decreases thereby, he compensates for the deficiency. Different for ghee because separation is impossible. Our [Hanafi] argument: The dye is property with value like the cloth; by his transgression, the value of his property does not become null. So preserving both rights as much as possible is obligatory. Thus the owner of the cloth is more entitled to the option because he is the owner of the original, and the other is the owner of an attribute attached to the original. Similarly, sawiq is the original, and ghee is subsidiary. Different from building because separation is possible by dismantling, and it exists after dismantling, so delivery is possible.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries presents the Hanafi rulings on improvements made by a usurper to usurped property. The text opens by establishing that when a usurper builds or plants on another's land, the landowner has the right to demand removal and return of the land. If the land decreases in value due to removal, the usurper must compensate that deficiency. In cases of small tears to the cloth (a separate issue), only the deficiency is compensated. The definition of a large tear vs small tear is given: a large tear destroys some of the item or its utility, while a small tear only reduces utility. The author then cites the hadith 'There is no right for an unjust root,' attributed to 'Ali, as the proof for ordering removal. Since the land remains the owner's property, the usurper must vacate it. However, if the land would be damaged by removal, the owner may instead claim the value of the building/planting as removed (i.e., as rubble or firewood) and take ownership of those materials. This is a istihsan ruling to avoid harm. The text then addresses dyeing or mixing: if a usurper dyes a stolen cloth or mixes sawiq with ghee, the owner has two options: either claim the value of the original item (white cloth or unmixed sawiq) or take the altered item and pay the increase in value caused by the dye/ghee. Imam al-Shafi'i's opposite view is presented: he requires the usurper to wash out the dye and return the cloth, compensating for any decrease. The Hanafi response argues that the dye is valuable property and washing destroys it, so the owner's option better preserves both parties' rights.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right.
عرق ظالم (irq zālim): Unjust root: metaphor for building or planting on another's land without permission.
صبغ (ṣabgh): Dye: a substance added to cloth that increases its value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what options does the owner have when a usurper dyes a stolen cloth?
A) The owner must take the dyed cloth and pay the increase; there is no other option.
B) The owner may either take the dyed cloth and pay the increase in value, or claim the value of white cloth.
C) The owner must force the usurper to wash out the dye and return the cloth.
D) The owner gets the dyed cloth for free, since it was stolen.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi option in dyeing and mixing balances the rights of both the owner and the usurper by allowing the owner to choose between compensation or taking the improvement and paying the increase.

Ownership of Usurped Property After Paying Value: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling on ownership of usurped property after paying value, with Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that the usurper becomes owner upon payment of value, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The usurper owns the property upon paying its value, to prevent ownerless property.

This section from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a critical difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a usurper can ever own the property he wrongfully took. The Hanafi school rules that if the property is destroyed or disappears, and the usurper pays its value, he becomes its owner retroactively. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that aggression cannot be a cause of ownership. Understanding this dispute reveals deep principles about how property rights are preserved in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative trigger
The text uses 'ghayyaba' (made disappear). Why is the active form important? It specifies that the usurper's action is the trigger; if the property disappears without his action, the ruling may differ.
Some scholars prefer 'ghaba' (disappeared) for broader coverage.
2
State the Hanafi position
The usurper owns the property after paying its value. The rationale: the substitute and original cannot coexist in one ownership; the original must leave the owner's possession and enter the usurper's to avoid ownerlessness.
Ownership is retroactive to the time of usurpation.
3
State the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that usurpation is pure aggression (udwan mahd) and cannot be a cause of ownership. A wrong cannot generate a right.
This is a principle-based objection.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi scholars respond that ownership here is a necessary legal consequence (hukm wad'i) to ensure property is not left without an owner. It is not a reward for wrongdoing but a practical necessity.
The purpose is to preserve the system of ownership.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the usurped property is a close relative (e.g., his father), under Hanafi the relative becomes free upon payment (because the usurper owns him); under Shafi'i, he does not, because the usurper never owns him.
This shows the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل لو غيب المغصوب، وضمن قيمته، ملكه. أي الغاصب الغاصب للمالك قيمة المغصوب الغاصب المغصوب حق كل واحد منهما إليه، والصبغ يتلاشى بالغسل، فلا يمكن إيصاله إلى صاحبه، وظاهر العبارة أن الحكم منحصر فيما ذكر، وقال أبو عصمة في مسألة الغصب إن شاء رب الثوب ،باعه فيضرب كل واحد منهما بقيمة ماله، وهذا وجه حسن في وصول حق كل واحد منهما إلى صاحبه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٣٢/٨] اعلم أنه قيد الصبغ بالأحمر في "ملا" "مسكين ولا حاجة إليه، بل هو مطلق سواء صبغه أسود أو أحمر، قال في "الفتح" عند قول ملا مسكين "أحمر": لو أبقى المتن على إطلاقه لكان أولى؛ إذ لا عبرة للألوان بل لحقيقة الزيادة والنقصان؛ لأن من الثياب ما يزداد بالسواد ومنها ما ينتقص، وكذا من الثياب ما يزداد بالحمرة والصفرة ومنها ما ينتقص، كما في "الدر"، قال العيني: والاختلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في كون السواد نقصانا أو زيادة اختلاف عصر وزمان، فإن بني أمية كانوا يمتنعون من لبس السواد في زمانهم وبنو العباس كانوا يلبسون السواد فأجاب كل على ما شاهده. فصل إلخ: لما فرغ من بيان كيفية ما يوجب الملك للغاصب بالضمان شرع في ذكر مسائل تتصل بمسائل الغصب تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۲۰/۸۸] لو غيب إلخ : أي لو غيب الغاصب المغصوب وضمن قيمته للمالك ملکه، قال في "ملا" "مسكين ولو قال إذا غاب المغصوب لكان أولى وجه الأولية: كون "غيب" في كلام المصنف معلوما، فلا يعلم حكم ما إذا غاب بلا فعله، وليس في كلام المصنف ما يعين كونه معلوما، فيجوز بناء "غيب" للمجهول، وحينئذ يفسد ما إذا كان بفعل الغاصب أو لا بفعله فلا أولوية، وهذا الجواب على تسليم الأولوية على تقدير كونه مبنيا للفاعل، وإلا ففي الأولوية نظر، فإنه لو قال: غاب لم يعلم منه حكم ما لو غيبه بخلاف ما عبر به المصنف؛ فإنه يعلم منه حكم ما لو غاب بالطريق الأولى. (كذا في الفتح بتغيير) ملكه إلخ: أي ملك الغاصب المغصوب بعد أداء الضمان، وقال الشافعي لا يملكه فلو كان المغصوب قريب الغاصب يعتق عليه بأداء الضمان عندنا وعنده لا يعتق، له: أن الغصب عدوان محض ليس فيه شبهة الإباحة، فكيف يستفاد الملك بالجناية المحضة ولنا أن المالك ملك البدل، فوجب أن يزول المبدل عن ملكه؛ لئلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك رجل واحد، ووجب أن يدخل في ملك الغاصب، وإلا لزم ثبوت الملك بلا مالك كذا في "الدرر". واعلم أنه بالضمان يثبت للغاصب الملك المستند إلى وقت الغصب، فتسلم له الأكساب لا الأولاد كذا في "الدر" عن "المنتقى"، وفي "العناية : ولا يراد بكون الغصب سببا للملك عند أداء الضمان أنه يوجبه مطلقا، بل بطريق الاستناد والثابت به ثابت من وجه دون وجه فلا يظهر أثره في ثبوت الزيادة المنفصلة ، قال الزيلعي ونحن لا نجعل الغصب سببا للملك، بل الغصب موجب برد العين عند القدرة، ورد القيمة عند العجز بطريق الجبران، وهذا الحكم هو المقصود بهذا السبب، ثم يثبت الملك به للغاصب شرطا للقضاء بالقيمة لا حكما ثابتا بالغصب مقصودا، ولهذا لا يملك الولد، بخلاف الزيادة المتصلة والكسب؛ لأنه تبع؛ إذ الكسب بدل المنفعة، ولا كذلك المنفصلة. [تبيين الحقائق: ٣٣٢/٦]
Section: If he makes the usurped property disappear and pays its value, he owns it. i.e., the usurper, the usurper to the owner the value of the usurped property, the usurper, the property that is rightfully each one's. And the dye disintegrates with washing, so it cannot be delivered to its owner. The apparent meaning of the phrase is that the ruling is limited to what was mentioned. Abu 'Isma said regarding the issue of usurpation: If the owner of the cloth wishes, he sells it and each takes a share proportional to his property. This is a good way to make each person's right reach its owner. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/232] Know that he restricted the dye to red in "Mulla" and "Miskin"; there is no need for that, rather it is absolute whether he dyes it black or red. He said in al-Fath regarding the statement of Mulla Miskin 'red': If the matn had kept it absolute, it would have been better; because there is no consideration for colors, but rather for the reality of increase and decrease. For some cloths increase in value with black, some decrease; similarly, some increase with red and yellow, some decrease. As in al-Durr. al-'Ayni said: The difference between the Imam (Abu Hanifa) and his two companions regarding whether black is a decrease or increase is a difference of time and era. For the Umayyads forbade wearing black in their time, while the Abbasids wore black, so each answered according to what he observed. 'Section etc.': When he finished explaining the manner of what makes the usurper own by way of compensation, he began to mention issues connected to the issues of usurpation. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/220] 'If he makes it disappear': i.e., if the usurper makes the usurped property disappear and pays its value to the owner, he owns it. He said in "Mulla" and "Miskin": 'If he had said 'if the usurped property disappears' it would have been better.' The reason for this preference: the word 'made disappear' in the author's text is active, so the ruling for when it disappears without his action is not known. There is nothing in the author's text to specify it as active, so it may be read as passive. Then it would not distinguish whether it was by the usurper's action or not, so there is no preference. This answer assumes that the statement is built on the active form. Otherwise, there is doubt about the preference. For if he said 'disappeared', the ruling for when he made it disappear would not be known, unlike the author's wording, which indicates by primary reasoning the ruling for when it disappears. (Kadha fi al-Fath with change) 'He owns it': i.e., the usurper owns the usurped property after paying the compensation. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not own it. Thus if the usurped property is a relative of the usurper, it becomes free upon payment of the compensation according to us, but not according to him. His [Shafi'i's] argument: Usurpation is pure aggression with no semblance of permissibility, so how can ownership be gained by mere transgression? Our [Hanafi] argument: The owner has obtained the substitute, so the original must leave his ownership to prevent the substitute and original from being in one man's ownership, and it must enter the usurper's ownership, otherwise it would entail property being ownerless. As in al-Durar. Know that by compensation, the usurper acquires ownership retroactive to the time of usurpation. Thus earnings are his, but not offspring, as in al-Durr from al-Muntaqa. In al-'Inayah: It is not meant that usurpation is a cause of ownership upon payment of compensation absolutely; rather by way of retroactivity, and what is established is established in one way not another, so its effect does not appear in the establishment of detached increase. Al-Zayla'i said: We do not make usurpation a cause of ownership. Rather, usurpation necessitates returning the item when possible, and paying the value when impossible by way of compensation. This ruling is the intended purpose of this cause. Then ownership is established for the usurper as a condition for the judgment of value payment, not as a ruling established purposefully by usurpation. For this reason, he does not own the offspring, unlike attached increase and earnings; because they are subsidiary, for earnings are compensation for utility, unlike detached increase. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 6/332]
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This section (fasl) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the important Hanafi principle that when a usurper causes the usurped property to disappear (ghayyaba) and pays its value, he becomes its owner. The commentary clarifies that the wording 'ghayyaba' (made it disappear) is preferable to 'ghaba' (disappeared) because it explicitly covers cases where the usurper actively causes disappearance. The ruling is based on preventing the original and its substitute from co-existing in one ownership, and preventing property from becoming ownerless. The text notes a difference with Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that a pure wrong cannot generate ownership. The Hanafi response is that the ownership here is a necessary consequence to avoid ownerless property, not a reward for the wrong. Importantly, the ownership is retroactive to the time of usurpation, but with limitations: the usurper gets the earnings (e.g., rent) but not offspring born before payment, because those are detached increases. The text also digresses to discuss the color of dye, noting that the ruling on increase or decrease depends on custom and era, not an absolute rule. Earlier scholars' differences on black vs. red dye reflect their times (Umayyad vs. Abbasid).

Key Terms
غيب (ghayyaba): To make the property disappear, either actively or passively; the text discusses active disappearance.
بدل (badal): Substitute value paid by the usurper in lieu of the original property.
زيادة منفصلة (ziyāda munfaṣila): Detached increase (e.g., offspring) that does not transfer to usurper with ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a usurper makes the property disappear and pays its value, which of the following occurs?
A) The property remains in the owner's ownership, and the usurper only pays compensation.
B) The usurper becomes owner of the property retroactively, and all increases (including offspring) belong to him.
C) The usurper becomes owner of the property retroactively, but he does not own offspring born before payment.
D) The usurper does not become owner, but the property is considered lost and the owner only claims value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that the usurper becomes owner of the disappeared property upon paying its value, based on the principle that property must not become ownerless.

Dispute Over the Value of Usurped Property
Ruling on who has the say in determining the value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who has the say in determining the value of usurped property when parties dispute, and explain the qiyas argument regarding the acceptance of evidence.

Core Idea
The usurper's word is taken with oath; the owner's evidence is preferred; the usurper's evidence is not accepted.

When the owner and usurper disagree on the value of usurped property, the Hanafi school applies procedural rules from qada. This lesson examines the default rule: the usurper's statement with oath if no evidence, and why the owner's evidence takes precedence if both present evidence. It also explores a subtle qiyas analogy with the depositary and why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The owner claims a higher value; the usurper claims a lower value. There are three sub-scenarios: no evidence, both have evidence, or only the usurper brings evidence.
The ruling depends on who is the denier (munkir) and who is the claimant (mudda'i).
2
State the rule when no evidence is presented
The usurper's statement with his oath is accepted because he is the denier of the increase. The maxim: 'al-qawl li man ankara' (the word is for the denier).
The usurper must take an oath to support his statement.
3
State the rule when both parties have evidence
The owner's evidence is accepted because it is affirmative (muthbita) evidence of a higher value. The usurper's evidence is not accepted because it is negative (nafiya) evidence that merely denies the increase.
Negative evidence is generally not accepted in Hanafi qada to rebut a claim.
4
Analyze the qiyas objection
Some Hanafi scholars analogize the usurper to a depositary (wadi') who claims to have returned the deposit. In that case, the depositary's evidence is accepted. They argue the 'illa is negation of a claim.
The analogy is based on the principle that evidence can be accepted to negate a claim.
5
Evaluate the distinction and final ruling
The majority distinguishes: the depositary only has the obligation of an oath, so presenting evidence resolves the entire dispute. The usurper has both an oath and liability for the value; his evidence only removes the oath but the value liability remains. Therefore, the analogy fails and the usurper's evidence is not accepted.
The correct view holds that the usurper's evidence is not accepted because it does not fully resolve the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول في القيمة للغاصب مع يمينه والبينة للمالك. والقول في القيمة إلخ: أي إذا اختلفا في القيمة ولا بينة لهما فالقول قول الغاصب مع يمينه؛ لأنه منكر للزيادة والمالك مدع، والقول للمنكر، وإذا كان لهما بينة فالبينة للمالك؛ لأنها مثبتة للزيادة، ولو أقام الغاصب البينة لا تقبل؛ لأنها تنفي الزيادة، والبينة على النفي لا تقبل، وقال بعض مشايخنا ينبغي أن تقبل بينة الغاصب، ألا ترى أن المودع إذا ادعى رد الوديعة تقبل، وبعض المشايخ فرق بين هذه المسألة ومسألة الوديعة، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن المودع ليس عليه إلا اليمين، وبإقامة البينة أسقطها، وارتفعت الخصومة، وأما الغاصب فعليه اليمين ،والقيمة وبإقامة البينة لم يسقط إلا اليمين، فلا يكون في معنى المودع. (ملتقط من الفتح والتكملة)
The statement regarding the value is with the usurper along with his oath, and the evidence is for the owner. The explanation: If they differ in the value and neither has evidence, then the statement is that of the usurper with his oath because he is the denier of the increase and the owner is the claimant, and the statement is with the denier. And if they both have evidence, then the evidence is for the owner because it confirms the increase. And if the usurper brings evidence, it is not accepted because it negates the increase, and evidence for negation is not accepted. Some of our shaykhs said that the evidence of the usurper should be accepted; do you not see that the depositary (wadi') when he claims to have returned the deposit, his evidence is accepted? And some shaykhs distinguished between this case and the case of the deposit, and that is correct because the depositary is only under oath, and by bringing evidence he lifts the oath and the dispute is resolved. As for the usurper, he is under oath and (liable for) the value, and by bringing evidence he only lifts the oath, so he is not like the depositary. (Selected from al-Fath and al-Takmila)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between the owner (mallik) and the usurper (ghasib) regarding the value of the usurped property. The ruling establishes procedural rules for settling such disputes. If no evidence is presented by either party, the word of the usurper is taken with his oath because he is the one denying the claim of a higher value (he is the 'denier') and the owner is claiming a higher value (claimant). In Hanafi procedural law, the statement of the denier is accepted with an oath. If both parties have evidence, the owner's evidence is preferred because it is affirmative (muthbita) evidence, while the usurper's evidence would be negative (nafiya) which is generally not accepted. However, some Hanafi scholars analogized this to the case of a depositary (wadi') who claims to have returned the deposit – in that case, his evidence is accepted to negate liability. The majority distinguishes: the depositary only faces an oath, so presenting evidence resolves the dispute entirely; but the usurper faces both an oath and liability for the value, so his evidence only removes the oath but not the liability for value. Therefore, his evidence is not accepted. The passage is extracted from al-Fath and al-Takmila commentaries.

Key Terms
الغاصب (al-ghasib): The person who has taken property from another without right; the usurper.
المالك (al-malik): The rightful owner of the property that was usurped.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, usually the testimony of two witnesses or one witness with an oath, as defined in Hanafi qada.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath taken by a party in a legal dispute to support their claim or denial.
المودع (al-mudi'): The depositary; the person who holds a deposit (wadi'ah) for another.
القيمة (al-qimah): The monetary value of the usurped property at the time of usurpation or at the time of claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between an owner and a usurper over the value of usurped property, if neither has evidence, whose statement is accepted?
A) The owner's statement without oath
B) The usurper's statement with his oath
C) The owner's statement with oath
D) The usurper's statement without oath

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over usurped property value, the usurper's word is taken with oath when no evidence exists; if both have evidence, the owner's evidence prevails; the usurper's evidence is not accepted because it would not fully resolve the dispute.

Owner's Option When Usurped Property Appears After Compensation
Ruling on owner's khiyar after property appears

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the scenarios where the owner has an option to rescind compensation and apply the correct ruling based on how the usurper was made liable.

Core Idea
The owner's option hinges on whether his consent was complete — determined by the method of proof.

When a usurper pays compensation but the property later surfaces, the owner's rights depend on how the compensation was established. Did the owner consent through his own claim, or was the amount set by the usurper's oath? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on the owner's khiyar in such cases, including a key dispute within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of liability
The passage describes four ways the usurper became liable: (1) owner's statement of value, (2) owner's evidence, (3) usurper's refusal to swear (nukul), and (4) usurper's oath. The first three are grouped together; the fourth is distinct.
2
Apply the consent rule
If liability was based on the owner's own claim or proof, the owner's consent to the compensation amount is considered complete — no option to rescind. If based on the usurper's oath, the owner's consent was incomplete (he accepted due to lack of proof), so he retains an option to take back the property and return the compensation.
Consent is the key principle: rida tamm (complete consent) vs. rida naqis (incomplete consent).
3
Examine the intra-madhhab dispute
Al-Karkhi held that if the property's value is equal to or less than the compensation paid under the usurper's oath, the owner has no option because his full ownership value is restored. However, the authoritative position (zahir al-riwaya) grants the option in all cases, regardless of relative value.
The dispute hinges on whether 'full value' automatically implies complete consent.
4
State the operative ruling
The mufta bihi position is zahir al-riwaya: when liability was based on the usurper's oath, the owner has the option to either confirm the compensation or take the property and return the compensation, even if the property's value equals or is less than the compensation.
This is the position of the majority of Hanafi scholars and is relied upon in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ظهر المغصوب بعد التضمين مما أدى ضمنه بقول المالك أو ببينة، أو بنكول الغاصب، فهو للغاصب، ولا خيار للمالك، وإن ضمنه بيمين الغاصب، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاء يمضي الضمان أو إن شاء يأخذ المغصوب ويرد العوض. فإن ظهر إلخ: أي وإن ظهر المغصوب وقيمته أكثر من الذي ضمنه الغاصب، والحال أن الغاصب قد ضمنه بقول المالك أو ضمنه ببينة المالك أو ضمنه بنكول الغاصب، فالمغصوب للغاصب، ولا خيار للمالك؛ لأنه رضي به، وتم ملكه برضاه حيث سلم له ما ادعاه ولم يتعرض لقدر الزيادة. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٣٧/٨ لا خيار للمالك: في أن يرد القيمة ويأخذ المغصوب. وإن ضمنه إلخ: أي وإن كان قد ضمن المالك الغاصب بيمين الغاصب، فالمالك بالخيار إن شاء يمضي الضمان أو إن شاء يأخذ المغصوب من الغاصب، ويرد العوض الذي أخذه من الغاصب؛ لعدم تمام رضاه بهذا المقدار من الضمان. رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٩/٢ وإنما أخذ دون القيمة؛ لعدم الحجة لا للرضاء به، ولو ظهر المغصوب وقيمته مثل ما ضمنه أو أقل في هذه الصورة، وهي ما إذا ضمنه بقول الغاصب مع يمينه، قال الكرخي: لا خيار له؛ لأنه توفر عليه مالية ملكه بكماله، وفي ظاهر الرواية يثبت له الخيار، وهو الأصح. (عيني، فتح)
If the usurped property appears after compensation, from what he paid when he was made liable by the owner's statement, or by evidence, or by the usurper's refusal to take an oath, then it belongs to the usurper, and there is no option for the owner. And if he was made liable by the usurper's oath, then the owner has the option: if he wishes he confirms the compensation, or if he wishes he takes the usurped property and returns the compensation. The explanation: If the usurped property appears and its value is more than what the usurper compensated, and the usurper was made liable by the owner's statement, or by the owner's evidence, or by the usurper's refusal, then the property is for the usurper, and no option for the owner because he (the owner) consented to it, and his ownership is complete by his consent since he surrendered what he claimed and did not address the amount of the increase. Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/237: No option for the owner to return the value and take the property. And if he was made liable by the usurper's oath, then the owner has the option: if he wishes he confirms the compensation, or if he wishes he takes the property from the usurper and returns the compensation he took from the usurper, due to the incompleteness of his consent to this amount of compensation. Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/329: He only took less than the value due to lack of proof, not due to consent. And if the property appears and its value is equal to or less than what he compensated, in this situation (i.e. when he was made liable by the usurper's statement with his oath), al-Karkhi said: he has no option because the complete value of his property has come to him. According to zahir al-riwaya, he has the option, and that is the more correct view. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where the usurper has already paid compensation (daman) to the owner, but later the actual usurped property is found. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) when the usurper was made liable based on the owner's statement (meaning the owner claimed a value, and the usurper paid accordingly), or based on evidence presented by the owner, or based on the usurper's refusal to take an oath (nukul), then once the property appears, it becomes the property of the usurper without any option for the owner. This is because the owner's consent is implied: he accepted the compensation based on his own claim or evidence, so his ownership of the property transfers to the usurper. (2) When the usurper was made liable based on his own oath (meaning the owner accepted the usurper's sworn statement of value and took the compensation), then the owner has the option to either keep the compensation and let the property go to the usurper, or to return the compensation and take back the property. This is because the owner's consent was not fully given; he only accepted the lesser amount due to lack of conclusive proof. Additionally, al-Karkhi stated that if the property's value is equal to or less than the compensation, there is no option, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) gives the owner the option in all cases.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option or choice given to the owner to either affirm the compensation or take back the property.
الضمان (al-daman): The compensation or liability paid by the usurper to the owner.
النكول (al-nukul): The refusal of a party to take an oath in a legal dispute, which leads to a ruling against them.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmission of Hanafi rulings from the early masters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the usurper was made liable based on his own oath and then the property appears with a higher value, what option does the owner have?
A) The owner must keep the compensation
B) The owner can either keep the compensation or take the property and return the compensation
C) The usurper has no right to the property
D) The property is returned to the owner without any payment

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a usurper compensates based on his own oath, the owner retains an option to rescind, but when compensation is based on the owner's claim or evidence, the option is lost.

Sale and Manumission by the Usurper: Deficient Ownership
Ruling with proof via qiyas on deficient ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the usurper's sale of usurped property is effective after liability enforcement, but his manumission is not, by applying the Hanafi concept of deficient ownership (al-milk al-naqis).

Core Idea
Deficient ownership suffices for sale but not for manumission.

This passage presents a nuanced Hanafi ruling: a usurper who sells usurped property and later compensates the owner sees his sale become effective, but if he manumitted a usurped slave, the manumission remains invalid even after compensation. The key is the quality of the usurper's ownership—deficient (naqis) rather than complete. This lesson unpacks the reasoning (wajh al-farq) and the qiyas that underlies this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
State the ruling on the sale (effective after daman) and the ruling on manumission (not effective after daman). Note that the passage gives opposite outcomes for two different acts by the same person.
2
Locate the wajh al-farq
Find the reason for the difference: the usurper's ownership is deficient (naqis). The passage explains that deficient ownership is established only from one aspect (necessity) and is not complete. This is the central legal basis.
The phrase 'ملك الغاصب ناقص' is the key.
3
Examine the qiyas with the buyer who hasn't paid
The passage compares the usurper to a buyer who purchases a slave but has not yet paid the price. The buyer's ownership is complete from the contract (children belong to him), while the usurper's ownership is incomplete even after compensation is enforced because it was originally based on usurpation.
This qiyas shows that complete ownership is needed for manumission, while deficient ownership suffices for sale.
4
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The passage references the mukatib (slave in kitabah) and ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade). Both can sell (using deficient ownership) but cannot manumit. Confirm that the same logic applies to the usurper.
These examples reinforce that sale requires less ownership than manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن باع المغصوب فضمنه المالك، نفذ بيعه، وإن حرّر الغاصب العبد المغصوب ثم ضمنه المالك القيمة لا ينفذ عتقه. وجه الفرق بينهما : أن ملك الغاصب ناقص؛ لأنه يثبت مستندا أو ضرورة، وكل ذلك ثابت من وجه دون وجه الملك ولهذا لا يظهر الملك في حق الأولاد ويظهر في حق الأكساب فيكون ناقصا؛ إذ لو كان تاما لكانت الأولاد له بأداء الضمان فإن من اشترى جارية قبل أداء الثمن تكون الأولاد للمشتري لتمام الملك والملك الناقص يكفي لنفوذ البيع، ألا ترى أن البيع ينفذ . من المكاتب، بل من المأذون دون عتقه.
If the usurper sold the usurped property and then the owner enforced liability, his sale is effective. And if the usurper manumitted the enslaved person (who was usurped) and then the owner enforced liability for the value, his manumission is not effective. The reason for the difference is that the usurper's ownership is deficient; because it is established based on necessity. And all that is established from one aspect but not from the aspect of complete ownership. Therefore, ownership does not appear in relation to children but appears in relation to earnings, so it is deficient. If it were complete, the children would belong to him upon payment of compensation, just as someone who buys a female slave before paying the price, the children belong to the buyer due to complete ownership. And deficient ownership suffices for the effectiveness of sale, but not for manumission. Do you not see that sale is effective from a mukatib (a slave in a contract of manumission), even from a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade), but his manumission is not.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage contrasts the rulings on two acts of the usurper: selling the usurped property and manumitting a usurped slave. If the owner subsequently enforces liability (daman) on the usurper by demanding and receiving its value, the sale (if it occurred before) becomes effective. However, if the usurper manumitted the slave and then the owner enforced liability, the manumission is not effective. The reason (wajh al-farq) is that the usurper's ownership over the usurped property is deficient (naqis). It is not full ownership because the property can be reclaimed by the owner if the value is not paid. This deficient ownership is sufficient to validate a sale (as a disposition that transfers property, similar to a mukatib or ma'dhun slave who can sell but not free), but it is not sufficient to validly manumit, which requires a stronger ownership. The analogy with the buyer of a female slave who has not yet paid the price is illustrative: the buyer's ownership is complete from the moment of contract, so any children born to the slave belong to him. In contrast, the usurper's ownership is not complete from the beginning; it only becomes complete upon payment of compensation. Therefore, certain dispositions are effective and others are not.

Key Terms
الملك الناقص (al-milk al-naqis): Deficient ownership: an ownership that is not absolute, here referring to the usurper's ownership which is subject to the original owner's right to reclaim.
المكاتب (al-mukatib): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, under which he works to buy his freedom.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the usurper's sale of usurped property effective if the owner enforces liability, but his manumission is not?
A) Because the usurper has full ownership over the property
B) Because sale requires less complete ownership than manumission
C) Because the owner consents to the sale but not the manumission
D) Because the usurper never really owns the property

Key Takeaway
The effectiveness of a legal act depends on the quality of ownership; deficient ownership validates sale but not manumission.
