Increments of Usurped Property: Are They a Trust or Guaranteed?
Ruling that increments of usurped property are a trust, liable only upon transgression or withholding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the liability for increments of usurped property and compare it with the Shafi'i view, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Increments of usurped property are a trust, not automatically guaranteed — liability only follows transgression or withholding.

This lesson examines a key difference between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i/Hanbali schools regarding the increments (zawa'id) that arise from usurped property. The ruling has significant practical consequences for determining a usurper's financial liability. By the end, you will understand the Hanafi proof based on the definition of ghasb and how it excludes increments from automatic guarantee.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: increments (connected and separate) are a trust in the usurper's hand, not automatically guaranteed.
Increments include fatness (connected) and offspring, fruit, wool, milk (separate).
2
Identify the opposing school and their position
State that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal hold increments are absolutely guaranteed on the usurper, regardless of transgression or withholding.
3
Analyze the opposing dalil
Understand their reasoning: because the original property was taken unlawfully, everything arising from it is also covered by the same guarantee.
This is a broader interpretation of the effects of ghasb.
4
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
Examine the Hanafi proof: ghasb is defined as removing the owner's hand and placing the usurper's hand over the property. Increments were never in the owner's hand, so they are not covered by the original guarantee.
This is a precise definitional argument (dalil aqli).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a usurper who returns the property with its increments is not liable for loss or damage to increments that occurred without his fault. Under Shafi'i law, he would be liable regardless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوائد المغصوب أمانة، فيضمن بالتعدي، أو بالمنع بعد طلب المالك. وزوائد المغصوب إلخ: أي وزوائد المغصوب سواء كانت متصلة كالسمن والجمال، أو منفصلة كالولد والثمرة والصوف واللبن أمانة في يد الغاصب فيضمن الغاصب بالتعدي، أو بالمنع بعد طلب المالك رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۹/۲] وقال الشافعي: هي مضمونة على الغاصب مطلقا، ولا فرق أن تكون الزيادة متصلة أو منفصلة؛ لوجود حد الغصب، وبه قال ،أحمد ولنا أن الغصب إزالة يد المالك عنه وإثبات يد الغاصب، ولا يتحقق ذلك في الزيادة؛ لأنها لم تكن في يد المالك، فلا تضمن إلا بالتعدي أو بالمنع عند طلبه؛ لأنه يصير متعديا تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٣٩/٨]
The increments of the usurped property are a trust; so the usurper is liable upon transgression (ta'addi) or upon withholding (man') after the owner's demand. The explanation: the increments of the usurped property, whether they are connected (like fatness and beauty) or separate (like offspring, fruit, wool, milk), are a trust in the hand of the usurper; he is liable for them upon transgression or upon withholding after the owner's demand. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/329) Al-Shafi'i said: They are absolutely liable on the usurper, whether the increment is connected or separate, due to the occurrence of ghasb (the act of usurpation), and Ahmad said the same. Our proof: ghasb is the removal of the owner's hand from the property and the placing of the usurper's hand on it, and that does not occur regarding the increment because it was not in the owner's hand. Therefore, it is not liable except upon transgression or withholding upon demand, because then he becomes a transgressor. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq 8/239)
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage establishes a key Hanafi principle regarding the increments (zawa'id) of usurped property. Increments include both connected increases (like fatness, beauty) and separate increases (like offspring, fruit, wool, milk). According to the Hanafi school, these increments are considered a trust (amanah) in the hands of the usurper. Therefore, the usurper is not automatically liable for them; he is only liable if he commits an act of transgression (ta'addi) against them (e.g., damages or uses them), or if he withholds them (man') after the owner demands their return. This contrasts with the position of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who hold that increments are absolutely liable on the usurper simply because the original transaction was ghasb (unlawful). The Hanafi proof is that ghasb only affects property that was in the owner's hand at the time of usurpation. Increments came into existence after the usurpation and were never in the owner's possession, so they are not subject to the same guarantee. They are treated as a trust, and liability only arises through subsequent misconduct.

Key Terms
الزوائد (al-zawa'id): Increments of the property, including both connected (e.g., fatness) and separate (e.g., offspring, fruit) additions.
الأمانة (al-amanah): A trust: property held by a person who is not liable for its loss except in cases of negligence or transgression.
التعدي (al-ta'addi): Transgression: any act that exceeds the bounds of lawful use, causing harm or destruction.
المنع (al-man'): Withholding: refusing to return the property after the owner's demand, which makes the holder a transgressor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi fiqh, when is the usurper liable for the increments (zawa'id) of usurped property?
A) Always, because the original act was ghasb
B) Only if the increments are connected (e.g., fatness)
C) Only if he commits transgression or withholds them after the owner's demand
D) Only if the increments are separate (e.g., offspring)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, increments of usurped property are a trust; liability only arises upon transgression (ta'addi) or withholding (man') after the owner's demand.

Offsetting Decrease with the Child's Value
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on offsetting decrease with child's value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a decrease in value due to childbirth is offset by the child's value and evaluate the Shafi'i/Zufar counter-argument.

Core Idea
The decrease and the child arise from one cause—liability is offset, not doubled.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a specific scenario: a usurped female slave gives birth, reducing her value. The Hanafi school holds that the child's value compensates for this loss, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school says: the decrease in value is a liability (mamun) on the usurper, but it is compensated (yujbar) by the child's value.
2
Identify the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i reject the compensation, arguing that the child is the usurper's property and cannot be used to offset his own liability.
They say: 'How can one's property compensate one's own property?'
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response: the cause (sabab) of both the decrease and the child is one—childbirth. The child's value naturally offsets the loss, so no net loss occurs.
The argument is based on causation (talil).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
This is not compensation with one's own property but a recognition that no actual loss remains after considering the new value generated from the same event.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under Hanafi law, if the child's value equals or exceeds the decrease, the usurper pays nothing; if less, he pays the difference. Under the opposing view, he pays the full decrease and keeps the child separately.
This shows the real-world impact of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نقصت بالولادة مضمون، ويجبر بولدها. وما نقصت إلخ: أي ما نقصت الجارية بالولادة مضمون على الغاصب، ولكن يجبر النقصان بولدها إن كان في قيمته وفاء، ويسقط ضمانه عن الغاصب، وإن لم يكن وفاء به يسقط بحسابه. وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يجبر النقصان بالولد؛ لأنه ملكه فكيف يجبر ملكه بملكه، ولنا أن سبب النقصان والزيادة واحد، وهو الولادة؛ لأنها أوجبت فوات جزء من مالية الأم، وحدوث مالية الولد، فإذا صار مالا انعدم ظهور النقصان به، فانتفى الضمان. رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۹/۲] مضمون هذا إذا حدث الحبل في يد الغاصب من غير المولى والزوج، أما إذا كان الحبل من أحدهما لا يجب عليه الضمان لا في النقصان ولا في الهلاك. (ملا مسكين) بولدها إن كان في قيمة الولد وفاء.
And whatever decreased (in value) due to childbirth is guaranteed, and it is compensated by her child. The explanation: The decrease in the female slave due to childbirth is guaranteed on the usurper, but the decrease is compensated by the child if its value is sufficient, and his liability for it is removed. If it is not sufficient, it is removed proportionally. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The decrease is not compensated by the child because it is his property, so how can his property be compensated by his property? Our proof: The cause (sabab) of the decrease and the increase is one, namely childbirth, because it caused the loss of part of the mother's value and the occurrence of the child's value. So when the child becomes property, the appearance of the decrease is removed by it, and liability is annulled. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/329) This applies when the pregnancy occurred in the hand of the usurper from someone other than the owner or husband. If the pregnancy was from one of them, then he is not liable for either the decrease or the destruction. (Mulla Miskin) with her child if the child's value is sufficient.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a female slave (jariyah) is usurped and then gives birth while in the usurper's possession, causing a decrease in her value (since childbirth can reduce the slave's market value). The ruling: the decrease is a liability (mamun) on the usurper, meaning he must compensate for it. However, the child born is also property that belongs to the usurper (as per the rule that increments of usurped property are a trust, but ownership of separate increments like offspring goes to the usurper upon payment of compensation? Actually, from earlier, increments are a trust but ownership follows the principle? Let's read the text: it says the decrease is compensated by the child. So the child's value is used to offset the liability for the decrease. The child itself becomes the property of the usurper (since it is a separate increment, but the text says 'his property' meaning the child is owned by the usurper). Zufar and al-Shafi'i object: you cannot compensate a deficiency with your own property (the child is owned by the usurper, so it's like paying yourself). The Hanafi response: the cause of both the decrease and the child is one and the same (childbirth). The decrease represents a loss of value from the mother, and the child represents a new value. Since both arise from the same event, the child's value offsets the decrease, effectively eliminating the loss. This is not compensating one's own property; it is a natural offset. The last note adds that if the pregnancy is from the owner or the husband (i.e., the father is the owner or husband of the slave), then the usurper is not liable at all for the decrease or destruction, because the increase (child) belongs to the owner or husband, not the usurper.

Key Terms
النقصان (al-nuqsan): The decrease in value of the property, here specifically the loss in value of a female slave after childbirth.
الولادة (al-wiladah): Childbirth, which causes both a loss in the mother's value and the creation of a new property (the child).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the decrease in value of a usurped female slave due to childbirth compensated?
A) The usurper must pay the full decrease to the owner
B) The usurper is not liable for the decrease
C) The decrease is compensated by the value of the child
D) The child is given to the owner as compensation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that when a single cause produces both loss and gain, the gain offsets the loss.

Zina with a Usurped Slave: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Liability for Death in Childbirth
Ruling on the usurper's liability if he commits zina with a usurped slave and she dies in childbirth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the liability of a usurper when a usurped slave dies from childbirth after being returned, and evaluate their respective proofs.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa holds the usurper liable for full value; the Sahibayn limit liability to the defect—the dispute hinges on the validity of the return.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about liability for damages when a usurper's actions lead to the death of a slave after her return. The case forces us to consider the conditions for a valid return (radd) of usurped property and the scope of liability for consequential harm. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply qiyas and distinguish between different types of defects.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Identify the key facts: a usurper commits zina with a usurped female slave, she becomes pregnant, the usurper returns her, and she later dies in childbirth while in the owner's possession.
The pregnancy is a direct result of the usurper's act (zina).
2
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
According to Abu Hanifa, the usurper is liable for the full value of the slave as of the day she became pregnant. His reasoning: the return was invalid because the slave was not returned in the same condition as taken (she now has a fatal defect). Therefore, the usurper remains responsible as if the slave was still in his possession.
He analogizes to a case where the slave commits a crime in the usurper's hand and is killed after return—then the usurper is liable for full value.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the usurper is only liable for the decrease in value due to the pregnancy (nuqsan al-hamil). They consider the return valid despite the pregnancy, treating pregnancy as a defect (ayb). Death from childbirth is a new event at the owner's, breaking causation.
They analogize to a case where a returned slave dies from a fever she had in the usurper's hands—only the defect is compensated.
4
Compare the analogies (crime/fever vs. pregnancy)
Examine the key difference in how each side views the casual link. Abu Hanifa sees death as a direct consequence of the pregnancy caused by the usurper. The Sahibayn see it as an independent incident after valid return.
This reflects a deeper dispute about the condition for valid return: is it necessary to return the property exactly as taken, or can a defect that is compensable be accepted?
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the usurper pays the full value; under the Sahibayn, only the depreciation due to pregnancy. The owner's recovery differs significantly.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi; it remains an intra-school ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زنى الجارية المغصوبة فحبلت ثم ردت إلى مالكها، فماتت عند المالك بسبب الولادة، ضمن الغاصب قيمتها يوم علقت، ولا يضمن الحرة أي ديتها إذا زنى بها فحبلت، ثم ماتت بالولادة، وهذا قول الإمام أبي حنيفة، وقالا لا يضمن الأمة أيضا، ويضمن نقصان الحبل؛ لأن الرد قد صح مع الحبل، والحبل عيب، فيجب عليه نقصان العيب وهلاكها بعد ذلك بسبب حادث عند المالك، فلا يبطل بالرد كما إذا حمت في يد الغاصب ،فردها، وماتت في تلك الحمى، أو زنت عند الغاصب ،فردها وجلدت بعد الرد عند المالك، وماتت من ذلك، فإنه لا يضمن إلا نقصان العيب، وكذا إذا سلم البائع الجارية للمشتري وهي حبلى، ولم يعلم المشتري بالحبل وماتت من الولادة، لم يرجع المشتري على البائع بشيء من الثمن اتفاقا وللإمام أنه لم يردها كما أخذها؛ لأنه أخذها وليس فيها عيب التلف وردها وفيها ذلك، فلم يصح الرد، فصار كما جنت جناية في يد الغاصب، فقتلت بها بعد الرد، أو دفعت بها بعد الرد، فإنه يرجع بقيمتها على الغاصب، بخلاف الحرة؛ فإنها لا تضمن بالغصب، وفي فصل الشراء الواجب التسليم، وبموتها لا ينعدم، وفي الغصب السلامة شرط لصحة الرد، فما لم يرد مثل ما أخذ لا يعتد به، =
And if the usurper commits zina with the usurped slave and she becomes pregnant, then she is returned to her owner, and she dies at the owner's due to childbirth, the usurper is liable for her value on the day she became pregnant. And he is not liable for a free woman (i.e., her blood money) if he committed zina with her and she became pregnant and then died in childbirth. This is the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not liable for the slave either; he is only liable for the decrease due to pregnancy (nuqsan al-hamil) because the return was valid despite the pregnancy, and the pregnancy is a defect. So he must pay for the defect (the decrease). And her death thereafter due to an incident occurring with the owner does not invalidate the return, similar to if she had a fever in the usurper's hand, then he returned her, and she died from that fever at the owner's; or if she committed a crime at the usurper's, then he returned her, and she was flogged after return at the owner's and died from that – in those cases, he is only liable for the defect. Similarly, if the seller delivers a slave to a buyer who is pregnant, and the buyer does not know of the pregnancy, and she dies from childbirth, the buyer does not claim anything from the seller by agreement. According to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), he (the usurper) did not return her as he took her; because he took her without a defect of destruction, and returned her with that, so the return is not valid. So it is as if she committed a crime in the hand of the usurper and was killed by it after return, or she was pushed after return, then he (the owner) claims her value from the usurper. Unlike a free woman, who is not guaranteed by ghasb. And in the chapter of sale, delivery is required, and her death does not negate it. In ghasb, safety is a condition for the validity of return, so if he does not return the same as he took, it is not considered.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific case: a usurper commits zina with a female slave he has usurped, she becomes pregnant, then he returns her to the owner, and she dies from complications of childbirth while in the owner's possession. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the usurper is liable for the full value of the slave as of the day she became pregnant (yawm 'uliqat). The reason is that the return was not valid because she now has a fatal defect (the pregnancy led to death). The Imam compares it to a case where the slave commits a crime (jinaayah) in the usurper's hand and then is killed by that crime after return; in that case, the usurper is liable for the full value. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they say the return is valid despite the pregnancy, and the pregnancy is just a defect (ayb). Therefore, the usurper is only liable for the decrease in value due to the defect (nuqsan al-hamil). Her death from childbirth is an independent event occurring at the owner's, so the usurper is not liable for it. They analogize to the case of a fever: if she had a fever in the usurper's hand, was returned, and then died from the fever at the owner's, the usurper is only liable for the defect (the fever's effect on value). The text also notes that in a sale scenario, if a buyer receives a pregnant slave and she dies from childbirth, the buyer has no claim against the seller by agreement. The passage ends with a note that in ghasb, the condition of safety (salamah) is required for the validity of the return, so if the returned property is not in the same condition as taken, the return is not considered valid.

Key Terms
الحبل (al-haml): Pregnancy (of the slave).
نقصان الحبل (nuqsan al-hamil): The decrease in value due to pregnancy (considered a defect in a slave).
الرد (al-radd): Return of the usurped property to the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is the usurper not liable for the full value of the slave when she dies from childbirth after being returned?
A) Because the return was invalid, so the death is not his fault.
B) Because the return was valid, and the death is a new incident at the owner's.
C) Because pregnancy is not a defect.
D) Because the slave was already pregnant before the usurper took her.

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on this case centres on whether return was valid; this determines if the usurper is liable for full value or only the defect.

The Validity of a Usurper's Disposition of Usurped Property
Ruling on usurper's sale and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the validity of a usurper's sale and explain why emancipation by a usurper is never valid.

Core Idea
A usurper's sale is valid only if the owner guarantees it; his emancipation is always invalid.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two critical fiqh rulings concerning the actions of a usurper (ghasib) on usurped property. We will dissect the condition under which a usurper's sale becomes executable (nāfidh) and understand the absolute invalidity of his emancipation, even if the owner later compensates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rulings
Read the base text: 'نفذ بيعه، وإن حرّره ثم ضمّنه لا.' Note that it first rules on the sale ('his sale is executed') and then on emancipation: 'even if he frees [the slave] and then guarantees, it is not [executed].'
2
Understand the Condition for Sale
The commentary clarifies: 'نفذ بيعه قيد نفاذ بيعه بتضمين المالك' — the sale is executed conditioned upon the owner's guarantee (taḍmīn). This means the owner accepts the sale and claims the price from the usurper.
The guarantee creates a retroactive validation of the usurper's ownership transfer.
3
Explore the Exceptions to the Sale
The passage lists scenarios that invalidate the usurper's sale even if he originally had a right to sell: the owner themselves sells the property, gives it as a gift, or dies and the usurper inherits it. In these cases, a final (bātt) ownership overrides the suspended (mawqūf) sale.
Inheritance gives ownership that is absolute and immediate, nullifying the conditional transfer.
4
Grasp the Distinction: Sale vs. Emancipation
Why can a sale be validated by subsequent guarantee, but not emancipation? Commentary: The slave was not the usurper's property at the time of freeing; therefore the act of emancipation (ʿitq) is a disposition that requires ownership at the moment of the act. The owner's later guarantee cannot retroactively create that ownership.
Sale is a transfer of ownership that can be ratified later; emancipation is an act that must be based on current ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفذ بيعه، وإن حرّره ثم ضمّنه لا. نفذ بيعه قيد نفاذ بيعه بتضمين المالك احترازا عما لو باعه الغاصب، فباعه المالك الغاصب أو من وهبه له، أو مات المالك والغاصب وارثه فإن بيع الغاصب يبطل في هذه الصور؛ لأنه طرء ملك بات على موقوف بأداء الضمان فأبطله، كذا بخط شيخنا (فتح) وإن حرره أي الغاصب العبد المغصوب. ثم ضمنه: أي ضمن المالك الغاصب
His sale is executed, and if he (the usurper) emancipates (the usurped slave) then (the owner) guarantees, it is not (executed). His sale is executed, conditioned upon the owner's guarantee, as a precaution against when the usurper sells it, then the owner (who is also the usurper?) sells it, or the one to whom he gifted it, or the owner dies and the usurper is his heir, then the usurper's sale is invalid in these cases; because ownership that was final (through inheritance) arrives upon a suspended (sale) by the payment of guarantee, so it invalidates it. Similarly in the handwriting of our teacher (Fath). And if he emancipates: meaning the usurper (emancipates) the usurped slave. Then he guarantees: meaning the owner guarantees the usurper.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the disposition of usurped property by the usurper. The base text states two rulings: (1) the usurper's sale of the usurped item is valid (nāfidh), but (2) if the usurper emancipates the slave and then the owner guarantees the value, the emancipation is not valid (lā). The commentary explains that the sale is valid only if the owner subsequently guarantees (taḍmīn) the value, meaning the owner accepts the sale and claims the price from the usurper. However, if the owner himself sells the item, or gives it as a gift, or dies and the usurper inherits it, then the usurper's sale becomes invalid because a final ownership (inheritance) overrides the suspended sale. As for emancipation, if the usurper frees the slave and then the owner guarantees, the emancipation is not effective because the slave was not his property at the time of emancipation; the guarantee does not retroactively validate the freeing.

Key Terms
غصب (ghaṣb): Wrongful taking and possession of another's property without right
تضمين (taḍmīn): Guarantee or compensation paid by the usurper to the owner for the usurped property
عتق (ʿitq): Emancipation of a slave
نفذ (nāfidh): Executed, legally effective
بطل (baṭala): To become void, invalid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the sale of usurped property by the usurper valid according to this passage?
A) The usurper must be the owner.
B) The owner must guarantee (taḍmīn) the value.
C) The sale must be done in public.
D) The property must be returned first.

Key Takeaway
A usurper's disposition of property: sale can be retroactively validated by the owner's guarantee, but emancipation is void regardless; certain events (owner's sale, gift, inheritance) supersede the usurper's sale.

Compensation for Childbirth Deficiency: The Hanafi Offset Rule
Dispute over offsetting deficiency with child's value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on compensation for deficiency due to childbirth in a usurped female slave and apply the exception when pregnancy is from the master or husband.

Core Idea
The child's value offsets the deficiency from childbirth, as both arise from the same cause.

This passage tackles a specific liability scenario: a usurper takes a female slave who later gives birth, reducing her value. The Hanafi school uniquely holds that the child's value compensates this loss. But Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree. Let's examine the reasoning and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that the deficiency is guaranteed upon the usurper, but it is compensated by the child if its value suffices. If not, compensation is proportional.
This applies only when pregnancy occurs in the usurper's possession from someone other than the master or husband.
2
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
The proof is rational: the same cause (childbirth) both decreases the mother's value and creates the child's value. Thus, the child's value effectively replaces the lost value, eliminating the apparent deficiency.
This is a causal argument (ta'līl) linking the loss and gain.
3
Contrast the opposing position
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the child is the usurper's property; one cannot compensate one's own liability with one's own property.
They view the child as separate wealth, not a direct offset.
4
Apply the exception
If the pregnancy is from the master (owner) or the husband, the usurper is not liable at all – neither for the deficiency nor for destruction – because the pregnancy was lawful.
This exception preserves the rights of the master/husband.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نقصت إلخ: أي ما نقصت الجارية بالولادة مضمون على الغاصب، ولكن يجبر النقصان بولدها إن كان في قيمته وفاء، ويسقط ضمانه عن الغاصب، وإن لم يكن وفاء به يسقط بحسابه. وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يجبر النقصان بالولد؛ لأنه ملكه فكيف يجبر ملكه بملكه، ولنا أن سبب النقصان والزيادة واحد، وهو الولادة؛ لأنها أوجبت فوات جزء من مالية الأم، وحدوث مالية الولد، فإذا صار مالا انعدم ظهور النقصان به، فانتفى الضمان. مضمون هذا إذا حدث الحبل في يد الغاصب من غير المولى والزوج، أما إذا كان الحبل من أحدهما لا يجب عليه الضمان لا في النقصان ولا في الهلاك.
And what is diminished etc.: meaning the deficiency in the slave girl due to childbirth is guaranteed upon the usurper, but the deficiency is compensated by her child if its value suffices, and the guarantee drops from the usurper; if it does not suffice, it drops proportionally. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The deficiency is not compensated by the child, because the child is his property, so how can his property be compensated by his property? And our proof: the cause of the deficiency and the increase is one, namely childbirth, because it caused the loss of part of the mother's value and the creation of the child's value, so when the child becomes wealth, the appearance of the deficiency disappears thereby, so the guarantee is negated. The meaning of this is when the pregnancy occurred in the usurper's hand from other than the master and husband; but if the pregnancy was from one of them, he is not liable for the deficiency nor for destruction.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a usurper takes a female slave (jāriya) who then gives birth while in his possession. The Hanafi ruling is that the deficiency (nuqṣān) in the slave's value due to childbirth is guaranteed upon the usurper, but this deficiency is compensated by the value of the child (walad). If the child's value covers the deficiency, the usurper's liability is fully discharged; if not, it is proportionally reduced. Zufar and Al-Shāfiʿī disagree, arguing that the child belongs to the usurper (as it was born in his possession), so how can his liability be offset by his own property? The Hanafi proof (lunā) is that the same cause—childbirth—both reduces the mother's value and creates the child's value. Therefore, the child's value replaces the lost value, eliminating the apparent deficiency. The text clarifies that this applies only if the pregnancy occurred from someone other than the owner (mawlā) or the husband. If the pregnancy is from either of them, the usurper is not liable at all for either the deficiency or destruction, because the pregnancy was lawful.

Key Terms
نقصان (nuqṣān): The decrease in value of the female slave due to childbirth
جارية (jāriya): Female slave
ولادة (wilādah): Childbirth
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper (one who takes property unlawfully)
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability, guarantee

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what happens to the usurper's liability for deficiency if the child's value equals the deficiency?
A) The usurper still pays half the deficiency.
B) The usurper's liability is fully discharged.
C) The child is returned to the owner to compensate.
D) The liability remains but is paid in installments.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, childbirth in a usurped slave causes both deficiency and increase from the same cause, so the child's value offsets the deficiency, with an exception for pregnancy from the master or husband.

Liability for Produce of Usurped Property
Inter-madhhab dispute on liability for produce of usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on produce of usurped property and explain why liability arises only upon transgression or refusal, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Produce of usurped property is a trust; usurper liable only for new transgression, not for mere possession.

When someone usurps property, does he automatically become liable for any produce that arises from it? The Hanafi school draws a careful distinction: the principal property is guaranteed, but its produce is treated as a trust. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that distinction and the conditions that trigger liability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school classifies all produce (zawāʾid) of usurped property as a trust (amānah) in the usurper's hand. The usurper is liable only if he commits transgression (taʿaddī) or refuses to return after the owner's demand.
Produce includes attached (fatness) and detached (milk, offspring, fruit).
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that al-Shāfiʿī and Aḥmad hold that all produce is automatically guaranteed (mḍmūn) because the usurper's possession itself is unlawful.
They rely on the broad definition of ghaṣb covering everything in the usurper's hand.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the opponent's argument: since the usurper's possession is transgressive from the start, everything that comes into his hand is also subject to the same liability.
Their dalīl is the definition of ghaṣb: 'taking another's property without right.'
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi proof: ghaṣb is defined as removing the owner's hand and establishing the usurper's hand over the same property. The produce was never in the owner's hand, so it is not covered by the original guarantee. A new cause (taʿaddī or al-manʿ) is required.
This is a rational (ʿaqlī) proof based on the nature of usurpation and trust.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi, a usurper who simply keeps the property without using it is not liable for its produce; under Shāfiʿī/Aḥmad, he is liable regardless.
The difference affects claims in court and the calculation of damages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوائد المغصوب إلخ: أي وزوائد المغصوب سواء كانت متصلة كالسمن والجمال، أو منفصلة كالولد والثمرة والصوف واللبن أمانة في يد الغاصب فيضمن الغاصب بالتعدي، أو بالمنع بعد طلب المالك. وقال الشافعي: هي مضمونة على الغاصب مطلقا، ولا فرق أن تكون الزيادة متصلة أو منفصلة؛ لوجود حد الغصب، وبه قال أحمد. ولنا أن الغصب إزالة يد المالك عنه وإثبات يد الغاصب، ولا يتحقق ذلك في الزيادة؛ لأنها لم تكن في يد المالك، فلا تضمن إلا بالتعدي أو بالمنع عند طلبه؛ لأنه يصير متعديا.
And the produce of the usurped property etc.: meaning the produce of the usurped property, whether it is attached like fatness and beauty, or detached like the child, fruit, wool, and milk, is a trust in the hand of the usurper, so the usurper guarantees (it) by transgression (ta'addī) or by refusal after the owner's demand. Al-Shafi'i said: It is guaranteed absolutely upon the usurper, regardless of whether the increase is attached or detached, due to the existence of the definition of usurpation; and Ahmad said the same. And our proof is that usurpation is removal of the owner's hand from (the principal) and establishing the usurper's hand, and that does not occur regarding the increase, because it was not in the owner's hand, so it is not guaranteed except by transgression or by refusal upon his demand; because he becomes a transgressor.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability for the produce (zawāʾid) of usurped property. The Hanafi position is that such produce is considered a trust (amānah) in the usurper's hands. The usurper is liable only if he transgresses (taʿaddī) by using it improperly, or if he refuses to return it after the owner demands it (al-manʿ baʿda ṭalab al-mālik). This applies whether the produce is attached (e.g., fatness, beauty) or detached (e.g., milk, wool, fruit, offspring). Al-Shafiʿī and Aḥmad hold that the produce is guaranteed in all cases because the usurper's possession itself is transgressive. The Hanafi proof (lunā) is that usurpation (ghaṣb) consists of removing the owner's hand and establishing the usurper's hand over the same property; the produce was never in the owner's hand, so it is not automatically guaranteed. Liability arises only from a new transgression or refusal to return.

Key Terms
زوائد (zawāʾid): Produce or increments from usurped property, e.g., offspring, milk, wool, fruit, fatness.
أمانة (amānah): A trust; property held without liability unless the holder transgresses or neglects.
تعدي (taʿaddī): Transgression or misuse beyond the bounds of trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a usurper liable for the produce of usurped property?
A) In all cases because possession was unlawful.
B) Only if the produce is attached to the property.
C) Only if he transgresses or refuses to return after demand.
D) Only if the produce is detached.

Key Takeaway
The produce of usurped property is a trust; liability requires a new act of transgression or refusal, not mere possession.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Usurper's Liability for Slave's Death After Zina
Explanation of intra-Hanafi dispute on liability of usurper for slave's death after zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a usurper's liability when a usurped slave dies in childbirth after zina, and identify the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Return of a usurped slave with a defect may or may not absolve the usurper of full value depending on the validity of the return.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. When a usurper commits zina with a slave, she becomes pregnant, is returned, and dies in childbirth, the key question is whether the return was valid. Abu Hanifa holds that the return is invalid because the slave was not returned in her original defect-free state, so the usurper owes full value. The Sahibayn argue that the return is valid; the pregnancy is a defect, so only the deficiency is owed. We will unpack the analogies and principles that shape each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the passage carefully: a usurper commits zina with a usurped female slave, she becomes pregnant, is returned to the owner, and then dies in childbirth at the owner's place. Identify the key facts and the legal question.
2
State the Position of Imam Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that the usurper guarantees the full value of the slave on the day she became pregnant. He also states that a free woman is not guaranteed (no diyah) because free persons are not subject to usurpation.
3
State the Position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the usurper does NOT guarantee the full value of the slave; instead, he guarantees only the deficiency (nuqsān) caused by the pregnancy. They also agree that a free woman is not guaranteed.
Their reasoning: the return was valid because pregnancy is a defect; the later death is a new accident at the owner's, so only the defect's deficiency is owed.
4
Analyze the Analogies Used by the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn compare this case to a slave who had a fever in the usurper's hand, was returned, and died from the fever after return. In that case, the usurper is only liable for the deficiency (the fever) not the death. Similarly, if the slave committed zina and was flogged after return and died, only the deficiency of the zina (as a defect) is owed.
Also compare to a sale where a pregnant slave is delivered without disclosure and dies: buyer has no recourse by consensus.
5
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the usurper did not return the slave as he took her: he took her without a defect leading to destruction and returned her with such a defect. Therefore, the return is invalid. The usurper remains in possession (as it were) and is liable for the full value. This is analogous to the slave committing a crime (jinaya) in the usurper's hand and being killed after return: the usurper is liable for the full value.
Note the distinction: in sale, delivery is obligatory and death does not nullify the obligation; in usurpation, safety is a condition for valid return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زنى إلخ أي لو زنى الغاصب بمغصوبة فحبلت ثم ردت إلى مالكها، فماتت عند المالك بسبب الولادة، ضمن الغاصب قيمتها يوم علقت، ولا يضمن الحرة أي ديتها إذا زنى بها فحبلت، ثم ماتت بالولادة، وهذا قول الإمام أبي حنيفة، وقالا لا يضمن الأمة أيضا، ويضمن نقصان الحبل؛ لأن الرد قد صح مع الحبل، والحبل عيب، فيجب عليه نقصان العيب وهلاكها بعد ذلك بسبب حادث عند المالك، فلا يبطل بالرد كما إذا حمت في يد الغاصب، فردها، وماتت في تلك الحمى، أو زنت عند الغاصب، فردها وجلدت بعد الرد عند المالك، وماتت من ذلك، فإنه لا يضمن إلا نقصان العيب، وكذا إذا سلم البائع الجارية للمشتري وهي حبلى، ولم يعلم المشتري بالحبل وماتت من الولادة، لم يرجع المشتري على البائع بشيء من الثمن اتفاقا. وللإمام أنه لم يردها كما أخذها؛ لأنه أخذها وليس فيها عيب التلف وردها وفيها ذلك، فلم يصح الرد، فصار كما جنت جناية في يد الغاصب، فقتلت بها بعد الرد، أو دفعت بها بعد الرد، فإنه يرجع بقيمتها على الغاصب، بخلاف الحرة؛ فإنها لا تضمن بالغصب، وفي فصل الشراء الواجب التسليم، وبموتها لا ينعدم، وفي الغصب السلامة شرط لصحة الرد، فما لم يرد مثل ما أخذ لا يعتد به، =  فماتت بالولادة ضمن قيمتها، ولا يضمن الحرة ومنافع الغاصب أو غيره فجعلت عند المالك أي بسبب الولادة أي الغاصب يوم علقت الغاصب وخمر المسلم أو خنزيره بالإتلاف
And if he commits zina etc.: meaning if the usurper commits zina with a usurped female, and she becomes pregnant, then she is returned to her owner, and she dies at the owner due to childbirth, the usurper guarantees her value on the day she became pregnant, and he does not guarantee the free woman i.e. her blood-money if he commits zina with her and she becomes pregnant and then dies in childbirth. This is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not guarantee the slave either, and he guarantees the deficiency of the pregnancy, because the return was valid with the pregnancy, and the pregnancy is a defect, so he is liable for the defect's deficiency, and her destruction after that is due to an accident at the owner, so it is not invalidated by the return, just as if she had a fever in the usurper's hand, he returned her, and she died from that fever, or she committed zina at the usurper, he returned her, and she was flogged after return at the owner, and died from that, then he does not guarantee except the deficiency of the defect. Similarly, if the seller delivered the slave girl to the buyer while she was pregnant, and the buyer did not know about the pregnancy, and she died from childbirth, the buyer does not recourse upon the seller for anything of the price by agreement. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa): He did not return her as he took her, because he took her without a defect of destruction and returned her with it, so the return was not valid, so it becomes as if she committed a crime (jinaya) in the usurper's hand, and she was killed by it after return, or she was pushed by it after return, then he recovers her value from the usurper, unlike the free woman, because she is not guaranteed by usurpation, and in the chapter of purchase, delivery is obligatory, and by her death it is not lost, and in usurpation, safety is a condition for the validity of return, so as long as he did not return the like of what he took, it is not considered. = She died in childbirth (so) he guarantees her value, and he does not guarantee the free woman, and the benefits of the usurper or other: they were made at the owner, i.e. due to childbirth, i.e. the usurper on the day she became pregnant, and the wine of the Muslim or his pig by destruction.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses a complex case: the usurper commits zina (fornication) with a usurped female slave (jāriya), she becomes pregnant, is returned to the owner, and then dies in childbirth. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the usurper is liable for the full value of the slave on the day she became pregnant (yawm ʿulliqat). He does not guarantee the value (diyah) of a free woman in a similar scenario, because free persons are not subject to usurpation. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that the usurper is not liable for the full value of the slave either; rather, he is only liable for the deficiency (nuqṣān) caused by the pregnancy (al-ḥaml). Their reasoning: the return was valid because the pregnancy is a defect, so upon return the usurper owes only the defect's deficit; the later death from childbirth is a new accident occurring after the return, so he is not liable for it. The text draws analogies: if the slave had a fever in the usurper's hand and died after return from that fever, or if she committed zina and was flogged after return and died, the usurper is only liable for the defect (i.e., the fever or the zina as a defect), not the death. The text also compares to a sale where the seller delivers a pregnant slave without disclosing, and she dies in childbirth; the buyer has no recourse, by consensus. For Abu Hanifa, the return is invalid because the usurper did not return the slave in the same condition as taken; he took her without a defect that leads to destruction, and returned her with it, so the return is void. Hence, the usurper is liable for the full value. The text then continues onto page 262, reiterating the liability for the slave but not the free woman, and introducing the next topics.

Key Terms
زنى (zinā): Unlawful sexual intercourse
علقت (ʿulliqat): Became pregnant (literally 'hung')
رد (radd): Return of the usurped property to the owner
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper, one who wrongfully takes property
نقصان (nuqṣān): Deficiency, decrease in value

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which analogy do the Sahibayn use to support their view that the usurper is only liable for the deficiency of the pregnancy?
A) A slave who is sold and dies in childbirth.
B) A slave who has a fever in the usurper's hand, is returned, and dies from the fever.
C) A slave who commits zina at the usurper and is flogged after return and dies.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
When a defect exists at the time of return, the Sahibayn hold the return valid and liability limited to the defect; Abu Hanifa requires return in the exact original condition for the return to be valid, thus imposing full liability.

Liability for a Usurped Pregnant Animal Dying in Childbirth
Clarifying liability for a usurped pregnant animal that dies in childbirth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation for a usurped animal that dies during childbirth and apply the causal principle that liability attaches only to the value at the time of usurpation.

Core Idea
If a usurped animal dies from natural childbirth, the usurper pays only the value at the time of usurpation, not the increase from pregnancy.

In the chapter on ghasp (usurpation), Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a specific case: a pregnant animal taken wrongfully dies while giving birth. The ruling clarifies that the usurper is not liable for the increased value of the pregnancy because the death was caused by the natural process of childbirth, not by any act of the usurper. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and also touches on a related scenario involving offspring lineage when zina is involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? A usurped pregnant animal dies during childbirth. According to the Hanafi school, the usurper must pay compensation (daman).
The act is ghasp (wrongful taking), and the damage is the death of the animal.
2
Determine the amount of compensation
The text states: 'he guarantees its value' but then limits it to 'the amount that was with him without the increase'. This means the value at the time of usurpation, not including any increase from pregnancy.
The pregnancy is considered an increase that arose after the usurpation, but the death was not caused by the usurper, so he does not pay for that increase.
3
Understand the causal reasoning
The commentator compares this to death by fever, where death occurs from internal causes (loss of strength, pain). Similarly, death in childbirth is caused by the natural process, not by any act of the usurper. Therefore, liability is limited to the base value.
The key distinction: if the usurper's action directly caused the death, he would be liable for the full value including increase.
4
Apply the extension: lineage of offspring if zina occurred
The passage adds a scenario: if the usurper commits zina with the animal and she conceives, and he claims the child, the lineage is established after compensating the owner. However, the child is a slave because freedom cannot be established through doubt (shubha).
This illustrates that compensation (daman) creates a degree of ambiguity (shubha) that affects lineage but not freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمغصوبة، فردّت، فماتت بالولادة ضمن قيمتها. فافترقا على أنه ممنوع، وفي الحمى الموت يحصل بزوال القوى وترادف الآلام، فلم يكن الموت حاصلا بسبب وجد في يد الغاصب، فيجب ضمان قدر ما كان عنده دون الزيادة. فماتت بالولادة إلخ: يعني ماتت بسبب الولادة لا على فورها، ولهذا قال في "النهاية": قيد بالموت في نفاسها ليكون الموت في أثر الولادة. ولو زنى بها واستولدها أي حبلت منه وادعى الولد، يثبت النسب بعد إرضاء المالك؛ لأن التضمين أورث شبهة، والنسب يثبت بها كما لو زفت إليه غير امرأته والولد رقيق؛ لأن الحرية لا تثبت بالشبهة كذا في "الدرر".
A usurped (pregnant animal), it was returned, and it died during childbirth: he guarantees its value. They differed that it is prohibited. In fever, death occurs by loss of strength and succession of pains, so death was not caused by something found in the hand of the usurper. Thus he must guarantee the amount that was with him without the increase. 'She died during childbirth, etc.' means she died due to childbirth, not immediately. Therefore, he said in al-Nihaya: it is restricted to death in her postpartum period so that death is in the aftermath of childbirth. And if he committed zina with her and impregnated her (i.e., she became pregnant from him) and he claimed the child, the lineage is established after satisfying the owner, because the guarantee caused a doubt, and lineage is established through it just as if someone else's wife was brought to him, and the child is a slave because freedom is not established through doubt. As in al-Durar.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on Ghasp concerning a usurped pregnant animal that dies during childbirth. The matn states that the usurper is liable for the value of the animal. The commentary clarifies that this liability is for the value at the time of usurpation, not including any increase from the pregnancy, because the death is caused by the childbirth itself, not by the usurper's action. The commentator distinguishes this from death by fever, where the cause is internal and the usurper is not liable for the full value. Then the passage discusses an additional scenario: if the usurper commits zina with the animal and she conceives, and he claims the offspring, the lineage is established after compensating the owner. However, the child is a slave because freedom cannot be established through shubha (ambiguity). This is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasp): Taking property wrongfully without the owner's consent in a way that causes liability.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurper takes a pregnant sheep, and it dies during childbirth, what is the usurper liable for?
A) Full value of the sheep and lamb.
B) Value of the sheep at the time of usurpation only.
C) Nothing, because death was natural.
D) Only the lamb.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a usurper is liable only for the value at the time of usurpation when the property perishes from an internal cause unrelated to his actions, such as childbirth or fever.

Ruling on Liability for a Free Person
Explaining that a free person is not subject to usurpation liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a free person in usurpation and explain the underlying reason based on the principle of ownership.

Core Idea
A free person cannot be owned, so they cannot be the object of usurpation guarantee.

This lesson examines a concise but fundamental rule in the Hanafi law of usurpation (ghasp): a free person is not subject to liability. This is not because the act is permissible, but because the legal basis for guarantee (daman) applies only to items that can be owned. Understanding this distinction clarifies why the guarantee persists even after an invalid return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the statement: 'ولا يضمن الحرة' — 'And he does not guarantee the free person.' This is the core ruling: no liability arises from the usurpation (ghasp) of a free person.
The pronoun 'he' refers to the usurper (ghāṣib).
2
Understand the Legal Reason (Ta‘līl)
The text explains: 'لأنها لا تضمن بالغصب' — 'because she is not liable in usurpation.' The reason is that a free person cannot be the subject of ownership, and the entire framework of daman (guarantee) in ghasp is built on property rights.
In Hanafi law, ghasp is the wrongful taking of another's property; a free person is not property.
3
Explore the Consequence of Invalid Return
The phrase 'ليبقى ضمان الغصب بعد فساد الردّ' — 'so that the guarantee of usurpation remains after the invalidity of the return' means that if the usurper returns the free person in a manner that is legally invalid (e.g., coercively or without proper release), the original guarantee remains unaffected. This is because the guarantee is attached to the act of usurpation itself, not to the person as property.
This contrasts with property, where a valid return extinguishes liability.
4
Apply to Free Persons Generally
The ruling applies to both male and female free persons; the feminine pronoun 'ha' is generic. Thus, no free person can be the object of ghasp liability.
Slaves, who are property, are subject to ghasp liability—this is a key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن الحرة. لأنها لا تضمن بالغصب؛ ليبقى ضمان الغصب بعد فساد الردّ.
And he does not guarantee the free person. Because she is not liable in usurpation; so that the guarantee of usurpation remains after the invalidity of the return.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This short ruling from the chapter on Ghasp states that a free person is not subject to the laws of usurpation. Since a free person cannot be owned, they cannot be the object of a guarantee (daman) in the same way as property. The commentator explains that the guarantee of usurpation remains even if the usurper returns the person, because the person is free and thus not the subject of a property-based transfer.

Key Terms
حر (hurr): Free person, as opposed to a slave.
ضمان الغصب (daman al-ghasp): Liability arising from usurpation, typically the value of the usurped property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a free person not liable in usurpation according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because a free person has no value.
B) Because a free person cannot be owned.
C) Because the usurper did not intend to take them.
D) Because the return invalidates the liability.

Key Takeaway
A free person is never subject to property-based usurpation liability because they are not ownable; any wrongful detention is addressed separately under tort law.

Ruling on Usufruct of Usurped Property: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on usufruct compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on usufruct of usurped property, explain the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi proof based on the nature of usufruct as an accident (arad).

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, usufruct of usurped property is not compensable because it is an accident occurring in the usurper's ownership.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq tackles a classic inter-madhhab dispute: if someone usurps property, must they pay for its use? The Hanafi school says no, even if the usurper benefited. The Shafi'i school says yes, because usufruct is valuable property. The Hanafi proof rests on the nature of usufruct as an accident (arad) that does not persist and thus cannot be owned by the original owner during the usurpation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its scope
Identify what the Hanafi school rules: the usurper does not guarantee the usufruct (manafi') of usurped property, whether he used it or left it idle. The position is absolute.
Note the examples: usurping a baker-slave for a month or smelling musk.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i view
State the Shafi'i (and Hanbali) position: the usurper must compensate for the usufruct because it is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that is guaranteed by contracts just like physical objects.
This is a disagreement over the nature of usufruct as property.
3
Analyze the primary Hanafi proof: usufruct as an accident
Understand the key argument: usufruct is an accident (arad) that does not persist. It comes into existence in the usurper's hand, not the owner's, because the owner does not possess the property during the usurpation. Therefore, the usufruct effectively belongs to the usurper as it arises in his ownership, and a person does not guarantee what he himself owns.
This is a reasoning based on the ontological status of usufruct.
4
Examine the companion precedent (Umar and Ali)
The Hanafi cite the case of al-maghrur (the deluded one) who mistakenly had intercourse with a slave girl. Umar and Ali ruled for return of the slave girl and dower but not compensation for the usufruct of her services or those of her children, implying usufruct is not compensable.
This is a qawl sahabi (companion statement) used as supporting evidence.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Identify the real-world difference: in a Hanafi court, the usurper pays nothing for use; in a Shafi'i court, he pays the market value. This affects litigation strategy and expectations.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنافع الغصب إلخ [ أي لا يضمن منافع الغصب مطلقا سواء صرفها إلى نفسه أو عطلها عطف على قوله: "الحرة" أي ولا يضمن منافع الغصب، صورته: إذا غصب عبدا خبازا مثلا، وأمسكه شهرا ثم رده إلى المالك، لا يضمن منافع الشهر عندنا، وكذا إذا غصب مسكا فشمه وردّه ونحو ذلك، وبه قال مالك، وقال الشافعي وأحمد يضمن؛ لأنها مال متقوم مضمون بالعقود كالأعيان. ولنا: أنها حصلت على الملك الغاصب فحدوثها في يده إذ لم تكن حادثة في يد المالك؛ لأنها أعراض لا تبقى فيملكها دفعا للحاجة، والإنسان لا يضمن ملك نفسه، ولأن عمر وعليا ما حكما بوجوب قيمة ولد المغرور وحريته ورد الجارية مع عقرها على المالك، ولم يحكما بوجوب أجر منافع الجارية والأولاد مع علمهما أن المستحق يطلب جميع حقه، وأن المغرور كان يستخدمها مع أولادها ولو كان ذلك له لما سكتا عن بيانه، وفي "إشارات الأسرار": المنافع لا تضمن بالغصب، سواء صرفها إلى نفسه، أو عطلها على المالك، وقال الشافعي: تضمن في الحالين، وقال صدر الإسلام البزدوي في شرح الكافى : ليس على الغاصب في ركوب الدابة وسكنى الدار أجر، وهو مذهب علمائنا.
And (the ruling on) the usufruct of usurpation, etc. i.e., he does not guarantee the usufruct of usurpation absolutely, whether he used it for himself or left it idle. It is connected to his statement: 'the free person' i.e., he does not guarantee the usufruct of usurpation. Its example: if he usurps a slave who is a baker, for instance, and kept him for a month then returned him to the owner, he does not guarantee the usufruct of the month in our school. Similarly, if he usurps musk and smells it and returns it and the like. Malik said likewise. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: he guarantees, because usufruct is valuable property guaranteed by contracts like physical objects. Our proof: it came into the ownership of the usurper, so its occurrence is in his hand since it did not occur in the hand of the owner, because they are accidents that do not last, so he owns them to meet a need, and a person does not guarantee what he himself owns. Also because Umar and Ali did not rule for the value of the offspring of the deluded one and his freedom and the return of the slave girl with her dower to the owner, and they did not rule for the wage for usufruct of the slave girl and the offspring, knowing that the claimant sought all his right and that the deluded one used her and her offspring; if it were due, they would not have remained silent about its statement. In 'Isharat al-Asrar': usufruct is not guaranteed through usurpation, whether he used it himself or left it idle. Al-Shafi'i said: it is guaranteed in both cases. Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi said in Sharh al-Kafi: There is no wage on the usurper for riding the animal or living in the house; this is the madhhab of our scholars.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the usufruct (manafi') of usurped property. The matn states that the usurper is not liable for the usufruct, whether he actually used the property himself or simply kept it idle and did not allow the owner to benefit from it. The commentator provides an example: if someone usurps a slave baker and keeps him for a month, he does not owe the value of the baking services. Similarly, if he usurps a piece of musk and smells it, he owes nothing for the scent. Imam Malik agreed with this position. However, Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad held that the usurper must pay for the usufruct because they consider usufruct to be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that is guaranteed by contracts just like physical objects. The Hanafi refutation is based on several arguments: (1) The usufruct arises in the hands of the usurper, not in the hands of the owner, because usufruct is an accident (arad) that does not persist. Since it occurs during the period of usurpation, it effectively becomes part of the usurper's ownership. (2) A person does not guarantee what he himself owns. (3) The precedent of Caliphs Umar and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) in the case of the 'deluded one' (al-maghrur) who had intercourse with a slave girl mistakenly: they only required the return of the slave girl and payment of the dower, not compensation for the usufruct derived from her services or those of her children. This shows that usufruct during wrongful possession is not compensable according to the Hanafi school. The commentary further cites Ibarat al-Asrar and the statement of Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi affirming the Hanafi position. Note that the Shafi'i position, mentioned for comparison, holds that usufruct is guaranteed in both cases of use or disuse.

Key Terms
منافع (manafi'): Usufruct or benefits derived from property, such as services or use.
عارض (arad): Accident; a non-permanent attribute that does not persist from one moment to the next.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person usurps a baker-slave for a month and returns him, what is the ruling on compensation for the baking services?
A) The usurper must pay the market wage for a month.
B) The usurper must pay nothing for the services.
C) The usurper must pay only if the slave was actually used.
D) The usurper must pay double the value.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, usufruct of usurped property is not compensable because it is an accident that arises in the usurper's hand, not a persistent property of the owner.

Liability for Destroying Wine and Pig: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi inter-madhhab dispute on liability for destroying dhimmi's wine/pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for destroying a dhimmi's wine or pig, explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school, and apply the intra-Hanafi differences regarding conversion to Islam.

Core Idea
Wine and pig are not property for Muslims but are for dhimmis—liability depends on the owner's legal capacity.

This passage explores a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh where the concept of mutaqawwim (valuable property) intersects with the rights of non-Muslim subjects. While Muslims cannot own wine or pigs, dhimmis can, and this affects the liability of anyone who destroys them. We will examine the Hanafi position, its contrast with the Shafi'i school, and the intra-Hanafi disagreements on the effect of conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the owner and the act
Determine who owns the wine or pig (Muslim or dhimmi) and that the act is destruction (itlaf).
2
Apply the mutaqawwim principle
For a Muslim, these items are not mutaqawwim, so no liability. For a dhimmi, they are mutaqawwim, so the destroyer is liable.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school holds no liability even for a dhimmi's wine/pig. Compare the dalil: Hanafi relies on 'leave them and what they practice'; Shafi'i argues that dhimmis follow Muslims in rulings.
thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi court compensates, Shafi'i does not.
4
Examine intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on conversion
If the claimant converts to Islam after judgment, Abu Yusuf says liability drops; Muhammad says value remains due. Both are riwayat from Abu Hanifa.
The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view (liability drops).
5
Apply to dhimmi vs. dhimmi case
A Muslim destroyer pays value (qimah); a dhimmi destroyer pays the like (mithl) because he can lawfully possess it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخمر المسلم إلخ عطف على قوله : "المنافع" أي لا يضمن خمر المسلم أو خنزيره بالإتلاف؛ لعدم تقومها في حقه، وضمن المتلف لو كانا أي الخمر والخنزير لذمي؛ لأنهما مال في حقه، وقد أمرنا أن نتركهم وما يدينون، وعند الشافعي: لا يضمنها لذمي أيضا؛ لأنهما غير متقومين في حق المسلم، فوجب أن يكونا في حقهم كذلك؛ لأنهم أتباع لنا في الأحكام، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أتلفهما ذمي لذمي، ثم عندنا يجب على المسلم قيمتهما، وإن كانت من ذوات الأمثال؛ لأن المسلم ممنوع من تمليكه وتملكه إياها بخلاف الذمي إذا استهلك خمر الذمي حيث يجب عليه مثلها؛ لقدرته عليه، ولو أسلم الطالب بعد ما قضى له بمثلها ، فلا شيء على المطلوب؛ لأن الخمر في حقه ليست متقومة، فكان بإسلامه مبرئا عما كان في ذمته من الخمر، وكذا لو أسلما، ولو أسلم المطلوب وحده، أو أسلم المطلوب ثم الطالب بعده، قال أبو يوسف: لا يجب عليه شيء، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، وقال محمد: يجب عليه قيمة الخمر، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة
And the wine of the Muslim, etc., is connected to his statement: 'the usufruct' i.e., he does not guarantee the wine of a Muslim or his pig through destruction, because they are not valuable (mutaqawwim) in his right. And the destroyer guarantees if the two (i.e., wine and pig) belong to a dhimmi, because they are property in his right, and we have been commanded to leave them and what they practice. According to al-Shafi'i: he does not guarantee them for a dhimmi either, because they are not valuable in the right of a Muslim, so they must be likewise in their right, because they follow us in rulings. According to this difference, if a dhimmi destroys them for a dhimmi, then in our school, a Muslim must pay their value, even if they are fungible goods (thawat al-amthal), because the Muslim is prohibited from owning them and making them his property, unlike the dhimmi. If the dhimmi consumes the wine of another dhimmi, he must pay the like (mithl) because he is capable of it. And if the plaintiff (claimant) becomes Muslim after judgment was made for him to receive its like, then nothing is owed by the defendant, because the wine in his right is not valuable, so his Islam made him free from what was in his dhimmah (liability) of wine. Similarly if both become Muslim. And if only the defendant becomes Muslim, or the defendant then the plaintiff afterwards, Abu Yusuf said: nothing is due, and this is a riwayah from Abu Hanifa. Muhammad said: the value of the wine is due, and this is also a riwayah from Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability for destroying wine and pigs, distinguishing between a Muslim owner and a dhimmi owner. The matn states that the usurper is not liable for destroying the wine or pig of a Muslim because these items are not considered valuable property (mutaqawwim) in the sight of the Muslim law. However, if the wine or pig belongs to a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected subject), the destroyer is liable because these items are considered property in the dhimmi's law. The Hanafi school bases this on the principle of leaving dhimmis to their own religious practices. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i held that even a dhimmi's wine and pig are not guaranteed, because they are not valuable in Islamic law and dhimmis are subject to the same rulings as Muslims. The passage further discusses the case where a dhimmi destroys another dhimmi's wine: the Hanafi view is that the Muslim destroyer must pay the value, not the exact item, because Muslims cannot lawfully possess wine. But if a dhimmi destroys another dhimmi's wine, he must pay the like (mithl) because he can possess it. Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if the claimant becomes Muslim after the judgment, Abu Yusuf says nothing is due; Muhammad says the value is due. Both are transmissions from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Valuable; property that is legally recognized as having monetary value and can be owned and traded.
ذمي (dhimmi): Non-Muslim subject living under the protection of an Islamic state, who pays jizyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a Muslim destroys the wine of a dhimmi, what is the ruling?
A) No liability because wine is not valuable.
B) The Muslim must pay a fine but not the value.
C) The Muslim must pay the value of the wine.
D) The Muslim must replace it with like wine.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's wine and pig are considered valuable property in Hanafi law, making destruction compensable, unlike for a Muslim.

Rulings on Usurped Wine Turned into Vinegar and Hide Tanned
Rulings on owner's right to take transformed goods and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the owner's right to take transformed usurped property and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for destruction of tanned hide.

Core Idea
Transformation does not transfer ownership; the usurper pays for added value only if it's a separate commodity.

This lesson examines two specific cases of property transformation (taghyir) by a usurper: turning wine into vinegar and tanning a dead animal's hide. These cases raise questions of ownership of the transformed item and liability for its loss. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples on whether the usurper must guarantee the value of the tanned hide if destroyed. Understanding this dispute illuminates the school's principles on the intersection of ownership, value addition, and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two transactions
The source discusses two distinct acts: (1) usurping wine from a Muslim and turning it into vinegar, and (2) usurping the hide of a dead animal and tanning it.
Tanning here uses valuable materials like oak bark or alum.
2
State the ruling for the owner's right to take back
For both cases, the owner may take the transformed product: the vinegar or the tanned hide. However, for the tanned hide, the owner must pay the usurper the increase in value due to tanning.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on destruction
If the usurper destroys the items, there is disagreement: Abu Hanifa says he guarantees only the vinegar (not the hide), while the two companions say he guarantees the tanned hide's value (minus the increase paid back by the owner). Determine the practical difference.
The practical effect (thamarah al-khilaf): Under Abu Hanifa, the owner bears the loss of the hide; under the Sahibayn, the owner receives the value of the tanned hide less the increase.
4
Trace the qiyas reasoning
Understand the analogy to dyed cloth: just as the owner of usurped cloth dyed by the usurper may take the dyed cloth and pay for the dye's value, here the owner may take the tanned hide and pay for the tanning increase. This is because the tanning agent is a separate valuable property that becomes attached.
The illah (effective cause) is the addition of value through the usurper's own property.
5
Identify the consensus and exception
There is consensus that if the items perish without act of the usurper (e.g., natural decay), he is not liable. Also, if the tanning is done with worthless materials, the usurper is liable anyway (because no increase to deduct).
This shows that liability hinges on the usurper's active destruction or use of his own property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن غصب من مسلم خمرا فخلل، أو جلد ميتة، فدبغ، فللمالك أخذهما، فردّ ما زاد بالدباغ، وإن أتلفهما، ضمن الخل فقط، دون الجلد عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: يضمن قيمة الجلد مدبوغا ويعطي المالك الغاصب ما زاد الدباغ فيه، ولو هلك الجلد والخل في يده، لا يضمن بالإجماع، وجه :قولهما أن ملكه باقٍ فيه ولهذا يأخذه، وهو مال متقوم فيضمنه له مدبوغا بالاستهلاك كما في مسألة الخل، ثم يعطيه المالك ما زاد الدباغ فيه بخلاف الهلاك؛ لأنه لم يوجد منه التفويت، ولو دبغه بشيء لا قيمة له، يضمنه
And if he usurps from a Muslim wine and then makes it vinegar, or (usurps) the hide of a dead animal and then tans it, the owner may take both of them, and the usurper returns the increase due to tanning. And if he destroys them, he guarantees only the vinegar, not the hide according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): he guarantees the value of the tanned hide and the owner gives the usurper the increase due to tanning. And if the hide and vinegar perish in his hand, he is not liable by consensus. The reasoning of the two companions: the owner's ownership remains in it, therefore he takes it, and it is valuable property, so he guarantees it tanned through consumption as in the case of the vinegar, then the owner gives him the increase due to tanning. This differs from the case of destruction because no loss was caused by him. And if he tans it with something of no value, he guarantees it.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi positions on the transformation (taghyir) of usurped property. Two cases are considered: (1) Wine turned into vinegar: The rule is that if the usurper transforms the wine into vinegar (by exposing it to air/sunlight or adding something), the owner may take the vinegar. The increase in value due to the transformation is not compensated, as the vinegar is considered the owner's property by purification. (2) Hide of a dead animal tanned: If the usurper tans the hide with valuable tanning agents, the owner may take the tanned hide but must pay the usurper the increase in value from the tanning. If the usurper destroys the items, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says he only guarantees the vinegar, not the hide; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he guarantees the tanned hide's value but the owner must pay the increase. The consensus is that if the items perish without the usurper's fault, no liability. The commentary also notes the distinction: tanning adds a separate valuable component (the tanning agent), like dyeing cloth, while making vinegar does not add material value, so the vinegar is simply the original wine in a purified state. Finally, if the tanning is done with worthless materials, the usurper guarantees the hide anyway.

Key Terms
تخليل (takhlil): The process of turning wine into vinegar by natural means.
دباغ (dibagh): Tanning of a hide using materials that make it pure and usable.
إتلاف (itlaf): Destruction or consumption of property causing loss.
تغصيب (taghsib): Usurpation; taking property without owner's consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, if a usurper tans a stolen hide with valuable materials and then destroys it, what is the outcome?
A) The usurper pays nothing because the hide originally had no value.
B) The usurper pays the full value of the tanned hide, and the owner keeps the entire amount.
C) The usurper pays the value of the tanned hide, and the owner pays back the increase due to tanning.
D) The owner may take the hide if it still exists, but if destroyed, no compensation.

Key Takeaway
Usurpation does not transfer ownership; the owner may reclaim transformed property, but must compensate the usurper for added value from his materials, and liability for destruction follows the two companions' view.

Ghasb of Wine and Animal Skin: Vinegar and Tanning
Rulings on ghasb of wine turned to vinegar and tanned skin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of vinegar made from usurped wine and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation.

Core Idea
Making vinegar from usurped wine does not transfer ownership if done without value; tanning adds value requiring compensation.

This lesson examines the fiqh of ghasb when the usurped property undergoes transformation. Can a usurper become owner by changing wine into vinegar or tanning a hide? The Hanafi school addresses this with a nuanced distinction between purification and value addition, leading to a split among the three imams. Understanding this helps resolve modern disputes over unauthorized processing of property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the property
Determine that the case is ghasb (usurpation) of wine and a dead animal skin, followed by transformation: making wine into vinegar (takhleel) and tanning the skin (dibagh).
The transformation is done by the usurper without permission.
2
Distinguish the two transformations
Recognize that making vinegar is a purification process (like washing a najis garment), while tanning attaches a valuable substance (like dyeing a cloth). This distinction determines ownership.
Imam Abu Hanifa uses qiyas: takhleel is like washing; dibagh is like dyeing.
3
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
For vinegar: the original owner takes the vinegar without paying anything (the transformation does not create value). For tanned skin: the owner takes the tanned skin but must pay the usurper the increase in value caused by tanning.
If destroyed, the usurper guarantees only the vinegar (not the skin) according to Abu Hanifa.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that nothing is owed for either transformation (a narration from Abu Hanifa). Muhammad holds that the usurper owes the value of the wine (another narration).
These are positions from the Sahibayn, both attributed to Abu Hanifa as narrations.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: the original owner remains owner of the vinegar and the tanned skin (with compensation for increase). This is the basis for fatwa.
The thamarah al-khilaf appears in cases of destruction: only vinegar is guaranteed, not the tanned skin.
