Agent as Opponent and Pre-emptor's Options in Pre-emption
Ruling that agent is opponent until delivery, and pre-emptor has options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the agent is the opponent until delivery, distinguish it from the opposing school's view, and apply the pre-emptor's options of inspection and defect independently of the buyer's actions.

Core Idea
The agent who buys is the opponent for the pre-emptor until delivery; the pre-emptor retains independent options regardless of the buyer's actions.

This lesson addresses two important issues in Hanafi pre-emption (shuf'a) law. First, who is the proper defendant when the property was purchased by an agent? The Hanafi school holds that the agent himself is the opponent until he transfers the property to his principal. Second, does the pre-emptor have the right to inspect the property and return it for defects, even if the buyer had already waived those rights? The answer is yes, by consensus. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly litigating pre-emption claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent's role as contracting party
The agent who purchases the property is the actual contractor (عاقد). The rights and obligations of the contract belong to him, not to the principal (owner). Therefore, he is the natural opponent for the pre-emptor in court.
2
Determine when the agent ceases to be opponent
The agent remains the opponent only 'as long as he has not delivered' (ما لم يسلم) the property to the principal. After delivery, the agent no longer has any connection to the property, and the principal becomes the opponent.
This same rule applies to a father or executor who buys for a minor.
3
Contrast with the opposing school's view
According to the three other Sunni schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), the principal is the opponent from the start, since the agent is merely a messenger (سفير). The Hanafi response is that the agent, as contractor, has the right to defend the contract.
Practical difference: Hanafis require the agent's presence in court; others require the principal's.
4
Recognize the pre-emptor's independent options
The pre-emptor has the option of inspection (خيار الرؤية) and the option of defect (خيار العيب) by consensus, even if the buyer had waived them. This is because the pre-emptor's taking of the property constitutes a new purchase from the buyer (or seller, depending on timing). He is not bound by the buyer's actions.
The buyer's inspection or waiver does not affect the pre-emptor's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوكيل بالشراء خصم للشفيع ما لم يسلّم إلى الموكل، فلا يشترط للقضاء حضور الموكل ولا كذلك البائع؛ لأنه ليس بنائب عن المشتري، فلا يكتفي بحضوره، بخلاف الوكيل والأب ووصيه كالوكيل. ما لم يسلم إلى الموكل: أي الوكيل بالشراء خصم للشفيع ما لم يسلم إلى الموكل؛ لأن الخصومة فيه من حقوق العقد، وهي إلى العاقد أصيلا كان أو وكيلا، ولهذا لو كان البائع وكيلا للشفيع أن يخاصمه، ويأخذها منه بحضور المشتري، كما إذا كان البائع هو المالك إلا أنه إذا سلمها إلى الموكل، لا يد للوكيل، ولا ملك له، ولا يكون خصما بعده، فصار كالبائع، فإنه يكون خصما ما لم يسلمه إلى المشتري، فإذا سلمها إليه لم يبق له يد، ولا ملك، فيخرج من أن يكون خصما. وعند الثلاثة الخصم هو الموكل ابتداء؛ لأن الوكيل بمنزلة السفير. وفي "التاتارخانية": لو اشترى لغيره بغير أمره، فهو خصم ما لم يسلم العين إلى من اشتراها له. وللشفيع خيار الرؤية والعيب وإن شرط المشتري البراءة من العيب، وهذا بالإجماع؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة شراء من المشتري إن كان الأخذ بعد القبض، وإن كان قبله فهو من البائع، فيثبت له الخيار. ولا يسقط الخيار برؤية المشتري، وبشرط براءته؛ لأن الشفيع ليس بنائب عنه، فلا يسقط حقه بإسقاط المشتري.
And the agent for purchase is an opponent for the pre-emptor as long as he has not delivered to the principal. So the presence of the principal is not required for judgment, nor is the seller like that because he is not a deputy of the buyer, so his presence is not sufficient, unlike the agent, the father, and the executor who are like the agent. 'As long as he has not delivered to the principal' means the agent for purchase is an opponent for the pre-emptor as long as he has not delivered to the principal because the litigation therein is among the rights of the contract, which belong to the contractor whether he is principal or agent. For this reason, even if the seller is an agent, the pre-emptor may litigate against him and take the property from him in the presence of the buyer, just as if the seller were the owner. However, when he delivers to the principal, the agent has no hand nor ownership, so he cannot be an opponent thereafter, becoming like the seller. According to the three (schools), the opponent is the principal from the beginning because the agent is like a messenger. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: If someone buys for another without his order, he is an opponent as long as he has not delivered the property to the one for whom he bought. And the pre-emptor has the option of inspection and defect, even if the buyer stipulated his exemption from defects; this is by consensus because taking by pre-emption is a purchase from the buyer if the taking is after possession, and if before then it is from the seller, so the option is established for him. The option is not dropped by the buyer's inspection or by his stipulation of exemption because the pre-emptor is not his deputy, so his right is not dropped by the buyer's dropping.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage deals with two distinct issues in pre-emption: who is the correct opponent in litigation, and what options the pre-emptor has. First, the agent who actually purchased the property on behalf of someone else is the proper opponent for the pre-emptor, as long as the agent has not yet delivered the property to his principal. This is because the rights arising from the contract belong to the contracting party (the agent) rather than the owner (the principal). The same rule applies to a father or executor who buys for a minor. The seller is not an opponent after the sale is complete. However, the three other schools hold that the principal is the opponent from the start, since the agent is merely a messenger. Second, the pre-emptor has the option of inspection (خيار الرؤية) and the option of defect (خيار العيب), even if the buyer had waived his own option regarding defects. This is by consensus. The rationale is that the pre-emptor is taking the property by purchase: if he takes after the buyer has taken delivery, he buys from the buyer; if before, he buys from the seller. In either case, he has the right to inspect and to return for defects. The buyer's waiver does not bind the pre-emptor because the pre-emptor is not the buyer's agent or representative.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالشراء (al-wakīl bi al-shirā’): An agent appointed to purchase property; he is the contracting party.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’ya): The right to inspect the property before completing the purchase and to nullify the sale if dissatisfied.
خيار العيب (khiyār al-‘ayb): The right to return the property for a hidden defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true regarding the pre-emptor's options of inspection and defect according to the passage?
A) They are waived if the buyer inspected the property.
B) They are waived if the buyer stipulated exemption from defects.
C) They are retained by the pre-emptor regardless of the buyer's actions.
D) They apply only if the pre-emptor takes possession before the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The agent who purchases property is the opponent for the pre-emptor until delivery; and the pre-emptor's options of inspection and defect are independent of the buyer's actions.

Dispute Over Price Between Pre-emptor and Buyer
Rulings on price dispute between pre-emptor and buyer, with evidence and seller involvement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price, identify the exception when both bring evidence, and apply the rule when the seller's claim is involved.

Core Idea
In a price dispute, the buyer's word is taken; but if both bring evidence, the pre-emptor's evidence prevails because it imposes an obligation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common litigation scenario: the pre-emptor claims a lower purchase price while the buyer claims a higher one. The Hanafi school provides nuanced rules based on who is the denier, the presence of evidence, and the role of the seller. We will unpack the default rule, the exception when both parties produce evidence, and the special case involving the seller's claim, including the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule and its rationale
The passage states that when the pre-emptor and buyer differ, the buyer's word is taken (with his oath) because the pre-emptor is claiming an obligation on the buyer, while the buyer denies it. The denier's word is preferred in Hanafi procedure.
This is consistent with the general principle that the denier's statement is accepted with an oath.
2
Understand why mutual oaths (taḥāluf) do not apply
Mutual oaths are prescribed only when both parties deny each other's claims. Here, only the buyer denies; the pre-emptor is the claimant. Therefore, the situation does not fit the text (nass) on mutual oaths, and analogy (qiyas) is not permitted.
The Hanafi rigorous usul prevents analogical extension of a specific textual ruling.
3
Analyze the evidence scenario: both parties bring witnesses
If each produces evidence, the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sheikhayn), but Abu Yusuf prefers the buyer's evidence. The passage also notes positions of Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik.
The ikhtilaf is both intra-Hanafi (Abu Yusuf) and inter-madhhab.
4
Grasp the underlying reasoning (talil)
The Sheikhayn argue that evidence is for imposing obligations (ilzam). The pre-emptor's evidence, if accepted, forces the buyer to deliver the property; the buyer's evidence only gives the pre-emptor an option (to take or leave). Thus the pre-emptor's evidence is more probative in meaning (akthar ithbātan maʿnan).
Even though the buyer's evidence shows a higher price (more probative in form), the legal effect makes the pre-emptor's evidence stronger.
5
Apply the rule when the seller's claim intervenes
If the buyer's seller gives a different price, the ruling depends on payment: if the price has not been paid, the pre-emptor takes at the seller's word; if fully paid (proven by evidence or oath), the pre-emptor takes at the buyer's word, because the seller has exited the dispute. If payment is not apparent, the seller's word is taken.
This scenario confirms that the default rule (buyer's word) applies when the only dispute is between pre-emptor and buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في مقدار الثمن، فقال الشفيع اشتريتها بمائة، وقال المشتري: اشتريتها بمائة وعشرين، فالقول للمشتري؛ لأن الشفيع يدعي عليه استحقاق الأخذ عند نقد الأقل، والمشتري ينكر ذلك، والقول للمنكر مع يمينه ولا يتحالفان؛ لأن التحالف عرف بالنص فيما إذا وجد الإنكار الجانبين، والدعوى من الجانبين والمشتري لا يدعي على الشفيع شيئا ولا يكون الشفيع منكرا، ولا يكون في معنى ما ورد به النص، فامتنع القياس. قال في "التكملة"، وأطلق المؤلف محله، فشمل ما إذا وقع الاختلاف قبل قبض الدار، ونقد الثمن، أو بعدهما قبل التسليم إلى الشفيع أو بعده، لكن في "التاتارخانية": اشترى دارا، وقبضها ونقد الثمن، ثم اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في الثمن، فالقول للمشتري. وإن برهنا فللشفيع، يعني لو أقام كل واحد من الشفيع والمشتري البينة على دعواه، فالبينة بينة الشفيع عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف البينة بينة المشتري؛ لأنها تثبت للزيادة والبينة المثبتة للزيادة أولى. وعند الشافعي وأحمد تهاترتا، والقول للمشتري، وعنهما يقرع، وعند مالك يحكم بالأعدل وإلا باليمين. ولهما: أن بينة الشفيع أكثر إثباتا معنى وإن كانت بينة المشتري أكثر إثباتا صورة؛ لأن البينات للإلزام، وبينة الشفيع ملزمة، بخلاف بينة المشتري، فإن بينة الشفيع إن قبلت وجب على المشتري تسليم الدار إليه، وإذا قبلت بينة المشتري لا يجب على الشفيع شيء، بل يتخير بين الأخذ والترك. وإن ادعى المشتري ثمنا، وادعى بائعه أقل منه، ولم يقبض الثمن، أخذها الشفيع بما قال البائع، وإن قبض أخذها بما قال المشتري. يعني لو أقام كل واحد من الشفيع والمشتري البينة على دعواه، فالبينة بينة الشفيع عندهما، وعند أبي يوسف البينة بينة المشتري. وإن قبض جميع الثمن، حتى لو قبض بعضه وبقي منه، فالقول قول البائع. وإنما يأخذها بما قال المشتري إذا ثبت قبض جميع الثمن بالبينة أو اليمين؛ لأن البائع باستيفائه الثمن خرج من البين، والتحق بالأجانب، فبقي الاختلاف بين الشفيع والمشتري، وقد ثبت أن القول فيه للمشتري. قال في "الشرنبلالية": هذا إذا كان قبض الثمن ظاهرا بأن ثبت بالبينة أو اليمين، ولو كان غير ظاهر، فقال البائع: بعت الدار بألف وقبضت الثمن، يأخذها الشفيع بألف.
And if the pre-emptor and the buyer differ over the amount of the price, the pre-emptor saying 'I bought it for one hundred' and the buyer saying 'I bought it for one hundred and twenty', then the word is the buyer's because the pre-emptor claims upon him the right to take upon paying the lesser amount, and the buyer denies that, and the word is the denier's with his oath, and they do not take mutual oaths because mutual oaths are known by textual evidence only when denial is from both sides, and here the claim is from one side and the buyer does not claim anything from the pre-emptor, nor is the pre-emptor a denier, so it is not analogous to the text. In al-Tatarkhaniyyah: if someone buys a house, takes possession and pays the price, then the pre-emptor and buyer differ over the price, the buyer's word is taken. And if both bring evidence, then the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), but according to Abu Yusuf the buyer's evidence is preferred because it proves an increase, and the evidence proving an increase is more deserving. According to Shafi'i and Ahmad, the evidence cancels out and the buyer's word is taken; according to them (other narration) they draw lots; according to Malik, the more just witness is preferred, otherwise the oath. Their (the two's) argument: the pre-emptor's evidence is more probative in meaning, even if the buyer's evidence is more probative in form, because evidence is for imposing obligation, and the pre-emptor's evidence is obligatory, unlike the buyer's evidence. If the pre-emptor's evidence is accepted, the buyer must deliver the house to him; if the buyer's evidence is accepted, nothing is obligatory on the pre-emptor, he merely chooses between taking and leaving. And if the buyer claims a price and his seller claims less, and the price has not been paid, then the pre-emptor takes at the seller's word; if paid, he takes at the buyer's word. It means if the full price has been paid, even if part was paid but some remains, the seller's word is taken. The pre-emptor only takes at the buyer's word if full payment is established by evidence or oath, because the seller, by receiving the price, exits the picture and becomes a third party, so the dispute remains between pre-emptor and buyer, and it has been established that the buyer's word is taken. Al-Shurunbulali says: this is when the payment is apparent, established by evidence or oath; if it is not apparent, and the seller says 'I sold the house for one thousand and received the price', then the pre-emptor takes it for one thousand.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rules for disputes over the purchase price between the pre-emptor and the buyer. The general principle is that the buyer's word is taken with an oath, because the pre-emptor is claiming a right (to take the property for a lower price) while the buyer denies it. However, mutual oaths are not administered because the situation does not mirror the standard case where both parties deny each other's claims. The text then examines several scenarios. First, if both parties produce evidence (witnesses), the default Hanafi position (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the pre-emptor's evidence prevails, because it imposes an obligation on the buyer to deliver the property. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the buyer's evidence (showing a higher price) deserves preference because it proves an increase. The other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) have their own views. Second, if the buyer claims a certain price and his seller claims a lesser price, and the price has not been paid, the pre-emptor takes at the seller's word; if the price has been fully paid, the pre-emptor takes at the buyer's word. The payment must be confirmed by evidence or oath. The seller's involvement ends once he has received the full price, so the dispute is then between the pre-emptor and the buyer only.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-taḥāluf): Mutual oaths taken by two parties when both deny each other's claims; not applicable here because only the buyer denies.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, used to prove a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a price dispute between the pre-emptor and the buyer, what is the default rule according to the Hanafi school?
A) The pre-emptor's word is taken with his oath
B) The buyer's word is taken with his oath
C) Both take oaths
D) The seller's word is taken

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer's word is default in price disputes, but the pre-emptor's evidence prevails when both bring witnesses because it imposes an obligation on the buyer.

What Nullifies the Right of Pre-emption (Shuf'ah)
Listing the actions and events that invalidate shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three nullifiers of shuf'ah according to the Hanafi school and explain the Hanafi proof that death of the pre-emptor nullifies the right, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is a personal right of opinion (rai); it lapses if not claimed promptly, by compromise, or by death.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) is a unique right that allows a co-owner to step into the buyer's shoes. However, this right is not eternal; certain actions or events can nullify it. In this passage, the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq identifies three nullifiers: failing to make the required demands, compromising for compensation, and the death of the pre-emptor. We'll explore each nullifier, understand the reasoning behind them, and examine the Shafi'i disagreement on the death nullifier.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three nullifiers
From the first line of the Arabic passage, extract the three things that nullify shuf'ah: abandoning the immediate demand (talab al-muwathabah) or the request (talab al-taqrir), making a compromise (sulh) for a consideration, and the death of the pre-emptor (shafi').
The passage also notes that death of the buyer does not nullify.
2
Understand the demand requirement
The text explains that if the pre-emptor, upon learning of the sale and being able to act, fails to make either of the two formal demands (muwathabah and taqrir), his right is nullified by disinterest (i'rad). The author prefers this formulation over al-Hidayah's 'omitting to have witnesses' because witnesses are not a condition.
This step shows the Hanafi principle that the right depends on active pursuit.
3
Analyze the sulh nullifier
If the pre-emptor accepts compensation from the buyer to drop his right, the right is nullified. However, the compensation must be returned by the pre-emptor because the right is not a property right; accepting money is considered bribery. This differs from qisas or marriage authority, where the right is established in the property.
The key distinction is that shuf'ah is a mere right of ownership, not an actual property interest.
4
Examine the death nullifier and the Shafi'i dispute
The Hanafi school holds that death of the pre-emptor nullifies the right; it is not inherited. The proof: shuf'ah is a mere opinion (rai) and a contingent right, unlike qisas where the liable person becomes like owned property. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing that shuf'ah is inheritable like qisas. The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes the nature of the rights.
An important exception: if the pre-emptor dies after the court decree or the buyer surrenders the property, the right is finalized and passes to heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يبطل به الشفعة
ما يبطل به الشفعة
وتبطل بترك طلب المواثبة أو التقرير، وبالصلح من الشفعة على عوض، وعليه رده، وبموت الشفيع، لا المشتري، تبطل بموت الشفعة
باب ما يبطل به الشفعة لما كان بطلان الشيء يقتضي سابقة وجوده ذكر ما تبطل به الشفعة بعد ذكر ما تثبت به الشفعة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۷/۸] وتبطل إلخ أي تبطل الشفعة بترك أحد الطلبين أو كليهما حين علم مع القدرة عليه بأن لم يمنعه أحد، ولم يكن في الصلاة؛ لأنها تبطل بالإعراض وترك أحدهما أو كليهما مع القدرة إعراض على ما تقدم، والأصل في هذا الباب أن تسليم الشفعة قبل البيع لا يصح، وأن من ثبت له الحق إذا أسقطه بعد ثبوته له سقط علم بثبوته له أو لم يعلم، وتعبير المؤلف بترك الطلب أولى من تعبير صاحب "الهداية" بترك الإشهاد؛ لأنه يرد على صاحب "الهداية" أن الإشهاد ليس بشرط، وترك ما ليس شرطا في شيء لا يبطله، وفي "المحيط " : لو سلم الشفعة للوكيل صح وسقطت ويصح تعليق الإسقاط بشرط، ولو قال: سلمت لك إن اشتريت لنفسك لم تبطل إذا كان اشتراها لغيره تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۷/۸] أو التقرير بأن لم يشهد على أحد المتبايعين ولا عند العقار (طائي) وبالصلح إلخ : يعني تبطل الشفعة إذا صالح المشتري الشفيع على عوض؛ لأنه أخذ العوض عن حق ليس بمال فسقط حقه وعليه رده يعني على الشفيع رد العوض؛ لأن حق الشفيع ليس بتقرر في المحل، وإنما هو مجرد حق التملك، فلا يجوز أخذ العوض، بخلاف الاعتياض عن القصاص وملك النكاح وإسقاط الرق؛ لأن ملكه في هذه الأشياء متقرر في المحل. (عيني، فتح) وعليه رده أي على الشفيع رد العوض لأنه رشوة. (عيني) وبموت الشفيع إلخ : يعني بموت الشفيع قبل الأخذ بعد الطلب أو قبله تبطل الشفعة، ولا تورث عنه، ولا تبطل بموت المشتري، وقال الإمام الشافعي: لا تبطل بموت الشفيع أيضا ؛ لأنه حق معتبر كالقصاص، وحق الرد بالعيب. ولنا أنه مجرد حق، وهو حق التملك، وأنه مجرد رأي وهو الصفقة، فلا يورث عنه، بخلاف القصاص؛ لأن من عليه القصاص صار كالمملوك لمن له القصاص، ولهذا جاز له أخذ العوض عنه، وملك العين يبقى بعد الموت، فأمكن إرثه، بخلاف الشفعة؛ لأنها مجرد رأي، ولهذا لا يجوز الاعتياض عنها، ولأن ملك الشفيع فيما يأخذ يشترط أن يكون باقيا من وقت البيع إلى وقت الأخذ بالشفعة، ولم يوجد في حق الميت وقت الأخذ، ولا في حق الوارث وقت البيع، فبطلت؛ لأنها لا تستحق بالملك الحادث بعد البيع، ولا بالزائل بعد الأخذ، وإذا مات الشفيع بعد قضاء القاضي له بالشفعة، أو سلم المشتري الدار له، فهي لورثته يأخذونها. وإنما لا تبطل بموت المشتري؛ لأن المستحق باق، ولم يتغير سبب حقه، وإنما انتقل إلى الورثة كما إذا انتقل إلى غيره - به الشفعة
Chapter on what nullifies pre-emption. What nullifies pre-emption: It is nullified by abandoning the demand of al-muwathabah (immediate demand) or al-taqrir (request), by compromise on pre-emption for a consideration, and he must return it; and by the death of the pre-emptor, not the buyer. Pre-emption is nullified by death of the pre-emptor. Chapter on what nullifies pre-emption: since the nullification of something requires its prior existence, the author mentions what nullifies pre-emption after mentioning what establishes it. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/277) 'And it is nullified...' i.e., pre-emption is nullified by omitting either or both of the two demands when knowledge and ability are present, as long as he is not prevented by anyone and is not in prayer; because pre-emption is nullified by disinterest, and omitting one or both demands when able is disinterest as previously explained. The principle in this chapter is that surrendering pre-emption before the sale is not valid, and that one who has a right, if he drops it after its establishment, it falls whether he knew of its establishment or not. The author's expression 'by omitting the demand' is more appropriate than the author of al-Hidayah's expression 'by omitting to have witnesses', because it can be objected to al-Hidayah that having witnesses is not a condition, and omitting something that is not a condition does not nullify it. In al-Muhit: if he surrenders pre-emption to the agent, it is valid and falls. Suspending the dropping on a condition is valid: if he says 'I surrender to you if you buy for yourself' it does not nullify if he bought for someone else. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/277) 'Or al-taqrir': by not having witness at either of the two contracting parties nor at the property. (Tai) 'And by compromise...' i.e., pre-emption is nullified if the buyer makes a compromise with the pre-emptor for a consideration; because he took consideration for a right that is not property, so his right falls. And he must return it: i.e., the pre-emptor must return the consideration, because the pre-emptor's right is not established in the property, it is merely a right of ownership; so taking consideration is not permissible, unlike taking compensation for qisas, marriage authority, and freeing of slaves, because ownership in these things is established in the property. ('Ayni, Fath) 'And he must return it': i.e., the pre-emptor must return the consideration because it is bribery. ('Ayni) 'And by death of the pre-emptor...' i.e., pre-emption is nullified by the death of the pre-emptor whether before taking after demand or before, and it is not inherited. It is not nullified by the death of the buyer. Imam al-Shafi'i said: it is not nullified by the death of the pre-emptor either, because it is a recognized right like qisas and the right of return due to defect. And for us (Hanafis): it is a mere right, a right of ownership, and it is mere opinion (rai) and the transaction; so it is not inherited, unlike qisas because the one who owes qisas becomes as if owned by the one who has the right of qisas, hence compensation for it is permissible, and ownership of the property (ayn) remains after death, so inheritance is possible. Unlike pre-emption, because it is mere opinion, hence compensation for it is not permissible, and because the pre-emptor's ownership of what he takes must exist from the time of sale to the time of taking; it did not exist for the deceased at the time of taking, nor for the heir at the time of sale, so it is nullified. If the pre-emptor dies after the judge's decree of pre-emption or after the buyer surrenders the house to him, then it is for his heirs; they take it. And it is not nullified by the death of the buyer because the entitled person remains, and the cause of his right has not changed; rather, it transfers to the heirs just as if it had transferred to someone else. Pre-emption is with him.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This is the chapter on what nullifies the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The author begins by stating three nullifiers: (1) abandoning the immediate demand (talab al-muwathabah) or the request (talab al-taqrir); (2) making a compromise (sulh) with the buyer for a consideration; and (3) the death of the pre-emptor (but not the death of the buyer). The commentary then expands each point. Regarding abandoning demand, the author notes that if the pre-emptor knows of the sale and has the ability to claim his right without being prevented or being in prayer, and he fails to make either of the two demands, his right is lost due to disinterest (i'rad). The author prefers this formulation over al-Hidayah's 'omitting to have witnesses' because having witnesses is not a condition for the validity of the right, and omitting a non-condition does not nullify the right. From al-Muhit, we learn that surrendering pre-emption to an agent is valid, and it can be made conditional (e.g., 'I surrender if you buy for yourself' only works if he indeed buys for himself). On compromise, if the pre-emptor accepts money or another consideration from the buyer to drop his right, the right is nullified, but he must return the consideration because he took it for a right that is not property (the right of pre-emption is not an established property right). This differs from qisas or marriage authority, where the right is already established in the property. Taking compensation for shuf'ah is considered bribery. Finally, death of the pre-emptor nullifies the right entirely; it does not pass to his heirs. The Hanafi proof is that shuf'ah is a mere right of ownership based on opinion (rai), not a fixed property interest, so it cannot be inherited. The Shafi'i school disagrees, equating shuf'ah with qisas or the right of return due to defect, which are inheritable. The Hanafi response distinguishes qisas: the person liable becomes like a owned slave, so ownership is established, whereas shuf'ah is contingent on the buyer's ownership at the time of taking, which the deceased lacks. However, if the pre-emptor dies after the court has awarded the property or the buyer surrenders it, then the right is considered finalized, and his heirs can take it. Death of the buyer does not nullify the right, as the right attaches to the property, which is inherited by the buyer's heirs.

Key Terms
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathabah): Immediate demand: the first formal demand for pre-emption made right after learning of the sale, typically in the presence of witnesses.
تقرير (taqrir): Request: the second formal demand made after the immediate demand, often at the property or before witnesses, to confirm the claim.
صلح (sulh): Compromise: an agreement between the pre-emptor and the buyer to drop the pre-emption right in exchange for some compensation.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner to step into the buyer's shoes and acquire the sold share by paying its price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following nullifies the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah)?
A) Death of the buyer.
B) Death of the pre-emptor before taking the property.
C) The pre-emptor making a demand.
D) The buyer building on the property.

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'ah is nullified by three things: abandoning the two demands, compromising for consideration (which must be returned), and the death of the pre-emptor; it is a personal right of opinion, not a property right, so it does not pass to heirs unless finalized before death.

Nullifiers of Preemption Right (Shuf‘a)
Listing actions that nullify the right of preemption before court judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the actions that nullify the right of preemption and identify the persons who are disqualified from claiming it.

Core Idea
Selling the property you seek to preempt before the judge rules forfeits your right—even if you did not know.

The right of preemption (shuf‘a) grants a co-owner or neighbor the ability to take over a sold property at the sale price. However, this right can be lost through certain actions or by the status of the claimant. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq enumerates the nullifiers and the persons who cannot claim preemption. Understanding these is crucial for anyone dealing with real estate transactions in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core nullifier
The passage begins by stating that selling the property subject to preemption before the judge's ruling nullifies the right. This is the primary nullifier and applies regardless of the preemptor's knowledge.
Analogous to explicit relinquishment (taslīm ṣarīḥ).
2
Recognize acts of aversion (i‘rāḍ)
Actions such as buying the property from the buyer, renting it, or negotiating for it indicate disinterest and nullify preemption. Similarly, making it a mosque or cemetery before judgment is tantamount to abandonment.
Even releasing the buyer from debt (ibrā’) nullifies, as it is a form of relinquishment.
3
Identify persons disqualified from claiming
No preemption for: the seller (including the agent seller), the one on whose behalf the sale was made (principal), and the guarantor of recourse (ḍāmin al-darak). Their involvement in the sale precludes them from revoking it.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: The three (Abū Yūsuf, Muḥammad, Zufar) hold that agency does not disqualify.
4
Identify persons who retain the right
The buyer or one for whom the property was bought (e.g., an agent buyer, a mudārib buying for the capital owner) retains preemption. Taking by preemption does not contradict the purchase; it affirms it.
For a permitted slave (ʿabd maʾdhūn), the master has preemption only if the slave owes a debt.

The Book of Preemption
What nullifies preemption: It is also nullified by selling the property subject to preemption before judgment for preemption. There is no preemption for one who sold, or for whom it was sold, or who guaranteed the recourse. For one who bought, or for whom it was bought, there is preemption, i.e., entitlement.
[Commentary:] The guarantee: because the cause ceases due to another cause, so he revokes it and takes it just as he revokes his other dispositions, even the mosque and cemetery. The house is not sold for the buyer's debt because the preemptor's right takes precedence over the buyer's right. If the judge or his executor sells it for the deceased's debt, the preemptor may revoke it just as if the buyer sold it during his lifetime. It cannot be said: The judge's sale is a judgment, so how can it be revoked? Because that judgment would be contrary to consensus. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq 8:278) (Fath) "By selling what one preempts with, etc." means preemption is nullified by selling the house that one seeks to preempt before judgment, i.e., before the judge's ruling for preemption. There is no difference between whether he knew at the time of selling his house about the purchase of the preempted house or not; because it does not differ in either case, so it becomes like explicit relinquishment. Just as it does not differ between whether he knew of its sale or not, similarly, the release of the debtor [by the preemptor] [nullifies preemption] because all that is a relinquishment, so it does not depend on knowledge, like divorce and manumission. (Rami al-Haqaiq 2:343) If he sold the property subject to preemption with a stipulation of option, his preemption is not nullified. If the preemptor bought it from the buyer, his preemption is nullified because by undertaking the purchase he indicated aversion. Similarly, if the preemptor rented it or negotiated for it, it is nullified because it is evidence of aversion. If the preemptor made the property subject to preemption a mosque, cemetery, or endowment before judgment for him in preemption, his preemption is nullified. (Mulattaq) "There is no preemption for one who sold, etc." meaning there is no preemption for one who sold by agency, i.e., if the preemptor was the agent of the seller of the preempted house; because he is authorized to complete the sale, and in taking by preemption, he revokes what he was authorized to complete. According to the three [Imams], preemption is established for him. Similarly, no preemption for one for whom, the pronoun "him" refers to "one"; meaning the property was sold for his sake, such as if the seller was a mudarib and the preemptor was the owner of capital, so the owner of capital has no preemption in it because the sale is for him. Similarly, if there was a slave permitted to trade, and he sold the house, the master has no preemption in it.
[Commentary continues:] Preemption because of what we mentioned. (Rami al-Haqaiq 2:343) From al-Takmila and al-Fath) "Sale for him, then he has preemption." Or "guaranteed the recourse, etc.": if the preemptor guaranteed the recourse for the seller, i.e., what becomes binding on the seller after eviction, then he has no preemption because the completion of the sale came from his side, so he cannot revoke what was completed from his side. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Raiq 8:379) Because by guaranteeing the recourse, he guaranteed to him that he would obtain the house, and that only occurs by him abandoning preemption, and in taking it he would invalidate that. According to the three [Imams]: it is established. (Ayni) "One who bought, etc.": one who bought or for whom it was bought has preemption. If he bought by way of agency and he is a preemptor, he has preemption because there is no invalidation of his purchase in it; rather, there is confirmation from his side because taking by preemption is like taking by purchase. If it was bought for him, similarly, like the mudarib who buys real estate from partnership capital, and the owner of capital is its preemptor, the owner of capital has preemption. Similarly, a permitted slave if he buys, the master has preemption if the slave has debt. If he has no debt, there is no benefit in taking because it is his property and no right of others is attached to it. [Rami al-Haqaiq 2:344]
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-based exposition on what nullifies the right of preemption (shuf‘a) in Hanafi law. It begins by stating the core rule: preemption is nullified by selling the property that one seeks to preempt before the judge rules in favor of preemption. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq and Fath explains that this applies regardless of the preemptor's knowledge, as it is analogous to explicit relinquishment. Similarly, the preemptor's release of the buyer from debt also nullifies the right, being a form of relinquishment. The passage then introduces exceptions: selling with a stipulation of option does not nullify; buying the property from the buyer does nullify, as does renting or negotiating for it, as these indicate disinterest. Converting the property into a mosque or cemetery before judgment also nullifies preemption. Next, the passage discusses persons who cannot claim preemption: the seller or the one on whose behalf the sale was made (e.g., the principal or the mudarib’s capital owner) cannot preempt because the sale is attributed to them. A guarantor of the recourse (damān al-darak) also loses preemption since the guarantee implies a commitment to the buyer's uninterrupted possession. Conversely, one who buys or for whom the property is bought (e.g., through agency or by a mudarib) retains the right of preemption, as taking by preemption does not contradict the purchase but rather confirms the process. The commentary concludes with the case of a permitted slave: the master can preempt only if the slave owes a debt, because only then does the master's right of preemption benefit him by securing payment.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf‘ah): The right of preemption, i.e., the right to take over property sold to another at the sale price.
الدرك (al-darak): The recourse or indemnity that becomes due to the buyer if the property is evicted by a prior rightful owner.
الإعراض (al-i‘rāḍ): Aversion or disinterest in claiming preemption, evidenced by actions like renting or negotiating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions does NOT nullify the right of preemption?
A) Selling the property subject to preemption before court judgment.
B) Making the property a mosque before judgment.
C) Selling the property with a condition of option (khiyar).
D) Guaranteeing the recourse from the seller's side.

Key Takeaway
Preemption is lost by any act indicating abandonment or by the claimant's own involvement in the sale, but not by conditional sales or by being the buyer.

Effect of Misinformation on Preemption Surrender
Ruling on when misinformation revives preemption right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a preemptor's surrender of his right remains valid if he was misinformed about the price or buyer, applying Hanafi principles of istikthār and specific surrender.

Core Idea
Surrender based on false information binds only if the truth would have made surrender even more likely; otherwise, the preemption right revives.

The preemptor's right to take a sold property is only validly surrendered when he knows the true facts that would motivate his decision. When he surrenders based on a mistaken high price, but the actual price is lower, the surrender is not binding because he surrendered to avoid a high cost. Similarly, surrender based on the type of price or the identity of the buyer is specific to that circumstance. This lesson explores the detailed Hanafi rulings on various scenarios of misinformation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Type of Misinformation
Classify the misinformation as relating to the amount of price, the type of price (currency, goods, etc.), the identity of the buyer, or the portion of the property sold.
2
Determine the Preemptor's Motivation
Consider what motivated the surrender: to avoid a high price (istikthār), ease of payment, personal relations with the buyer, or other factors. The ruling depends on whether the known truth would have changed that motivation.
For example, surrendering due to high price does not apply if the price is lower.
3
Apply the Principle of Specific Surrender
Apply the rule that surrender in one respect does not imply surrender in another. If the preemptor surrendered based on a particular fact, and that fact is false, the surrender is limited to that circumstance.
Surrender for one type of price (e.g., gold) does not cover another (e.g., wheat) unless the types are equivalent in thamaniyyah.
4
Distinguish Valid and Invalid Surrenders
Based on the above, determine whether the surrender remains valid or the preemption right revives. Valid when the truth is even more favorable (higher price, same or more valuable currency). Invalid when the truth is less favorable (lower price, different type, different buyer).
Exception: dinars and dirhams are considered one kind in thamaniyyah per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Recognize the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (istihsan: no preemption if dinars vs dirhams) and Zufar (qiyas: preemption remains). Understand the basis: one kind vs two kinds in thamaniyyah.
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قيل للشفيع: إنها بيعت بألف، فسلم، ثم علم
الدار
الشفعة لأجل الاستكثار
ما يبطل به الشفعة
أنها بيعت بأقل أو ببر أو شعير
من الألف علم أن الدار بيعت
قيمته ألف أو أكثر، فله الشفعة ولو بان أنها بيعت بدنانير قيمتها ألف، فلا شفعة،
درهم من الألف
وإن قيل له: إنه اشترى فلان فسلّم، فبان أنه غيره، فله الشفعة،
للشفيع
الشفعة
ظهر المشتري أي للشفيع
فله الشفعة : [ لأن التسليم لم يصادف على الوجه المستحق؛ لأنه كان للاستكثار أو لتعذر الجنس. (عيني)] أي بقيت للشفيع الشفعة في كل هذه الوجوه والأصل فيه: أن الغرض في الشفعة يختلف باختلاف قدر الثمن وجنسه والمشتري، فإذا سلم على بعض الوجوه، ثم تبين خلافه بقيت الشفعة بحالها؛ لأن التسليم لم يوجد على الوجه الذي استحقه، بيانه: أنه إذا أخبر أن الدار بيعت بألف درهم، فسلم الشفعة، ثم علم أنها بيعت بأكثر، فالتسليم صحيح؛ لأنه سلم لاستكثار الثمن، فإذا كان أكثر من ذلك كان أرضى بالتسليم، وإن علم أنها بيعت بأقل أو بحنطة أو شعير قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، فهو على شفعته؛ لأن تسليمه عند كثرة الثمن لا يدل على تسليمه عند القلة.
وكذا تسليمه في أحد الجنسين لا يكون تسليما في الآخر، فربما يسهل عليه أداء أحدهما، ويتعذر الآخر، وكذا كل موزون أو مكيل أو عددي متقارب، بخلاف ما إذا علم أنها بيعت بعروض قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، فإنه تسليم؛ لأنه إنما يأخذ بقيمته دراهم أو دنانير، ولو بان أنها بيعت بدنانير قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، صح التسليم، وإن كان أقل فهو على شفعته، وكذا لو أخبر أن الثمن عروض، ثم ظهر أنه مكيل أو موزون فهو على شفعته.(فتح الله المعين) ولو بان إلخ: أي ولو ظهر أن الدار بيعت بدنانير قيمتها ألف لا شفعة؛ لأنهما جنس واحد في الثمينة، وقال زفر والثلاثة هو على شفعته؛ لأنهما جنسان حقيقة رمز الحقائق : ٣٤٤/٢] قال في "الفتح": وهو أي بطلان الشفعة قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وهو استحسان والقياس أن تثبت له الشفعة.
فلا شفعة إلخ الأنهما جنس واحد في الثمنية أي لو أخبر الشفيع أن الدار المشفوعة اشتراها فلان، فسلماها له إما إكراما له أو خوفا من شره، ثم ظهر أن المشتري ،غيره فله الشفعة؛ لتفاوت الناس في الأخلاق، فمنهم من يرغب في معاشرته، ومنهم من يجتنب مخافة شره، فالتسليم في حق البعض لا يكون تسليما في غيره، ولو علم أن غيره كان له أن يأخذ نصيب غيره ؛ لأن التسليم لم يوجد في حقه، ولو بلغه شراء النصف فسلم، ثم بلغه شراء الكل، فله الشفعة في الكل؛ لأنه سلم النصف، وكان حقه في أخذ الكل، والكل غير النصف، فلا يكون إسقاطه إسقاطا للكل.
المشتري هو مع
وفي عكسه لا شفعة في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأن التسليم في الكل تسليم في أبعاضه كلها؛ ولأن رغبات الناس في الكل أكثر عادة من رغباتهم في الأشقاص؛ لخلو الكل عن عيب التشقيص، فإذا لم يرغب فيه، فأولى أن لا يرغب في التشقيص، وقيل: له الشفعة؛ لأنه قد لا يتمكن من تحصيل ثمن الجميع، وقد تكون حاجته إلى النصف؛ ليتم به مرافق ملكه، فلا يحتاج إلى الجميع، وشيخ الإسلام مال إلى هذا القول، وحمل ما ذكره في ظاهر الرواية –
If the preemptor is told: 'It was sold for a thousand,' and he surrendered, then he learns that the house was sold for less, or for wheat or barley worth a thousand or more, then he has preemption. Even if it becomes clear that it was sold for dinars worth a thousand, then no preemption; [if it was] dirhams from the thousand? He knows that the house was sold for something worth a thousand or more, so he has preemption. And if it becomes clear that it was sold for dinars worth a thousand, then no preemption. If he is told: 'So-and-so bought it,' and he surrendered, then it becomes clear that it was someone else, then he has preemption.
The preemptor's preemption appears, i.e., for the preemptor he has preemption because the surrender did not occur in the manner that was due; because it was due to want of more or because of impossibility of the medium? (Ayni) i.e., the preemptor retains preemption in all these cases. The principle: the purpose in preemption differs according to the amount of the price, its kind, and the buyer. So if he surrenders in one respect, then the contrary becomes clear, preemption remains as it was because the surrender did not occur in the manner he was entitled to. Explanation: if he is told that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams, so he surrenders preemption, then he learns that it was sold for more, the surrender is valid because he surrendered due to the high amount of the price, and if it was more than that, he would be more pleased with the surrender. If he learns that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley worth a thousand or more, then he remains on his preemption because his surrender at the time of high price does not indicate surrender at the time of low. Similarly, his surrender in one type is not surrender in the other, because perhaps it is easy for him to pay one and difficult to pay the other. Similarly, every weighed, measured, or closely countable item. Unlike if he knows that it was sold for goods worth a thousand or more, then it is surrender because he takes it at its value in dirhams or dinars. If it becomes clear that it was sold for dinars worth a thousand or more, the surrender is valid; if less, then he remains on his preemption. Similarly, if he is told that the price is goods, then it appears to be measured or weighed, then he remains on his preemption. (Fath Allah al-Mu‘in) 'And if it becomes clear, etc.' i.e., if it becomes clear that the house was sold for dinars worth a thousand, no preemption because they are one kind in terms of being valuable. Zufar and the three [Imams] say he is on his preemption because they are two types in reality. (Rami al-Haqaiq 2:344) He said in al-Fath: this, i.e., nullification of preemption, is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and it is istihsan; the analogy would be that preemption is established for him.
'So no preemption, etc.' because they are one kind in terms of being valuable. That is, if the preemptor is told that the preempted house was bought by so-and-so, so he surrendered it to him either as a favor to him or out of fear of his evil, then it becomes clear that the buyer was someone else, then he has preemption because people differ in character; some desire his company, some avoid him for fear of his evil. So surrender with respect to one is not surrender with respect to another. If he knew that it was someone else, he could take his share because surrender did not occur with respect to him. If news of the purchase of half came to him and he surrendered, then news of the purchase of the whole came, he has preemption in the whole because he surrendered the half, and his right was to take the whole, and the whole is not the half, so his abandonment of the half is not abandonment of the whole.
The buyer is with him, and in the opposite case, no preemption in the apparent narration because surrender of the whole is surrender of all its parts, and because people's desires for the whole are usually greater than their desires for parts, since the whole is free from the defect of partitioning. So if he does not desire it, then it is more appropriate that he does not desire parts. It is said: he has preemption because he may not be able to obtain the price of all, and his need may be for the half to complete the conveniences of his property, so he does not need the whole. Shaykh al-Islam inclined to this view and interpreted what was mentioned in the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: the preemptor is told inaccurate information about the price or identity of the buyer, then surrenders his right, and later learns the truth. The key principle is that surrender is valid only if it aligns with the actual circumstances that would have motivated the preemptor. If the preemptor surrenders thinking the price is high, and it turns out lower, the surrender is not binding because he surrendered to avoid a high price, not a low one. Similarly, if the type of price differs (e.g., gold vs. silver, or goods vs. currency), surrender under one type does not imply surrender under another, as ease of payment varies. If the price is in goods, surrender based on their value in dirhams is considered final. Regarding the buyer: surrendering because the buyer is a specific person (e.g., feared or respected) does not apply if the buyer turns out to be someone else. The passage also discusses partial vs. complete purchase: if told half was bought, surrenders, then learns all was bought, preemption remains for the whole because the right was for all. The opposite (surrendering the whole then learning only half was bought) yields no preemption in the apparent narration, but some scholars (including Shaykh al-Islam) allow it due to practical need. The text also notes the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (istihsan: no preemption if dinars vs. dirhams) and Zufar and the three Imams (qiyas: preemption remains).

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslīm): Surrender or relinquishment of the preemption right, often by verbal statement or action indicating abandonment.
الاستكثار (al-istikthār): The desire to avoid paying a high price, which may motivate surrender.
الثمنية (al-thamaniyyah): The quality of being a standard measure of value (like gold and silver).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A preemptor is told the house was sold for 1000 dirhams, so he surrenders. Later he learns it was actually sold for 800 dirhams. What is the ruling?
A) He has no preemption because he already surrendered.
B) He retains preemption because he surrendered to avoid a high price, not a lower one.
C) He retains preemption only if the difference is more than one-third of the value.
D) He has no preemption because the surrender was valid due to istikthār.

Key Takeaway
A preemptor's surrender is only binding when the true circumstances match the motivation for surrender; otherwise, the right to preemption revives.

Effect of Price Misrepresentation on Shuf'ah
Ruling: when shafi' relinquishes based on price, later found different

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on shuf'ah when the price type or amount is misrepresented, and distinguish it from the opposing view of Zufr and the three imams.

Core Idea
The validity of relinquishing shuf'ah depends on what the shafi' was told; actual higher price confirms it, but different genus restores the right.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when a shafi' who has been given incorrect information about the sale price can still reclaim his right of pre-emption. The key lies in understanding that the shafi''s intention is tied to both the amount and the genus of the price. The Hanafi approach uses istihsan to treat dirhams and dinars as equivalent for this purpose, differing from Zufr and the three imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
Read the passage to see the basic case: the shafi' is told a price, he relinquishes, then the actual price turns out different. Note which variations are discussed.
The passage covers higher price, lower price, different commodity (wheat/barley), different currency (dinars), and goods (urud).
2
Distinguish between higher price and lower/other genus
If the actual price is higher, the relinquishment stands because the shafi' was seeking to avoid a high price; a higher price is even more reason to give up. If lower or different genus, he retains shuf'ah because his consent was specific.
The principle: 'al-taslim lam yujad 'ala al-wajh al-mustahaqq' – the relinquishment did not occur on the proper basis.
3
Understand the dispute over dinars vs dirhams
When informed of dirhams but it was dinars, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold no shuf'ah because both are thaman (price); Zufr says shuf'ah remains because they are different genera.
The Hanafi position is istihsan; qiyas would give shuf'ah, but istihsan overrides because the shafi''s objective is the value, not the specific coin.
4
Analyze the role of istihsan
The text explicitly states that this view is istihsan. Recognize that the Hanafi school prioritizes the underlying intent (the shafi' wants the price, not a particular type) over strict analogy.
This contrasts with the opposing view which treats dinars and dirhams as distinct realities.
5
Apply to other cases (goods vs measured/weighed)
Notice the extension: if goods (urud) were the price, relinquishment is valid because the shafi' will take the value in dirhams/dinars. But if it was a measured/weighed commodity, his relinquishment for one does not apply to the other.
This shows consistent application of the 'jins' concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قيل للشفيع: إنها بيعت بألف، فسلم، ثم علم أن الدار الشفعة لأجل الاستكثار ما يبطل به الشفعة أنها بيعت بأقل أو ببر أو شعير من الألف علم أن الدار بيعت قيمته ألف أو أكثر، فله الشفعة ولو بان أنها بيعت بدنانير قيمتها ألف، فلا شفعة، درهم من الألف [؟]  [commentary] لأن التسليم لم يصادف على الوجه المستحق؛ لأنه كان للاستكثار أو لتعذر الجنس. (عيني) أي بقيت للشفيع الشفعة في كل هذه الوجوه والأصل فيه: أن الغرض في الشفعة يختلف باختلاف قدر الثمن وجنسه والمشتري، فإذا سلم على بعض الوجوه، ثم تبين خلافه بقيت الشفعة بحالها؛ لأن التسليم لم يوجد على الوجه الذي استحقه، بيانه: أنه إذا أخبر أن الدار بيعت بألف درهم، فسلم الشفعة، ثم علم أنها بيعت بأكثر، فالتسليم صحيح؛ لأنه سلم لاستكثار الثمن، فإذا كان أكثر من ذلك كان أرضى بالتسليم، وإن علم أنها بيعت بأقل أو بحنطة أو شعير قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، فهو على شفعته؛ لأن تسليمه عند كثرة الثمن لا يدل على تسليمه عند القلة. وكذا تسليمه في أحد الجنسين لا يكون تسليما في الآخر، فربما يسهل عليه أداء أحدهما، ويتعذر الآخر، وكذا كل موزون أو مكيل أو عددي متقارب، بخلاف ما إذا علم أنها بيعت بعروض قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، فإنه تسليم؛ لأنه إنما يأخذ بقيمته دراهم أو دنانير، ولو بان أنها بيعت بدنانير قيمتها ألف أو أكثر، صح التسليم، وإن كان أقل فهو على شفعته، وكذا لو أخبر أن الثمن عروض، ثم ظهر أنه مكيل أو موزون فهو على شفعته.(فتح الله المعين) ولو بان إلخ: أي ولو ظهر أن الدار بيعت بدنانير قيمتها ألف لا شفعة؛ لأنهما جنس واحد في الثمينة، وقال زفر والثلاثة هو على شفعته؛ لأنهما جنسان حقيقة رمز الحقائق : ٣٤٤/٢] قال في "الفتح": وهو أي بطلان الشفعة قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وهو استحسان والقياس أن تثبت له الشفعة.
If the shafi' is told it was sold for a thousand, so he relinquishes, then he knows that the house [was sold for more]... it is a cause for seeking more... what nullifies shuf'ah: that it was sold for less or for wheat or barley from the thousand, he knows the house was sold for its value of a thousand or more, so he has shuf'ah. Even if it appears it was sold for dinars worth a thousand, no shuf'ah... because the relinquishment did not occur in the manner entitled; because it was for seeking excess or due to difficulty of the genus. The principle: the objective in shuf'ah differs by amount, genus, and buyer. If he relinquishes in some aspects then the contrary appears, shuf'ah remains because the relinquishment did not occur in the manner entitled. Explanation: if told sold for a thousand silver, he relinquishes, then learns sold for more, the relinquishment is valid because he relinquished due to high price, so if it was more, he is even more satisfied. If sold for less or wheat/barley worth a thousand or more, he retains shuf'ah because his relinquishment at high price does not indicate at low. Similarly in one genus not the other. But if sold for goods worth a thousand or more, it is a relinquishment because he takes its value. If it appears sold for dinars worth a thousand or more, relinquishment valid; if less, he retains shuf'ah. Zufr and the three said he retains shuf'ah because they are two genera. In al-Fath: the nullification of shuf'ah is the opinion of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it is istihsan, and qiyas would give him shuf'ah.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting rulings on the effect of price misrepresentation on the right of shuf'ah. The author explains that if the shafi' is informed of a specific price and relinquishes his right, but later discovers the actual price or medium of exchange is different, the general principle is that the relinquishment is specific to the known price and genus. If the price was actually higher, the shafi' is deemed even more satisfied, so the relinquishment stands. If it was lower or of a different genus (e.g., wheat instead of silver), he retains his right because his relinquishment at a high price does not imply consent at a low price. The same applies if the medium is a different currency (dinars vs dirhams) or type of commodity (measured vs weighed). However, if both are monetary (dinars and dirhams), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that they are the same genus (thaman) so relinquishment is valid, while Zufr and the three other imams consider them different genera. The Hanafi position is based on istihsan, preferring the view that since both serve as price, they are effectively the same. The commentary elaborates that the shafi' retains shuf'ah in all these cases because the initial relinquishment was not properly founded. This is a clear example of the Hanafi method of reasoning: the underlying rationale ('illa) of the ruling is the shafi''s objective, which varies with price and genus.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Right of a neighbour or partner to purchase property being sold, by pre-emption.
الاستكثار (al-istikthar): Seeking a greater amount; the shafi' relinquishes due to high price, but if it turns out higher, he is even more satisfied.
الجنس (al-jins): The category or genus of price, e.g., dirhams vs dinars vs goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A shafi' is told the property was sold for 1000 dirhams. He relinquishes. Later he learns it was actually sold for 800 dirhams. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The relinquishment is valid because he consented to a high price.
B) He retains his right of shuf'ah because the price was lower.
C) He has the option to either take or leave.
D) The relinquishment is void because of misrepresentation.

Key Takeaway
The validity of relinquishing shuf'ah is specific to the reported price amount and genus; a higher price confirms consent, but a lower price or different genus revives the right, with a Hanafi exception for dinars and dirhams considered one genus.

Pre-emption When Buyer Pays with a Garment: Pre-emptor Pays Cash
Ruling on pre-emption when buyer substitutes garment for cash

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a pre-emptor claims a property purchased with a garment instead of cash, and explain the legal stratagem to shield the seller from loss if the property is reclaimed.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor must pay the original cash price, not the substitute garment.

This lesson examines a nuanced case from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding shuf'ah (pre-emption). The buyer paid for a property with a garment instead of cash. The pre-emptor's right is based on the original sale price, not the substitute. The text also discusses a legal stratagem to protect the seller if the property is later reclaimed (istihqaq). Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to apply pre-emption rules in transactions involving non-cash consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction
Recognize that the buyer purchased the property for an agreed cash price (thaman) but paid with a garment. The garment is a substitute for the cash debt.
2
Determine the Pre-emptor's Right
The pre-emptor (shafi') takes the property by paying the cash price, not the garment. The pre-emption attaches to the original sale, not the substitute.
The garment payment is considered a separate contract of sale between seller and buyer.
3
Analyze the Potential Harm to Seller
If the property is reclaimed by a rightful owner (istihqaq), the seller might be left with a debt because the cash price was not actually received (the garment was a substitute). The set-off (muqasah) between the price and the garment becomes void.
4
Apply the Stratagem (Hilah)
To protect the seller, the text recommends that instead of a garment, the buyer pay in a different currency (e.g., dinars instead of dirhams). This creates a currency exchange (sarf) contract; if the property is reclaimed, the sarf is void due to separation before possession, and only the dinars are returned, not the full cash price.
The stratagem is not allowed for the neighbor and partner (الجار والشريك).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دفع ثوبا عنه، فالشفعة بالثمن لا الثوب. البائع أي عوضا عن الثمن تجب للشفيع فالشفعة بالثمن إلخ : أي إن اشترى الدار بثمن ثم دفع إلى البائع ثوبا بدل الثمن تجب الشفعة للشفيع بالثمن لا بالثوب؛ لأن الثوب عوض عما في ذمة المشتري، فيكون البائع مشتريا للثوب بعقد آخر غير العقد الأول، وهذه الحيلة نمنع الجار والشريك؛ لأنه يبتاع العقار بأضعاف قيمته ويعطيه بها ثوبا قدر قيمة العقار غير أنه يخاف أن يتضرر البائع بذلك؛ لأنه لو استحقت الدار، تبقى الدراهم كلها في ذمة البائع؛ لوجوبه عليه بالبيع وبراءته حصلت بطريق المقاصة بثمن العقار، فإذا استحق تبين أنه ليس عليه ثمن العقار، فبطلت المقاصة، فيجب على البائع الثمن كله. والحيلة في ذلك أن يدفع إليه بدل الدراهم الثمن الدنانير بقدر قيمة العقار، فيكون صرفا بما في ذمته من الدراهم، ثم إذا استحق العقار تبين أن لا دين على المشتري، فيبطل الصرف للافتراق قبل القبض، فيجب رد الدنانير لا غير.
Then he gave a garment in payment for it, and the pre-emption is with the price, not the garment. The seller (i.e., as a substitute for the price, the pre-emptor is entitled)—the pre-emption is with the price etc.: meaning if he bought the house for a price then gave the seller a garment in place of the price, the pre-emption is owed to the pre-emptor at the price, not at the garment, because the garment is a substitute for what is in the buyer's liability, so the seller becomes a buyer of the garment in a separate contract from the first contract. This stratagem we prevent the neighbor and the partner, because he buys the property at many times its value and gives him a garment worth the value of the property, but he fears the seller may be harmed by that because if the house is reclaimed, all the dirhams remain in the seller's liability due to their obligation on him by the sale, and his release occurred by way of set-off with the price of the property; if it is reclaimed it becomes clear that he is not owed the price of the property, so the set-off is void, and the seller must pay the full price. The stratagem for that is that he gives him instead of the dirhams the price in dinars equal to the value of the property, so it becomes a currency exchange for what is in his liability of dirhams; then if the property is reclaimed it becomes clear that there is no debt on the buyer, so the exchange is void due to separation before possession, and he must return only the dinars.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The matn states a specific case: if the buyer pays for a property with a garment (thawb) instead of cash, the pre-emptor (shafi') takes the property by paying the cash price (thaman), not the garment. The commentary explains that the garment payment is a separate transaction; the seller becomes a buyer of the garment. The pre-emptor's right is based on the original sale price. The commentary then discusses a potential harm: if the property is reclaimed (istihqaq), the seller would be left with a debt. To avoid this, a stratagem is advised: pay in dinars instead of dirhams, making it a currency exchange (sarf) that would be void if the property is reclaimed, thus protecting the seller.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property sold to a stranger by paying the price.
ثوب (thawb): A garment used as a substitute for cash payment in the transaction.
حيلة (hīlah): A legal stratagem used to achieve a lawful end without violating the letter of the law; here, to avoid harm to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer pays for a house with a garment, what does the pre-emptor pay to take the house?
A) The value of the garment
B) The cash price (thaman)
C) Half the garment value
D) Nothing, pre-emption is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the pre-emptor pays the original cash price, not any substitute the buyer used to satisfy the seller.

The Hanafi Dispute on the Permissibility of Hilah to Drop Pre-emption
Intra-Ḥanafī dispute with proofs and tarjīḥ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad regarding the karāhah of using a hīlah to drop pre-emption, explain their respective proofs, and identify the muftā bihī position.

Core Idea
Abū Yūsuf permits hīlah to drop pre-emption as self-protection; Muḥammad considers it disliked because it defeats the purpose of repelling harm.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafī dispute on whether employing a legal stratagem (hīlah) to drop the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) is disliked (makrūh) or not. The matn from Kanz al-Daqā'iq states it is not disliked, which reflects Abū Yūsuf's view. Muḥammad al-Shaybānī disagrees, arguing that since pre-emption is meant to prevent harm, any stratagem to evade it is inherently disliked. We will analyze the proofs of both imams and the practical consequence (thamarah) of their disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the exact ruling in dispute: the ruling on using a hīlah to drop shuf'ah and zakāh.
2
Identify the two positions
Abū Yūsuf: not disliked (lā tukrah). Muḥammad: disliked (tukrah).
3
Follow Muḥammad's proof
He argues that shuf'ah was legislated to repel harm (ḍarar), repelling harm is obligatory, and causing harm (by evading shuf'ah) is ḥarām, so the hīlah is necessarily makrūh.
4
Follow Abū Yūsuf's proof
He argues that the pre-emptor needs to protect himself from harm, and a hīlah for self-protection is legitimate even if it causes harm to others.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf
According to Abū Yūsuf, the hīlah is permissible (not disliked); according to Muḥammad, it is disliked but the transaction remains valid.
6
Note the muftā bihī
For shuf'ah, fatwa is given according to Abū Yūsuf; for zakāh, according to Muḥammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تكره الحيلة لإسقاط الشفعة والزكاة. ولا تكره الحيلة إلخ [ أي ما يتكلف لدفع مكروه أو جلب محبوب. (فتح)] هذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد: نكره؛ لأن الشفعة وجبت لدفع الضرر، وهو واجب، وإلحاق الضرر به حرام، فكانت مكروهة ضرورة، ولأبي يوسف: أنه يحتاج لدفع الضرر عن نفسه، والحيلة لدفع الضرر عن نفسه مشروع، وإن كان غيره يتضرر بذلك. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٨٥/٨] ثم الحيلة في هذا الباب نوعان: حيلة لإسقاطها بعد وجوب الشفعة، نحو: أن يقول المبتاع للشفيع: أنا أبيعها منك بما اشتريت فلا فائدة لك في الأخذ فيقول الشفيع نعم، أو ما يدل على الرضى، بطلت شفعته، أو يقول له: إني وهبت منك هذه الدار، فتقبل مني، فلما قال: "قبلت" تبطل شفعته، ثم يمتنع عن التسليم، فلا تتم الهبة، وإن سلم إليه، يرجع في هبته، فهذه الحيلة مكروهة بالاتفاق، والثانية: حيلة قبل الشفعة، وجوب وهي ما عدها في هذا الباب، وقيل: يفتى في الشفعة بقول أبي يوسف، وبقول محمد في الزكاة، كذا في "الأصل"، قال العيني: وهو المختار عندي. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٥/٢] (ملا مسكين، فتح)
And the legal stratagem (hīlah) to drop pre-emption and zakāh is not disliked. And the legal stratagem is not disliked etc. [i.e., what is undertaken to repel something disliked or attract something beloved (Fatḥ).] This is according to Abū Yūsuf. According to Muḥammad, it is disliked because pre-emption was established to repel harm, and repelling harm is obligatory, and causing harm is unlawful, so the stratagem is necessarily disliked. According to Abū Yūsuf: he needs to repel harm from himself, and a legal stratagem to repel harm from oneself is legitimate, even if others are harmed by it. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq 8/285) Then the legal stratagem in this chapter is of two types: a stratagem to drop it after pre-emption becomes established, such as the buyer saying to the pre-emptor, 'I will sell it to you for what I bought it, so there is no benefit for you in taking it,' and the pre-emptor says 'yes' or something indicating consent, then his pre-emption is void; or he says to him, 'I have gifted this house to you, so accept it from me,' and when he says 'I accept,' his pre-emption becomes void, then he refrains from delivery so the gift is not completed, and if he delivers it, he takes back his gift. This type of stratagem is disliked by agreement. The second type: a stratagem before pre-emption becomes established, and that is what is counted in this chapter. And it is said: fatwa is given according to Abū Yūsuf in pre-emption, and according to Muḥammad in zakāh, as in al-Aṣl. Al-ʿAynī said: That is the chosen opinion in my view. (Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq 2/345) (Mullā Miskīn, Fatḥ).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states that a legal stratagem (hīlah) to drop pre-emption and zakāh is not disliked. The commentary clarifies this is the position of Abū Yūsuf, while Muḥammad considers it disliked. Abū Yūsuf argues that the pre-emptor needs to protect himself from harm, and a stratagem for that purpose is legitimate even if it harms others. Muḥammad argues that since pre-emption is meant to prevent harm, avoiding it is obligatory, and causing harm to the pre-emptor is unlawful; thus the stratagem is necessarily disliked. The commentary then divides stratagems into two types: after pre-emption has become established (which are unanimously disliked) and before it becomes established (the subject of this chapter). The relied-upon (muftā bihi) position is Abū Yūsuf's for pre-emption and Muḥammad's for zakāh.

Key Terms
حيلة (hīlah): A legal stratagem; a means to achieve a lawful end without violating the letter of the law.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption.
مكروهة (makrūhah): Disliked; in this context, it refers to the legal stratagem being considered makrūh (either tanzīhī or taḥrīmī) according to Muḥammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Yūsuf, what is the ruling on using a legal stratagem to drop pre-emption?
A) It is disliked (makrūh)
B) It is not disliked (lā tukrah)
C) It is forbidden (ḥarām)
D) It is obligatory (wājib)

Key Takeaway
The use of a legal stratagem to drop pre-emption is not disliked according to Abū Yūsuf, which is the relied-upon position for shuf'ah.

Types of Legal Stratagems in Pre-emption and the Muftā Bihī Position
Explanation of two types of hīlah and tarjīḥ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of legal stratagems in pre-emption (after and before the right becomes established) and state the muftā bihī position for each category.

Core Idea
Stratagems after pre-emption becomes established are unanimously disliked; those before it are the subject of the dispute.

In the previous lesson, we examined the general dispute on hīlah. This lesson dives deeper into the classification of hīlah in the context of pre-emption. The commentary distinguishes two types: one employed after the pre-emption right has become established, which is disliked by all scholars, and one used before the right is established, which is the type discussed in this chapter and is the subject of the intra-Ḥanafī difference. We will also learn the muftā bihī rule: Abū Yūsuf's view is adopted for pre-emption, and Muḥammad's for zakāh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
The first type: hīlah after wujūb al-shuf'ah (e.g., buyer's trick to make the pre-emptor consent or accept a gift). This is universally disliked (makrūh bi al-ittifāq).
2
Understand the second type
The second type: hīlah before wujūb al-shuf'ah. This is the hīlah referred to in the matn's ruling of 'not disliked' according to Abū Yūsuf.
3
Identify the examples
For the first type: the buyer says he'll sell it to the pre-emptor for the same price, and the pre-emptor agrees; or the buyer gifts the property and the pre-emptor accepts, then the buyer refuses delivery or takes back the gift. These are disliked by agreement.
4
Know the muftā bihī
Although Muḥammad considers all such hīlah disliked, the fatwa for shuf'ah follows Abū Yūsuf (not disliked) for the second type. For zakāh, the fatwa follows Muḥammad (disliked).
5
Confirm with later authorities
Al-ʿAynī in Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq confirms this as the chosen view.
