6 min
Selecting Shares in Pre-emption: Multiple Buyers vs. Multiple Sellers
Ruling on pre-emptor selecting shares with multiple buyers vs sellers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selecting which buyer's share to take in pre-emption (shuf'ah) and explain the distinction between multiple buyers and multiple sellers based on the harm principle.

Core Idea
Pre-emptor can pick a buyer's share, but must take all sellers' shares — splitting transactions harms the buyer.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi pre-emption law: when the pre-emptor (shafi') faces multiple buyers, he may select one and leave the others, but when there are multiple sellers, he must take all shares together. The reasoning hinges on protecting the buyer from additional harm caused by a split transaction. Understanding this principle will help you apply the rules of shuf'ah correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine whether the multiplicity is among buyers (multiple buyers, one seller) or among sellers (multiple sellers, one buyer).
The ruling depends entirely on who is multiple.
2
State the ruling
If multiple buyers: the pre-emptor may take the share of some and leave others. If multiple sellers: the pre-emptor must take all shares together; he cannot select.
3
Understand the rationale
Selecting among sellers would split the transaction for the buyer, causing him harm through co-ownership. Pre-emption is already an exception, so it should not cause extra harm. With multiple buyers, the pre-emptor steps into one buyer's shoes, so no split occurs.
This is the ḥikmah (wisdom) behind the rule.
4
Apply the distinction before/after possession
The ruling applies uniformly whether the pre-emptor takes before or after taking possession, according to the sound opinion.
No difference based on timing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذ حظ البعض بتعدد المشتري، لا بتعدد البائع. وأخذ حظ البعض إلخ: أي أخذ الشفيع حظ البعض وقت تعدد المشتري لا تعدد البائع صورته: أن المشتري إذا تعدد بأن اشترى جماعة عقارا، والبائع واحد، يتعدد الأخذ بالشفعة بتعددهم حتى كان للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم، ويترك الباقي، وإن تعدد البائع بأن باع جماعة عقارا مشتركا بينهم والمشتري واحد لا يتعدد الأخذ بالشفعة بتعددهم، حتى لا يكون للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم دون بعض. والفرق أن في الوجه الثاني بأخذ البعض تتفرق الصفقة على المشتري، فيتضرر بعيب الشركة والشفعة شرعت على خلاف القياس لدفع الضرر عن الشفيع فلا تشرع على وجه يتضرر به المشتري ضررا زائدا على الأخذ بالشفعة، وفي الوجه الأول يقوم الشفيع مقام أحدهم، فلا تتفرق الصفقة، ولا فرق في هذا بين ما إذا كان قبل القبض أو بعده على الصحيح رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٥/٢] (تكملة)
And taking the share of some when there are multiple buyers, not when there are multiple sellers. 'And taking the share of some etc.': meaning the pre-emptor takes the share of some when there are multiple buyers, not when there are multiple sellers. Its example: if the buyer is multiple, such as a group buying a property with one seller, the taking of pre-emption becomes multiple according to their number, so the pre-emptor may take the share of some of them and leave the rest. If the seller is multiple, such as a group selling a jointly owned property with one buyer, the taking of pre-emption does not become multiple according to their number, so the pre-emptor may not take the share of some without others. The difference is that in the second case, taking some would cause the transaction to be split for the buyer, leading to harm due to co-ownership. Pre-emption is legislated contrary to analogy to remove harm from the pre-emptor, so it may not be legislated in a way that causes additional harm to the buyer during pre-emption. In the first case, the pre-emptor takes the place of one of them, so the transaction is not split. There is no difference in this between before or after possession according to the sound opinion. (Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq 2/345) (Takmilah).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary. The matn distinguishes two scenarios. Scenario 1: When there are multiple buyers (e.g., two people buy a property) and one seller, the pre-emptor may take the share of one buyer and leave the other. This is because the pre-emptor steps into the shoes of that specific buyer, so the transaction remains intact for the other buyer. Scenario 2: When there are multiple sellers (e.g., two co-owners sell their shares to one buyer), the pre-emptor must take the shares of all sellers together; he cannot select some. The reason: taking only part would split the transaction for the buyer, causing him harm through co-ownership (i.e., being a partner with the pre-emptor). Since pre-emption itself is an exception to standard rules, it should not cause extra harm to the buyer. This distinction applies whether the pre-emptor takes before or after possession.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption.
حظ (ḥaẓẓ): Share or portion of the property being purchased.
تعدد (ta'addud): Multiplicity; here, multiple buyers or multiple sellers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can the pre-emptor take the share of only some of the buyers?
A) When there are multiple buyers
B) When there are multiple sellers
C) When the property is undivided
D) Never, he must take all shares

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor may select shares among multiple buyers, but with multiple sellers, he must take all shares together to prevent splitting the transaction.

Pre-emption and Annulment of Partition in Undivided Property
Ruling on pre-emption after partition of undivided half

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on whether a pre-emptor may annul a partition between buyer and seller, and distinguish the relied-upon position from the narration of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor cannot annul a partition that completes possession, despite its exchange elements.

When a buyer purchases half of an undivided property and then partitions with the seller, the pre-emptor faces a dilemma: can he annul that partition? The answer depends on whether the partition is seen as completing possession (which the pre-emptor cannot touch) or as a transaction of exchange (which he can). The Hanafi masters debate this, and the relied-upon view is that the partition stands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The case: someone buys half an undivided house and partitions with the seller. The pre-emptor takes the buyer's share but wants to annul the partition. Determine the central legal question: can he annul it?
2
Analyze the nature of partition
The commentary explains that partition has two aspects: separation (ifraz) and exchange (mubadala). From separation, no annulment; from exchange, annulment possible. Due to this dual nature, doubt arises.
3
Understand the relied-upon position
The majority (Muhammad, al-Sadr al-Shahid, al-Durr al-Mukhtar) hold that the pre-emptor cannot annul the partition, whether by judicial order or consent. The doubt prevents his power to annul.
This is the mufta bihi.
4
Consider the opposing narration
Abu Hanifa is reported to allow annulment if the partition was without a judge (non-judicial). This is because the pre-emptor's right to annul transactions then applies fully.
This is a weaker view.
5
Distinguish the second scenario
If the buyer partitions with the other co-owner (the one who did not sell), the pre-emptor can annul, because that partition is not part of completing the original sale's possession.
This clarifies the scope of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى نصف دار غير مقسوم أخذ الشفيع حظ المشتري بقسمته. وإن اشترى إلخ: يعني لو اشترى نصف دار غير مقسوم، فقاسم المشتري البائع، يأخذ الشفيع نصيب المشتري الذي حصل له بقسمته وليس له أن ينقض القسمة، سواء كانت بقضاء أو تراض؛ لأن القسمة من تمام القبض لما فيه من تكميل الانتفاع تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۸۷/۸] فإن قلت: فيها معنى المبادلة والشفيع يملك نقض تصرفاته فكذا نقض قسمته؟ قلت فيها إفراز من وجه؛ ولهذا يجري فيها الجبر، ومبادلة من وجه؛ ولهذا يجري فيها أحكام البيع من رد بعيب أو خيار رؤية، فبالنظر إلى هذا يملك، وبالنظر إلى ذلك لا يملك، فلا يملك بالشك، ثم إذا لم يكن له نقض قسمته كان له أن يأخذ نصيب المشتري في أي جانب كان قال في الدر المختار: هو الأصح ، وروي عن أبي حنيفة إنما يأخذه إذا وقع في جانب الدار يشفع بها؛ لأنه لم يبق جارا فيما يقع في الجانب الآخر، ثم إذا كانت القسمة بغير حكم ففي نقض القسمة روايتان قال الصدر الشهيد في "واقعاته": والمختار لا نقض. وفي "الدر المختار": هو الأصح، وروي عن أبي حنيفة لو كانت بغير قضاء له النقض، وهذا الحكم المذكور من عدم النقض إذا قاسم المشتري مع البائع، وأما إذا باع أحد الشريكين نصيبه من دار مشتركة وقاسم المشتري الشريك الذي لم يبع، يكون الشفيع نقضه؛ لأن العقد لم يقع من الذي قاسم فلم تكن القسمة من تمام القبض الذي هو حكم البيع الأول، بل هو تصرف بحكم الملك فينقضه الشفيع كما ينقض بيعه وهبته. (ملخصا) غير مقسوم إلخ: قيد بكونه اشترى غير مقسوم؛ لأنه لو اشترى نصفا مقسوما ولم يكن بحذاء دار الشفيع بأن كان في الجانب الآخر لا شفعة للشفيع فيه، وهو نظير ما تقدم من قوله : " وإن باعها إلا ذراعا". (فتح) حظ المشتري بقسمته: أي بقسمة البائع في أي جانب كان على الأصح وليس له نقض قسمته سواء كانت بحكم بالتراضي أو لا. (فتح)
And if he bought half of an undivided house, the pre-emptor takes the buyer's share by his partition. 'And if he bought etc.': meaning if he bought half of an undivided house, and the buyer partitioned with the seller, the pre-emptor takes the buyer's share that came to him by the partition, and he cannot annul the partition, whether it was by judicial order or by mutual consent, because the partition completes the possession in terms of perfecting the benefit. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq 8/287) If you say: It contains the meaning of exchange, and the pre-emptor has the right to annul his transactions, so why cannot he annul his partition? I say: It is a separation from one perspective, and for that reason compulsion applies to it, and an exchange from another perspective, and for that reason the rules of sale apply to it from defect return and option of inspection. From the first perspective he has the power, and from the second he does not, so he does not have it due to doubt. Then, if he cannot annul his partition, he may take the buyer's share in whichever side it falls. Al-Durr al-Mukhtār says: That is the most correct (al-aṣaḥḥ). And it is narrated from Abū Ḥanīfah that he takes it only if it falls in the side of the property that faces his own; because he is no longer a neighbor in the other side. Then if the partition is without a judge, there are two narrations regarding annulment. Al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd in his Wāqīʿāt said: The chosen opinion is no annulment. In al-Durr al-Mukhtār: It is the most correct, and it is narrated from Abū Ḥanīfah that if it is without a judge, he has the right to annul. This ruling of non-annulment applies when the buyer partitions with the seller. But if one of the two partners sells his share in a jointly owned house and the buyer partitions with the partner who did not sell, the pre-emptor can annul it, because the contract was not made with the one who partitioned, so the partition is not part of completing possession that is a ruling of the first sale; rather it is a transaction by virtue of ownership, so the pre-emptor annuls it just as he annuls his sale and gift. (Summarized) 'Undivided etc.': The restriction is that he bought it undivided; because if he bought a divided half and it was not adjacent to the pre-emptor's house, i.e., it was in the other side, there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor in it, similar to what preceded: 'if he sold it except a cubit'. (Fatḥ) 'The buyer's share by his partition': i.e., by the seller's partition, in whichever side it is, according to the most correct view, and he cannot annul his partition whether it was by judicial order or by mutual consent or not. (Fatḥ)
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute. The case: A person buys half of an undivided house, then partitions with the seller. The pre-emptor may take that half, but he cannot annul the partition. The commentary explains why: partition is both a separation (ifraz) and an exchange (mubadala). From the separation aspect, compulsion applies; from the exchange aspect, sale rules apply. The pre-emptor has the power to annul transactions but not if doubt exists, so he does not annul. The relied-upon position is that he cannot annul, regardless of whether the partition was judicial or consensual. However, if the partition was without a judge, there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that he may annul. The passage also distinguishes a scenario where the partition is with the non-selling co-owner: then the pre-emptor can annul, because it is not part of the original sale.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition; dividing jointly owned property into separate shares.
شفيع (shafī'): The person entitled to pre-emption.
نقض (naqḍ): Annulment; the act of voiding a legal transaction or partition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, under what condition can the pre-emptor annul a partition?
A) When the partition was done by mutual consent
B) When the partition was done by a judge
C) When the partition was done with the other co-owner (not the seller)
D) The pre-emptor can never annul a partition

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor cannot annul a partition between buyer and seller of an undivided half because partition completes possession, despite its exchange-like features.

Pre-emption by an Authorized Slave in Debt
Ruling on pre-emption rights for an authorized slave in debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for an authorized slave in debt regarding pre-emption and explain the reasoning based on qiyas.

Core Idea
An indebted authorized slave can pre-empt his master, and vice versa, because pre-emption is like purchase and the slave's debt separates ownership.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in pre-emption law: the case of a slave who is authorized to trade but is burdened with debt. The ruling extends the right of pre-emption in a way that may seem counterintuitive, but it is grounded in the principle that pre-emption is akin to a purchase, and the slave's debt creates a separation of beneficial ownership. Understanding this ruling clarifies how property rights interact with the unique legal personality of a ma'dhun slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the debt condition
Note two scenarios: (1) The master sells a house, and the slave (authorized, in debt) wants to pre-empt. (2) The slave sells a house, and the master wants to pre-empt. The key condition is that the slave's debt covers both his property and his person (i.e., he is insolvent).
The debt must be 'yuhitu bi-malihi wa raqabatihi' – encompassing his assets and his person.
2
Recognize that pre-emption is treated like a purchase
The text states: 'al-akhdh bi al-shuf'ah bi-manzilat al-shira'' – taking by pre-emption is like a purchase. This analogy (qiyas) is the core of the reasoning. So rules about purchase apply to pre-emption when there is no explicit text to the contrary.
3
Apply the rule for purchase between master and indebted slave
Since the slave is in debt, he can validly purchase property from his master because the master does not own what is in the slave's hand; the slave has more claim (milk al-yad). Therefore, the slave can also pre-empt from the master.
The debt creates a separation of ownership – the slave's creditors have priority, making the master's ownership over the slave's property limited.
4
Consider the reverse scenario (master pre-empting from slave)
If the slave is the seller and is in debt, the master can pre-empt the house from the buyer. This is because the sale is ultimately for the master's benefit, and the master's pre-emption right is not lost when the property is bought for him.
The text contrasts this with the case where the slave is not in debt; then the master cannot pre-empt because the sale is for the master himself, and one cannot pre-empt against oneself.
5
Note the exception when the slave is not in debt
If the slave is not in debt (or debt does not encompass his property and person), then the master cannot claim pre-emption when the slave sells. Because the sale directly benefits the master, and pre-emption is not valid for the seller's own benefit.
This exception highlights that the debt condition is crucial for the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعبد المأذون المديون الأخذ بالشفعة من سيّده كعكسه. وللعبد المأذون إلخ صورته: رجل باع دارا، وللبائع عبد مأذون له في التجارة، وعليه دين يحيط بماله ورقبته فللعبد أن يأخذ الدار بالشفعة؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة بمنزلة الشراء، وشراء أحدهما من الآخر يجوز إذا كان على العبد دين؛ لأنه يفيد ملك اليد؛ لكون المولى لا يملك ما في يد العبد المديون، أو لكون العبد أحق به. (عيني، فتح) کعکسه: أي كعكس الحكم المذكور، وهو ما إذا كان العبد هو البائع وعليه دين فلمولاه أخذها بالشفعة كما ذكرنا، بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن عليه دين يحيط بماله ورقبته والعبد بائع؛ لأن بيعه لمولاه، ولا شفعة لمن بيع له على بخلاف ما إذا اشترى العبد فإنه لمولاه الشفعة؛ لأنه ابتيع له، وقد مر أن من ابتاع أو ابتيع له لا تبطل شفعته. رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٥/٢] (فتح)
And for the slave who is authorized and in debt, taking pre-emption from his master, and its reverse. 'And for the authorized slave etc.': Its form: A man sold a house, and the seller has a slave who is authorized to trade and has a debt that encompasses his property and his person; so the slave may take the house by pre-emption, because taking by pre-emption is like purchase, and one of them buying from the other is permissible if the slave has debt, because it confers ownership of possession (milk al-yad) since the master does not own what is in the hand of the indebted slave, or because the slave is more entitled to it. (ʿAynī, Fatḥ) 'Its reverse': i.e., the reverse of the mentioned ruling, which is when the slave is the seller and has debt, then his master takes it by pre-emption as we mentioned, unlike when he does not have debt that encompasses his property and his person and the slave is the seller, because his sale is for his master, and there is no pre-emption for one to whom it is sold. In contrast, if the slave buys, his master has pre-emption because it is purchased for him, and it has passed that one who buys or for whom something is bought does not lose his pre-emption. (Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq 2/345) (Fatḥ).
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement. It establishes that a slave who is authorized to trade (maʾdhūn) and is in debt (with debts covering his property and person) can exercise pre-emption against his master. The reasoning: pre-emption is like a purchase, and if the slave is in debt, purchasing from his master is valid because the master does not own the slave's property, and the slave has more claim. The reverse is also true: if the slave is the seller and in debt, the master can take pre-emption from the slave. However, if the slave is not in debt, the master cannot take pre-emption when the slave sells, because the sale benefits the master, and the master cannot pre-empt himself.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (ʿabd maʾdhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
ديون (duyūn): Debts; here specifically debts that cover both the slave's property and his person (i.e., he is insolvent).
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave authorized to trade and in debt sells his property. Who has the right of pre-emption?
A) Only the slave himself
B) The master of the slave
C) No one has pre-emption
D) Only the buyer

Key Takeaway
An authorized slave in debt can claim pre-emption from his master, and the master can claim from the slave, due to the analogy of pre-emption with purchase and the effect of debt on ownership.

Pre-emption After Partition: The Shafi'i's Right and Limitations
Ruling on shafi'i's right after buyer partitions with seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a shafi'i may take the buyer's share after a partition and when he cannot rescind the partition, applying the Hanafi distinction between ifraz and exchange.

Core Idea
A shafi'i cannot undo a partition that perfects possession, only take the resulting share.

When a buyer purchases an undivided half of a property and then partitions it with the seller, the shafi'i (pre-emptor) still has the right to take the buyer's share. But can he also undo the partition? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, illuminated by its commentator (Fath al-Qadir), explains why partition is treated differently from a sale: because it completes possession (qabd). We will unpack the reasoning and the intra-Hanafi dispute about rescission when the partition was done without a court order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the shafi'i's right
The buyer purchases an undivided half of a house. The shafi'i (a co-owner or neighbour) exercises pre-emption and takes the buyer's share. But the buyer had already partitioned the property with the seller. The question: does the shafi'i take the share as it stands after partition, and can he undo the partition?
The passage states the shafi'i takes the buyer's share in the partition and cannot rescind the partition.
2
Understand the reason: partition completes qabd
The commentary explains that partition is considered completion of taking possession (qabd) because it perfects the buyer's benefit. Although partition has aspects of exchange (it allocates shares), it is primarily a separation (ifraz). Therefore, it is not a pure transaction that the shafi'i can rescind.
The author uses a dialectical objection (fa-in qulta) to address the counterargument: if partition is like a sale, then the shafi'i should be able to rescind it. The response distinguishes ifraz from exchange.
3
Analyse the two cases of partition
The passage distinguishes two situations: (1) buyer partitions with the seller – the shafi'i cannot rescind; (2) buyer partitions with another co-owner (not the seller) – the shafi'i can rescind. The latter is not completion of the original sale's possession; it is a new disposition by ownership.
This distinction is crucial: the shafi'i's power to rescind depends on whether the partition was part of the original sale's qabd.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on court-ordered partition
When the partition was done without a court order (bi-ghayr hukm), there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one allows rescission, another does not. Later authorities (al-Sadr al-Shahid, al-Durr al-Mukhtar) choose the no-rescission view as the most correct (asahh).
The mufta bihi (operative position) is no rescission, even without court order.
5
Apply the adjacency condition
The passage adds a restriction: if the buyer's share after partition falls on the side not adjacent to the shafi'i's property, the shafi'i has no pre-emption. This is analogous to a prior rule about selling a house except a cubit.
Pre-emption requires that the sold property be contiguous to the shafi'i's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى نصف دار غير مقسوم أخذ الشفيع حظ المشتري بقسمته، وليس له أن ينقض القسمة، سواء كانت بقضاء أو تراض؛ لأن القسمة من تمام القبض لما فيه من تكميل الانتفاع. فإن قلت: فيها معنى المبادلة والشفيع يملك نقض تصرفاته فكذا نقض قسمته؟ قلت فيها إفراز من وجه؛ ولهذا يجري فيها الجبر، ومبادلة من وجه؛ ولهذا يجري فيها أحكام البيع من رد بعيب أو خيار رؤية، فبالنظر إلى هذا يملك، وبالنظر إلى ذلك لا يملك، فلا يملك بالشك، ثم إذا لم يكن له نقض قسمته كان له أن يأخذ نصيب المشتري في أي جانب كان قال في الدر المختار: هو الأصح، وروي عن أبي حنيفة إنما يأخذه إذا وقع في جانب الدار يشفع بها؛ لأنه لم يبق جارا فيما يقع في الجانب الآخر، ثم إذا كانت القسمة بغير حكم ففي نقض القسمة روايتان قال الصدر الشهيد في "واقعاته": والمختار لا نقض. وفي "الدر المختار": هو الأصح، وروي عن أبي حنيفة لو كانت بغير قضاء له النقض، وهذا الحكم المذكور من عدم النقض إذا قاسم المشتري مع البائع، وأما إذا باع أحد الشريكين نصيبه من دار مشتركة وقاسم المشتري الشريك الذي لم يبع، يكون الشفيع نقضه؛ لأن العقد لم يقع من الذي قاسم فلم تكن القسمة من تمام القبض الذي هو حكم البيع الأول، بل هو تصرف بحكم الملك فينقضه الشفيع كما ينقض بيعه وهبته. (ملخصا) غير مقسوم إلخ: قيد بكونه اشترى غير مقسوم؛ لأنه لو اشترى نصفا مقسوما ولم يكن بحذاء دار الشفيع بأن كان في الجانب الآخر لا شفعة للشفيع فيه، وهو نظير ما تقدم من قوله: " وإن باعها إلا ذراعا". (فتح) حظ المشتري بقسمته: أي بقسمة البائع في أي جانب كان على الأصح وليس له نقض قسمته سواء كانت بحكم بالتراضي أو لا. (فتح)
And if he buys half of an undivided house, the shafi'i takes the buyer's share in his partition, and he does not have the right to rescind the partition, whether it was by court order or mutual consent, because partition completes the taking of possession (qabd) since it perfects the benefit. If you say: it has an element of exchange and the shafi'i has the power to rescind his transactions, so similarly he should rescind his partition? I say: it is a separation (ifraz) in one respect, and for this reason coercion (jabr) applies in it, and an exchange in another respect, and for this reason the rules of sale apply to it such as return for defect or option of inspection. So from one perspective he has power, from another he does not, so he does not have power due to doubt. Then if he does not have the power to rescind his partition, he may take the buyer's share in whichever side it falls. It is said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: that is the most correct view. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he only takes it if it falls on the side of the house where pre-emption applies, because he is no longer a neighbour regarding what falls on the other side. Then if the partition was without a court order, there are two narrations regarding rescinding it. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said in his Waqi'at: the chosen view is no rescission. In al-Durr al-Mukhtar: it is the most correct view, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that if it was without a court order, he has the power to rescind. This ruling of no rescission applies when the buyer partitioned with the seller. As for when one of the co-owners sells his share in a jointly owned house and the buyer partitions with the co-owner who did not sell, the shafi'i has the power to rescind it, because the contract was not made by the one who partitioned, so the partition is not completion of the qabd which is the rule of the first sale, but rather it is a disposition by virtue of ownership, so the shafi'i rescinds it just as he rescinds his sale and gift. (Summarized) "Undivided" etc.: The restriction to buying undivided half is because if he bought a divided half and it was not adjacent to the shafi'i's house, i.e., it was on the other side, then there is no pre-emption for the shafi'i in it, and this is analogous to what preceded from his statement: "if he sold it except a cubit." (Fath) "The buyer's share in his partition": i.e., by the seller's partition in whichever side it falls, according to the most correct view. And he does not have the power to rescind his partition whether it was by court order or by mutual consent or not. (Fath)
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the book Kanz al-Daqaiq. It explains the pre-emption (shuf'a) rights when a person buys an undivided half of a property and then partitions it with the seller. The key ruling: the shafi'i (pre-emptor) takes the buyer's share as it stands after the partition, but cannot undo the partition itself. The reason given is that the partition perfects the possession (qabd) and is not merely an exchange. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if partition has an element of exchange, why can't the shafi'i rescind it like other transactions? The answer is that partition is primarily a separation (ifraz) and coercion (jabr) applies, showing it's not a pure exchange. The passage also discusses details: if the partition was without a court order, there are two views; the chosen view is no rescission. However, if the partition was between the buyer and another co-owner (not the seller), the shafi'i can rescind it because it is not completion of the original sale's possession. The restriction to an undivided half is emphasized: if the half was already divided and not adjacent to the shafi'i's property, there is no pre-emption. This is consistent with the principle that pre-emption requires adjacency. The text cites al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Fath al-Qadir as authorities.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption allowing a co-owner or neighbour to purchase a sold share to prevent harm.
القسمة (al-qisma): Partition of jointly owned property into separate shares, which perfects possession.
الإفراز (al-ifraz): Separation of shares from jointly owned property; partition viewed as ifraz rather than exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases an undivided half of a house and partitions it with the seller. The shafi'i wants to take the buyer's share and also rescind the partition. According to the Hanafi school, what is the shafi'i's right?
A) He can take the share and rescind the partition only if it was done without a court order.
B) He can take the share but cannot rescind the partition regardless of how it was done.
C) He can rescind the partition but cannot take the share after partition.
D) He cannot take the share because partition cancels pre-emption.

Key Takeaway
The shafi'i takes the buyer's share after partition but cannot undo the partition because it perfects possession (qabd), and this applies even without a court order.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Guardian Relinquishing Minor's Shuf'a
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father/guardian/agent relinquishing shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the validity of a guardian relinquishing a minor's shuf'a, explain the legal reasoning (qiyas) behind each, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A guardian's relinquishment of a minor's pre-emption is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but invalid according to Muhammad and Zufar, because they differ on whether it is pure harm or a benefit-harm trade-off.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq delves into a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: can a father or guardian give up the minor's right of shuf'a? The answer divides the early Hanafi authorities. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that shuf'a is akin to trade, and leaving it is within the guardian's discretion, while Muhammad and Zufar see it as annulling a right meant to prevent harm, thus invalid. Understanding their proofs is essential for knowing when a minor can later reclaim the property. The passage also extends the discussion to agents and price variations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
State clearly that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the relinquishment valid (binding on the minor), while Muhammad and Zufar hold it invalid (minor retains right upon majority).
This is the core thamarah al-khilaf: whether the minor can later take the property.
2
Analyze the proof of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They analogize shuf'a to trade (mubadalah al-mal bi al-mal). Just as a guardian can choose not to trade, he can leave shuf'a. This is not pure harm because keeping the money may be more beneficial.
The key illa: shuf'a is a beneficial exchange, so leaving it is a discretionary decision.
3
Analyze the proof of Muhammad and Zufar
They analogize relinquishment to pardoning qisas, freeing a slave, or remitting a debt—all annulments of rights without compensation, causing pure harm. Since shuf'a is meant to repel harm, annulling it causes harm.
The key illa: shuf'a is a protective right, so giving it up is pure detriment.
4
Compare the two qiyas and evaluate the tarjih
The text leans toward Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as their analogy captures the dual nature of shuf'a (both benefit and potential harm). The ruling is not unequivocal harm; discretion is allowed.
The later Hanafi tradition generally follows the majority view.
5
Apply the nuances: price deviations and agents
If the sale price is significantly above market, relinquishment may be valid by consensus (taking would be harmful); if below, it is invalid according to Imam. Agents: agent for purchase can validly relinquish; agent for demanding shuf'a has further dispute (court vs. anywhere).
These nuances show the fiqh sensitivity to context and interests of the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح تسليم الشفعة من الأب والوصي والوكيل. يعني أن الحمل والصغير في استحقاق الشفعة كالكبير؛ لاستوائهما في سببه، فيقوم بالطلب، والأخذ والتسليم ممن يقوم مقامهما، وهو الأب، ثم وصيه ثم أب الأب، ثم وصيه ثم الوصي الذي نصبه القاضي؛ فلو سلم هؤلاء الشفعة تسليمهم عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف حمله، حتى لو اشترى رجل دارا وشفيعها صبي، فسلم أبوه أو الوصي، يصح تسليمه حتى لا يكون للصبي أن يأخذها إذا بلغ. وقال محمد وزفر يحملها لا يصح حتى كان له أن يأخذها بالشفعة إذا بلغ، وعلى هذا الخلاف بطلان الشفعة بسكوت الأب والوصي عند العلم بالشراء للإمام محمد وزفر يحملها أن هذا إبطال لحق الصبي، فلا يصح كالعفو عن القود وإعتاق عبده وإبراء غريمه، ولأن تصرفهما نظري، والنظر يتعين في الأخذ، ألا ترى أنه شرع لدفع الضرر، فكان في إبطاله إلحاق الضرر به، فلا يملك. ولهما: أن الأخذ بالشفعة في معنى التجارة بل هو عينها ألا ترى أنه مبادلة المال بالمال وترك الأخذ بها ترك التجارة، فيملكه كما يملك ترك التجارة، يوضحه أن لو أخذها بالشفعة ثم باعها من ذلك الرجل بعينه، جاز، فكذا إذا سلمه إليه بل أولى، ولأن هذا تصرف دائر بين النفع والضرر ، فيحتمل أن يكون الترك أنفع بإبقاء الثمن على ملك الصبي، بخلاف العفو عن القود وما ذكر معه؛ لأنه ضرر محض غير متردد، ولأنه إبطال بغير عوض، هذا إذا بيعت بمثل قيمتها، وإن بيعت بأكثر من قيمتها بما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله فقيل: جاز التسليم بالإجماع؛ لأن النظر متعين فيه، وقيل: لا يجوز التسليم بالإجماع، وهو الأصح؛ لأنه لا يملك الأخذ، فلا يملك التسليم كالأجنبي فيكون الصبي على حقه إذا بلغ، وإن بيعت بأقل من قيمتها بمحاباة كثيرة، فعند الإمام: لا يصح تسليم الأب والوصي، ولا رواية عن أبي يوسف قال في "النهاية": ولما لم يصح على قول الإمام، لا يصح على قول محمد وزفر بالأولى. (تكملة، ملا مسكين) والوكيل بالجر عطفا على الأب يعني الوكيل بالشراء تسليم الشفعة منه صحيح، والمراد بالوكيل ههنا الوكيل بطلب الشفعة، أما الوكيل بالشراء، فتسليمه الشفعة صحيح بالإجماع، وكذا سكوته إعراض بالإجماع، والوكيل بطلب الشفعة إنما يصح تسليمه في مجلس القاضي عند الإمام، وعند أبي يوسف يصح في مجلس القاضي وغيره، وعند محمد وزفر : لا يصح تسليمه أصلا؛ لأنه أتى بضد ما أمر به فصار كما لو وكله باستيفاء الدين، فأبرأه منه. ولهما: توكيل بالشراء له؛ لأن الأخذ بها شراء، والوكيل بالشراء له أن يشتري فله أن يترك الشفعة غير أن أبا يوسف يقول: هو وكيل مطلق، فينفذ تصرفه مطلقا، والإمام يقول: الوكيل بطلب الشفعة وكيل بالخصومة، ولا تعتبر الخصومة في غير مجلس القاضي، فلا يكون وكيلا في غير مجلس الحاكم، ولو أقر الوكيل بطلب الشفعة على وكله بأن سلم الشفعة جاز إقراره عليه عند الإمام ومحمد إذا كان في مجلس القاضي، وإن كان في غيره فلا يجوز إلا أنه يخرج من الخصومة، وقال أبو يوسف يجوز مطلقا، وقال زفر لا يجوز مطلقا.
And the relinquishment of pre-emption by the father, guardian, and agent is valid. This means that the fetus and minor in entitlement to pre-emption are like the adult, because they are equal in its cause, so the one who stands in their place undertakes the demand, taking, and relinquishing. That is the father, then his executor, then the paternal grandfather, then his executor, then the executor appointed by the judge. So if these persons relinquish pre-emption, their relinquishment is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, to the extent that if a man bought a house and its pre-emptor is a minor, and his father or guardian relinquished, his relinquishment is valid, so the minor has no right to take it when he reaches majority. Muhammad and Zufar said: it is not valid, so he has the right to take it by pre-emption when he reaches majority. And upon this difference is the nullification of pre-emption by the silence of the father and guardian upon knowledge of the purchase. According to Imam Muhammad and Zufar, this is an annulment of the minor's right, so it is not valid, like the pardon of qisas, freeing his slave, and remitting his debt. And because their disposition is discretionary (nazari), and discretion requires taking it, do you not see that it was legislated to repel harm, so in annulling it is causing harm to him, so they do not have the authority. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): taking by pre-emption is in the nature of trade, rather it is exactly trade, do you not see that it is an exchange of property for property, and leaving it is leaving trade, so they have authority just as they have authority to leave trade. This is clarified by the fact that if he took it by pre-emption and then sold it to that very man, it would be permissible, so likewise if he relinquished it to him, rather it is more apt. And because this disposition lies between benefit and harm, it is possible that leaving it is more beneficial by keeping the price in the minor's ownership, unlike the pardon of qisas etc., because that is pure harm without hesitation, and because it is annulment without compensation. This is when it was sold at its value. If it was sold at more than its value by an amount not ordinarily overlooked in bargaining, then some said: relinquishment is valid by consensus because discretion is definite in it, and some said: relinquishment is not valid by consensus, and that is the more correct, because he does not have the authority to take, so he does not have the authority to relinquish like a stranger, so the minor retains his right when he reaches majority. And if it was sold for less than its value with significant favor, then according to Imam: the relinquishment of father and guardian is not valid, and there is no narration from Abu Yusuf. It is said in al-Nihaya: since it is not valid according to Imam's opinion, it is even more so not valid according to Muhammad and Zufar. (Takmila, Mulla Miskin) And "al-wakil" in the genitive case as a conjunction to "al-ab", meaning the agent for purchase, his relinquishment of pre-emption is valid. And what is meant by agent here is the agent for demanding pre-emption. As for the agent for purchase, his relinquishment of pre-emption is valid by consensus, and similarly his silence is considered a waiver by consensus. As for the agent for demanding pre-emption, his relinquishment is only valid in the court of the judge according to Imam. According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid in the court and elsewhere. According to Muhammad and Zufar, it is not valid at all because he did the opposite of what he was instructed, so it is as if he was appointed to collect a debt and he remitted it. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Imam?): he is appointed for purchase because taking pre-emption is purchase, and the agent for purchase has the right to buy, so he has the right to leave pre-emption. However, Abu Yusuf says: he is an absolute agent, so his disposition is effective absolutely. Imam says: the agent for demanding pre-emption is an agent for litigation, and litigation is only considered in the court, so he is not an agent outside the court. And if the agent for demanding pre-emption confessed against his principal that he relinquished pre-emption, his confession is valid against his principal according to Imam and Muhammad if it was in the court, but if outside it is not valid, except that he is disqualified from litigation. Abu Yusuf said it is valid absolutely, and Zufar said it is not valid at all.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents an extensive intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a father, guardian, or agent relinquishing the pre-emption right of a minor. The background: a minor (or fetus) has the right of shuf'a like an adult, but the father, guardian, etc., act on his behalf. The question is whether they can give up that right. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the relinquishment is valid and binding, so the minor cannot later take the property upon majority. Their reasoning: taking shuf'a is akin to trade; leaving it is like leaving trade, which the guardian can do. They also argue that the decision involves balancing benefit and harm; sometimes leaving it is more beneficial (e.g., keeping the money instead of tying it up in property). Muhammad and Zufar disagree, saying the guardian cannot forfeit the minor's right because it is pure harm (since shuf'a is meant to prevent harm) and analogous to other acts like pardoning qisas, which are clearly harmful. The passage then outlines nuances: if the sale price is significantly higher than market value, some say relinquishment is valid by consensus (because taking would be harmful), while others say it's invalid (the minority view is the more correct). If the price is significantly lower, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the relinquishment is invalid. The discussion then extends to agents: an agent for purchase can validly relinquish shuf'a by consensus, but an agent specifically appointed to demand shuf'a (litigation agent) has disputed validity: Imam Abu Hanifa restricts it to the court, Abu Yusuf allows it anywhere, and Muhammad and Zufar disallow it entirely. The passage concludes with the effect of confession in such cases.

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslim): Relinquishment or waiver of the right of pre-emption.
الوصي (al-wasi): An executor appointed in a will or by the court to manage a minor's affairs.
الوكيل (al-wakil): Agent; here, specifically an agent for purchasing or for demanding pre-emption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, why is the father's relinquishment of the minor's pre-emption valid?
A) Because pre-emption is a right that belongs to the father.
B) Because taking pre-emption is like trade, and leaving trade is within the guardian's authority.
C) Because the minor cannot own property anyway.
D) Because the father's decision is always final.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a guardian's relinquishment of a minor's shuf'a hinges on whether it is seen as analogous to trade (valid) or to pure harm (invalid); the majority Hanafi view deems it valid, with price-dependent exceptions.

Guardian's Surrender of Shuf'ah: Valid or Invalid?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guardian surrender of shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad/Zufar on guardian surrender of shuf'ah, and apply the conditions that determine validity.

Core Idea
Guardian surrender of shuf'ah is valid unless it causes clear harm, per Abu Hanifa.

Shuf'ah (preemption) is a right that can be claimed or surrendered. When the right belongs to a minor, the guardian (father, executor, etc.) acts on their behalf. But is the guardian's surrender valid, or does the minor retain the right until adulthood? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit it, while Muhammad and Zufar forbid it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical cases where the ruling changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute is about whether a guardian (father/executor/grandfather) can validly surrender a minor's right of shuf'ah. Understand the two opposing positions: Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf say yes; Muhammad & Zufar say no.
2
Analyze the proofs
Trace the qiyas used by each side. Abu Hanifa compares shuf'ah to trade (abandoning trade is permitted), while Muhammad compares it to pardoning qisas (pure harm, not permitted). Note how the concept of 'benefit to the minor' is interpreted differently.
3
Apply to sale price variants
The passage specifies three price scenarios: fair price, excessive profit, and excessive loss. For each, determine if the ruling changes or remains disputed. For fair price, the dispute stands; for excessive profit, a consensus view emerges but is contested; for excessive loss, surrender is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.
4
Distinguish agent types
Separate the ruling for an agent appointed to purchase (wakil bi-shira') from an agent appointed only to demand shuf'ah (wakil bi-talab). The former's surrender is valid by consensus; the latter's surrender is valid only in court according to Abu Hanifa, everywhere according to Abu Yusuf, and never according to Muhammad and Zufar.
Note the underlying usul: the agent's authority is limited by the mandate.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf for normal cases (fair price). For cases of clear harm (excessive loss or improper benefit), surrender is invalid. The minority view of Muhammad and Zufar is not adopted for general practice.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah preferred Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يبطل به الشفعة
وصح تسليم الشفعة من الأب والوصي والوكيل.
تسليم الشفعة إلخ يعني أن الحمل والصغير في استحقاق الشفعة كالكبير؛ لاستوائهما في سببه، فيقوم بالطلب، والأخذ والتسليم ممن يقوم مقامهما، وهو الأب، ثم وصيه ثم أب الأب، ثم وصيه ثم الوصي الذي نصبه القاضي؛ فلو سلم هؤلاء الشفعة تسليمهم عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف حمله، حتى لو اشترى رجل دارا وشفيعها صبي، فسلم أبوه أو الوصي، يصح تسليمه حتى لا يكون للصبي أن يأخذها إذا بلغ. وقال محمد وزفر يحملها لا يصح حتى كان له أن يأخذها بالشفعة إذا بلغ، وعلى هذا الخلاف بطلان الشفعة بسكوت الأب والوصي عند العلم بالشراء للإمام محمد وزفر يحملها أن هذا إبطال لحق الصبي، فلا يصح كالعفو عن القود وإعتاق عبده وإبراء غريمه، ولأن تصرفهما نظري، والنظر يتعين في الأخذ، ألا ترى أنه شرع لدفع الضرر، فكان في إبطاله إلحاق الضرر به، فلا يملك.
ولهما: أن الأخذ بالشفعة في معنى التجارة بل هو عينها ألا ترى أنه مبادلة المال بالمال وترك الأخذ بها ترك التجارة، فيملكه كما يملك ترك التجارة، يوضحه أن لو أخذها بالشفعة ثم باعها من ذلك الرجل بعينه، جاز، فكذا إذا سلمه إليه بل أولى، ولأن هذا تصرف دائر بين النفع والضرر ، فيحتمل أن يكون الترك أنفع بإبقاء الثمن على ملك الصبي، بخلاف العفو عن القود وما ذكر معه؛ لأنه ضرر محض غير متردد، ولأنه إبطال بغير عوض، هذا إذا بيعت بمثل قيمتها، وإن بيعت بأكثر من قيمتها بما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله فقيل: جاز التسليم بالإجماع؛ لأن النظر متعين فيه، وقيل: لا يجوز التسليم بالإجماع، وهو الأصح؛ لأنه لا يملك الأخذ، فلا يملك التسليم كالأجنبي فيكون الصبي على حقه إذا بلغ، وإن بيعت بأقل من قيمتها بمحاباة كثيرة، فعند الإمام: لا يصح تسليم الأب والوصي، ولا رواية عن أبي يوسف قال في "النهاية : ولما لم يصح على قول الإمام، لا يصح على قول محمد وزفر بالأولى. (تكملة، ملا مسكين)
أنه
والوكيل بالجر عطفا على الأب يعني الوكيل بالشراء تسليم الشفعة منه صحيح، والمراد بالوكيل ههنا الوكيل بطلب الشفعة، أما الوكيل بالشراء، فتسليمه الشفعة صحيح بالإجماع، وكذا سكوته إعراض بالإجماع، والوكيل بطلب الشفعة إنما يصح تسليمه في مجلس القاضي عند الإمام، وعند أبي يوسف يصح في مجلس القاضي وغيره، وعند محمد وزفر : لا . يصح تسليمه أصلا؛ لأنه أتى بضد ما أمر به فصار كما لو وكله باستيفاء الدين، فأبرأه منه. ولهما: توكيل بالشراء له؛ لأن الأخذ بها شراء، والوكيل بالشراء له أن يشتري فله أن يترك الشفعة غير أن أبا يوسف يقول: هو وكيل مطلق، فينفذ تصرفه مطلقا، والإمام يقول: الوكيل بطلب الشفعة وكيل بالخصومة، ولا تعتبر الخصومة في غير مجلس القاضي، فلا يكون وكيلا في غير مجلس الحاكم، ولو أقر الوكيل بطلب الشفعة على وكله بأن سلم الشفعة جاز إقراره عليه عند الإمام ومحمد إذا كان في مجلس القاضي، وإن كان في غيره فلا يجوز إلا أنه يخرج من الخصومة، وقال أبو يوسف يجوز مطلقا، وقال زفر لا يجوز مطلقا.
What nullifies shuf'ah: The surrender of shuf'ah from the father, executor, and agent is valid. 'Surrender of shuf'ah' etc. means that the fetus and minor in entitlement to shuf'ah are like the adult; because they are equal in its cause. So the demand, taking, and surrender are carried out by whoever stands in their place, which is the father, then his executor, then the grandfather, then his executor, then the executor appointed by the judge. So if these surrender the shuf'ah, their surrender is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. So if a man buys a house and its preemptor is a minor, and the father or executor surrenders, his surrender is valid, so the minor has no right to take it when he becomes adult. Muhammad and Zufar said: His surrender is not valid, so he has the right to take it by shuf'ah when he becomes adult. On this difference also depends the invalidation of shuf'ah by the silence of the father and executor upon knowledge of the purchase. According to Imam Muhammad and Zufar, this is an annulment of the minor's right, so it is not valid, like the pardon of qisas, manumission of his slave, and remission of his debtor's debt. And because their action is supervisory, and supervision is specifically for taking; do you not see that it (shuf'ah) was legislated to repel harm, so invalidating it causes harm to him, thus they do not have the authority. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): Taking by shuf'ah is in the meaning of trade, rather it is exactly trade; do you not see that it is exchange of property for property, and abandoning taking it is abandoning trade, so they have authority over it just as they have authority over abandoning trade. This is clarified by the fact that if one took it by shuf'ah and then sold it to that same person, it is permissible, so likewise if he surrendered it to him, rather more so. And because this action is oscillating between benefit and harm, so it is possible that abandoning is more beneficial by keeping the price in the possession of the minor, unlike the pardon of qisas and what was mentioned with it, because it is pure harm without hesitation, and because it is annulment without compensation. This is when it is sold at its value. If it is sold for more than its value to an extent not ordinarily overlooked among people, it is said that surrender is valid by consensus because the benefit is specifically in it; and it is said that surrender is not valid by consensus, which is the correct view, because he does not own the taking, so he does not own the surrender, like a stranger, so the minor retains his right when he becomes adult. If it is sold for less than its value with a large favor, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) the surrender of father and executor is not valid, and there is no narration from Abu Yusuf. It is said in al-Nihaya: Since it is not valid according to the Imam, it is even more so not valid according to Muhammad and Zufar. (Supplement from Mulla Miskeen) 'And the agent' (bil-jarr) is a conjunction to 'the father', meaning the agent for purchase: his surrender of shuf'ah is valid. The agent here refers to the agent for demanding shuf'ah. As for the agent for purchase, his surrender of shuf'ah is valid by consensus, and likewise his silence is a relinquishment by consensus. The agent for demanding shuf'ah: his surrender is valid only in the court of the judge according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid in court and elsewhere. According to Muhammad and Zufar, his surrender is not valid at all, because he did the opposite of what he was ordered, so it is like when someone appoints him to collect a debt and he remits it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): It is an agency for purchase for him, because taking by shuf'ah is a purchase, and the agent for purchase has the right to buy, so he has the right to abandon shuf'ah. However, Abu Yusuf says: He is an absolute agent, so his action is valid absolutely. The Imam says: The agent for demanding shuf'ah is an agent for litigation, and litigation is not valid outside the court, so he is not an agent outside the court. If the agent for demanding shuf'ah confesses against his principal that he surrendered shuf'ah, his confession is valid according to Imam and Muhammad if in court; if outside court it is not valid, except that he is dismissed from the litigation. Abu Yusuf said it is valid absolutely, and Zufar said it is not valid absolutely.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi legal dispute concerning the validity of surrendering the right of preemption (shuf'ah) by guardians and agents on behalf of a child. The text first states that the fetus and minor are entitled to shuf'ah like an adult, so the demand, taking, and surrender must be performed by those who stand in their place: father, executor, grandfather, etc. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the surrender by these guardians is valid, so the child loses the right upon adulthood. Muhammad and Zufar disagree, arguing that such surrender invalidates the child's right without benefit and is akin to pardoning qisas, which is pure harm and thus not permitted for a guardian. The debate centers on the nature of shuf'ah: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf liken it to trade, where the guardian can abandon it as he can abandon trade, while Muhammad and Zufar view the guardian's duty as strictly to benefit the child, and surrender is contrary to that duty. The text further differentiates cases based on the sale price: if sold at a fair price, the above dispute applies; if sold at an excessive profit, some say surrender is valid by consensus (since clearly beneficial), but the correct view is that it is not valid because the guardian cannot take if it is against the child's interest; if sold at a loss with a large favor, the surrender is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. Then the text discusses the agent (wakil): an agent for purchase can surrender shuf'ah by consensus; an agent specifically for demanding shuf'ah has his surrender valid only in the judge's court according to Abu Hanifa, valid everywhere according to Abu Yusuf, and never valid according to Muhammad and Zufar—the latter because he acts against his mandate. The passage concludes with the ruling on confession of surrender by the agent.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of preemption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the share sold to a third party, to prevent harm.
تسليم الشفعة (taslim al-shuf'ah): The act of voluntarily surrendering or relinquishing the right of preemption, which nullifies the right.
وكيل بطلب الشفعة (wakil bi-talab al-shuf'ah): An agent appointed specifically to demand the right of preemption, not to purchase or surrender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a father surrenders his minor son's right of preemption when the property is sold at a fair price, what is the status of that right?
A) The surrender is invalid, and the son can claim the property upon adulthood.
B) The surrender is valid, and the son loses the right permanently.
C) The surrender is valid only if the property was sold at a loss.
D) The surrender requires court approval to be valid.

Key Takeaway
The surrender of shuf'ah by a guardian is valid in normal cases per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but invalid when it clearly harms the minor (e.g., excessive profit or loss).

Definition and Legal Essentials of Qisma
Defines qisma and outlines its rukn, sabab, shart, hukm, and dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of qisma, identify its pillar (rukn), cause (sabab), condition (shart), ruling (hukm), and proof (dalil), and apply these to determine the validity of a division.

Core Idea
Division is both separation and exchange: it separates shares but each partner gives up part of his share in compensation.

This passage opens the Book of Division (Kitab al-Qisma) in a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It clarifies what qisma means linguistically and legally, distinguishes it from pre-emption, and systematically lists the five essential elements of a valid division: its pillar, cause, condition, legal effect, and scriptural proof. Understanding these elements is foundational for applying the Hanafi law of partition in practice.

Guided Path
1
Define qisma linguistically and legally
Linguistically, qisma means sharing (al-iqtisam) and removing commonality. Legally, the passage defines it as 'collecting a common share in a specific thing'—a combination of separating (ifraz) and exchanging (mubadala). Note that the Hanafi view includes exchange because when shares are divided, each partner receives his portion and that portion contains a piece of the other's original share, requiring compensation.
The definition applies to division in material things (a'yan); division in usufructs (manafi') is an exchange of a usufruct for its like.
2
Identify the two types of division
The passage explicitly states two types: division in material things (a'yan) and division in usufructs (manafi'). The definition provided is for the first type. The second type is an exchange (mubadala) of a usufruct for its like.
This classification affects how the rules apply—for example, in a'yan the focus is on physical separation, while in manafi' the focus is on timing and use.
3
Extract the rukn (pillar) of division
The pillar is 'an action by which separation between shares is achieved,' such as measuring, weighing, counting, or sowing. Without such an act, the division does not occur.
The act must be appropriate to the type of property—e.g., measuring for land, weighing for grain.
4
Identify the sabab (cause) and shart (condition)
The cause is a request from a partner to benefit from his share. If no one requests division, it is not valid. The condition is that no benefit is lost through the division; the separated part must remain as it was before in substance and benefits.
This condition ensures that division does not cause loss (e.g., dividing a small shop that becomes useless).
5
State the hukm (legal effect) and dalil (proof)
The effect is that each partner's share becomes individually specified (ta'ayyun). The proofs are from the Quran (e.g., verse on dividing water, verse on attending division of inheritance, verse on spoils), the Sunnah (the Prophet's practice in spoils and inheritances), and consensus.
These proofs establish the legitimacy and binding nature of division in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة
تعريف القسمة وأحكامها
كتاب القسمة: أي هذا كتاب في بيان أحكام القسمة ووجه المناسبة بينه وبين كتاب الشفعة: أن الشفعة شرعت لدفع ضرر الجار، وتكميل منفعة الملك جبرا، فكذا القسمة شرعت لتكميل منفعة الملك، ويجري فيها الجبر إلا أن في الشفعة كمل معنى المبادلة فتقدم، وذكر الاتقاني أن وجه المناسبة من حيث إن كلا منهما من نتائج النصيب الشائع؛ لأن أقوى أسباب الشفعة الشركة في نفس المبيع، وتقدم الشفعة على القسمة؛ لأن التملك بالشفعة ربما يكون سببا للقسمة، والسبب مقدم على المسبب. (من) ملا مسكين وفتح الله المعين باختصار)
القسمة:
هي في اللغة: اسم للاقتسام كالقدوة للاقتداء ومعناها: رفع الشيوع وقطع الشركة، وفي الشرع ما بينه بقوله هي إلخ. (عيني) هي جمع نصيب إلخ: هذا معناه شرعا؛ لأنه ما من جزء معين إلا وهو مشتمل على النصيبين، فكان ما يقبضه كل واحد منهما نصفه ملكه ونصفه ملك صاحبه، فإذا وقعت القسمة صار حصة صاحبه فيما وقع في نصيبه عوضا عما فاته في نصيب صاحبه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٩٠/٨] واعلم أن القسمة نوعان: قسمة في الأعيان وقسمة في المنافع، وهذا التعريف الذي ذكره للقسمة في الأعيان، وأما القسمة في المنافع فلم يعرفها المصنف، وهي شرعا مبادلة المنفعة بجنسها، ثم لابد هنا من بيان ركن القسمة وسببها وشرطها وحكمها ودليل مشروعيتها، فركنها فعل يحصل به التمييز بين الأنصباء كالكيل والوزن والعد والزرع. وسببها: طلب الشركاء أو أحدهم الانتفاع بحصته حتى إذا لم يوجد الطلب، لا تصح القسمة. وشرطها: عدم فوت المنفعة بالقسمة، فإنها إفراز ما لكل واحد قبل ا القسمة الملك من والمنفعة، وإنما يتحقق هذا إذا بقي المفرز على ما كان قبل الإفراز بأصله ومنافعه وحكمها تعين نصيب كل واحد على حدة؛ لأنه الأثر المترتب عليها. ودليلها: الكتاب: ﴿وَتَبْتْهُمْ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ قِسْمَةٌ بَيْنَهُمْ﴾ (القمر: ۲۸) ﴿وَإِذَا حَضَرَ الْقِسْمَةَ أُولُو الْقُرْبَى (النساء: ۸)، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا غَنِمْتُمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ فَأَنَّ لِلَّهِ خُمُسَهُ وَلِلرَّسُولِ ﴾ (الأنفال: (٤١)، وإنما يعلم الخمس من أربعة الأخماس بالقسمة، والسنة: لمباشرته عليها لها في الغنائم والمواريث، والإجماع. (تكملة، فتح)
The Book of Division: Definition of Division and Its Rulings. The Book of Division: i.e., this is a book in explaining the rulings of division and the aspect of appropriateness between it and the Book of Pre-emption: that pre-emption was legislated to repel the harm of a neighbor and to complete the benefit of ownership by compulsion, so likewise division was legislated to complete the benefit of ownership, and compulsion occurs in it, except that in pre-emption the meaning of exchange is complete so it was given precedence. And al-Atqani mentioned that the aspect of appropriateness is that both are results of a common share; because the strongest cause of pre-emption is partnership in the very thing sold, and pre-emption precedes division because ownership by pre-emption may be a cause for division, and the cause is given precedence over the effect. (From Mulla Miskeen and Fath Allah al-Mu'een, summarized). Division: linguistically it is a noun for sharing like 'qudwa' for following, and its meaning is removing commonality and cutting partnership. In sacred law it is what the author indicated by his saying 'it is' etc. (Ayni). 'It is a collection of a share' etc.: this is its meaning legally; because there is no specific part except that it contains two shares, so what each one takes is half his property and half his companion's property. When division occurs, his companion's share in what falls into his portion becomes compensation for what he missed in his companion's portion. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/290). Know that division is of two types: division in material things and division in usufructs. This definition that the author mentioned is for division in material things. As for division in usufructs, the author did not define it, and it is legally an exchange of a usufruct for its like. Then it is necessary here to explain the pillar of division, its cause, its condition, its legal effect, and the proof of its legitimacy. Its pillar: an action by which separation between shares is achieved, like measuring, weighing, counting, and sowing. Its cause: the request of the partners or one of them to benefit from his share, such that if no request exists, the division is not valid. Its condition: that the benefit is not lost by the division; for it is the separation of what each has before division in ownership and benefit, and this is realized only if the separated part remains as it was before separation in its substance and benefits. Its legal effect: the specification of each one's share individually; because it is the effect resulting from it. Its proof: from the Book: 'And inform them that the water is to be divided between them' (al-Qamar 28), 'And when the division is attended by relatives' (al-Nisa 8), and He said: 'And know that whatever you have taken as spoils, for Allah is a fifth thereof and for the Messenger' (al-Anfal 41), and the fifth is only known from the four-fifths by division. And the Sunnah: his direct practice of it in spoils and inheritances. And consensus. (Takmila, Fath).
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Division (Kitab al-Qisma) from a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by explaining the relationship between the chapter on Division and the preceding chapter on Pre-emption (Shuf'a). Both are means to complete the benefit of ownership, but pre-emption involves exchange, while division involves separation. The author then defines qisma linguistically as 'removing commonality' and legally as 'collection of a common share in a specific [thing]'. He states that division is of two types: division in material things (a'yan) and division in usufructs (manafi'). The definition given is for the first type. The passage then comprehensively lists the legal essentials: the pillar (rukn) is an act that distinguishes shares; the cause (sabab) is the request of a partner; the condition (shart) is that the division does not cause loss of benefit; the ruling (hukm) is that each share becomes specified; and the proofs (adilla) are from the Quran (verses indicating division of water, inheritance, and spoils), the Sunnah (the Prophet's practice in spoils and inheritances), and consensus. This is a foundational unit for understanding the Hanafi law of partition.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): Legally: collecting a common share in a specific thing, involving separation (ifraz) and exchange (mubadala).
الإفراز (al-ifrāz): The act of distinguishing and separating shares from common property.
المبادلة (al-mubādala): Exchange; in qisma, the implicit exchange of shares when the property is divided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The payment of a fee

Key Takeaway
Division (qisma) in Hanafi law is both a separation and an exchange; its validity depends on a proper act (rukn), a request (sabab), and preservation of benefit (shart).

The Dual Nature of Qisma: Ifraz and Mubadala in the Hanafi School
Explains that qisma comprises both ifraz and mubadala, with views of other schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ifraz and mubadala in qisma and state the Hanafi position on when each dominates, citing the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Qisma is both separation and exchange — which aspect dominates depends on whether the property is fungible and uniform.

When partners divide jointly owned property, is it simply marking what belongs to each, or does each partner effectively exchange their share in one part for the other's share in another? The Hanafi school teaches that both processes occur simultaneously, but their weight varies. Understanding this distinction is not just theoretical—it determines when a partner can take his share without the other's presence, and whether a reluctant partner can be compelled to divide. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position and contrasts it with the views of Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of qisma
The text states that qisma includes both ifraz (separation/distinguishing) and mubadala (exchange). First, understand what each term means in the context of dividing joint property.
2
Analyze the Hanafi rationale for both components
The passage argues that every specific part of joint property contains shares of both partners. When one partner receives his allotted piece, he gives up his right in the other piece. This is an exchange in substance, but since he also takes his own share, it is a separation. Follow this reasoning closely.
This is a rational analysis (qiyas khafi).
3
Distinguish between uniform and non-uniform property
The Hanafi view (and Malik's) distinguishes: when the property is of one genus and description (e.g., wheat of same grade), the parts are identical, so the exchange aspect is nominal—dominant is ifraz. When items differ (e.g., livestock of different kinds), exchange becomes real.
4
Contrast with Shafi'i and Hanbali views
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that qisma is pure ifraz in all cases; a reported Shafi'i view calls it a sale. Identify the key difference and how it leads to practical consequences.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome: for fungibles (mithli), a partner may take his share in the absence of the other because it is predominantly ifraz; for non-fungibles (qimi), he may not because exchange requires consent. Also, compulsion applies when the property is uniform.
The text explicitly mentions this consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الإفراز والمبادلة: يعني أن القسمة تشتمل على معنى الإفراز وهو التمييز، وعلى معنى المبادلة؛ لأنه ما من جزء معين إلا وهو مشتمل على النصيبين، فكان ما يجتمع في نصيب أحدهما بعضه كان له وبعضه كان لصاحبه، فصار له عوضا عما في يد صاحبه فكان مبادلة من حيث أنه أخذ حق صاحبه في مقابلة حقه وإفرازا من حيث أنه يقبض حقه أيضا، هذا عندنا وعند مالك فيما اتحد جنسا وصفة ،إفراز، وفي غيره ،مبادلة، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: إفراز في الكل، وعن الشافعي: بيع في الكل. (مسكين، عيني) وهو الظاهر أي الإفراز هو الظاهر في المثلي كالمكيل والموزون والعددي المتقارب؛ لعدم التفاوت بين أبعاضه؛ لأن ما يأخذه كل واحد منهما من نصيب شريكه مثل حقه صورة ومعنى، فأمكن أن يجعل عين حقه، ولهذا جعل عين حقه في القرض والصرف والسلم. (كذا في الزيلعي والفتح)
في المثلي، فيأخذ حظه حال غيبة صاحبه، وهي في غيره، فلا يأخذ، ويجبر في متحد أحد الشريكين أي نصيبه
وهو الشريك الآخر
الآبي من الشركاء
On separation and exchange: meaning that division includes the meaning of separation (ifraz), which is distinguishing, and the meaning of exchange (mubadala); because there is no specific part except that it contains two shares. So what comes together in the share of one of them is partly his and partly his companion's, so it becomes for him compensation for what is in his companion's hand. Thus it is an exchange in that he takes his companion's right in exchange for his right, and a separation in that he also takes his right. This is with us (Hanafis) and with Malik: when the genus and description are uniform, it is separation; otherwise, it is exchange. With al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: it is separation in all cases, and from al-Shafi'i: it is a sale in all cases. (Miskeen, Ayni). And the apparent: meaning that separation is apparent in fungible items (mithli) like measured, weighed, and roughly countable items, due to the lack of disparity between their parts; because what each takes from his partner's share is like his right in form and meaning, so it is possible to make it the very essence of his right. For this reason, it is made the very essence of his right in qard, sarf, and salam. (Thus in al-Zayla'i and al-Fath). In fungible items, he takes his share in the absence of his partner; in non-fungible items, he does not take. And compulsion is applied in uniform genus when one of the two partners requests it.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage delves into the dual nature of qisma: it is both a separation (ifraz) and an exchange (mubadala). The Hanafi position is that when the property is of uniform type and quality (e.g., wheat of the same grade), the dominant aspect is separation because each part is identical to the other. In such cases, division is essentially a designation of each partner's pre-existing share. When the items differ (e.g., a horse and a cow), division involves exchange because partners are swapping different values. The passage contrasts this with the views of Imam Malik (who makes the same distinction), Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad (who consider all division as pure separation), and a reported Shafi'i view that it is a sale. The practical effect of this classification is significant: if division is separation, a partner can take his share in the absence of the other; if it is exchange, he cannot. This difference is articulated in the following lines: in mithli (fungible) items, a partner may take his share without the other's presence; in non-mithli, he may not. The passage then transitions to the rule of compulsion in division: when the item is of one genus, a reluctant partner can be compelled to divide.

Key Terms
الإفراز (al-ifraz): Separation or distinguishing; the act of marking out each partner's share from the joint property.
المبادلة (al-mubadala): Exchange; in qisma, the implied swap of interests between partners.
المثلي (al-mithlī): Fungible items with no significant individual variation, e.g., measured, weighed, or roughly countable goods.
القيمي (al-qimī): Non-fungible items with unique value, e.g., land, animals, unique artifacts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, when does division predominantly have the character of separation (ifraz)?
A) When the property is non-fungible (qimi)
B) When the property is fungible and of uniform type (mithli)
C) When the partners differ in their shares
D) When division is ordered by a judge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies qisma as both ifraz and mubadala; the dominant aspect depends on whether the property is uniform and fungible, with practical consequences for unilateral division and compulsion.

Compulsion in Division: The Rule of Uniform Genus
States ruling on compulsion in division based on genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on compulsion in division and identify the condition of uniform genus that triggers compulsion.

Core Idea
Division can be compelled only if the property is of one genus — mixed genus requires mutual consent.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes a key rule in Hanafi fiqh regarding joint property: when can a partner be forced to divide? The answer hinges on whether the property is of a single type (uniform genus) or mixed. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying compulsion (jabr) in sales and divisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is division (qisma) of jointly owned property. The ruling is that a reluctant partner can be compelled (jabr) by a judge under specific conditions.
Compulsion is not always available; it depends on the nature of the property.
2
Locate the condition for compulsion
The condition is that the property must be of a single genus (muttaḥid al-jins). Examples: all wheat, all sheep. The text says: 'ويجبر في متحد الجنس'.
Uniformity of genus is the key factor.
3
Understand the rationale via qiyas
The ruling is analogized to a debtor being compelled to sell his property to pay a debt. Since division in uniform genus involves an exchange of equivalent values, compulsion is justified when a right of another (the co-owner) is attached.
This is a clear analogy (qiyas jaliy).
4
Apply the condition: mixed genus
If the property consists of different genera (e.g., a camel and a sheep), compulsion is not applied because equivalence is impossible due to gross disparity in purposes.
Mutual agreement is still permissible.
5
Note the exception: possession for fungibles
A partner may take his share in the absence of the other only if the fungible item (al-mithli) is already in the joint possession of both partners. If not yet possessed, he cannot take without permission.
This is a branch from the main rule.

Source Text

ويجبر في متحد أحد الشريكين أي نصيبه
وهو الشريك الآخر
الآبي من الشركاء
الجنس عند طلب أحد الشركاء لا في غيره، وندب نصب قاسم رزقه
كالغنم والثياب والصوف مثلا

المال؛ ليقسم بلا أجر، وإلا فينصب قاسم يقسم بأجر بعدد الرؤوس،
بين الناس
لا على قدر ا
الأنصباء
فيأخذ حظه إلخ: تفريع على كون القسمة في المثلي إفرازا وتمييزا لحظه، لكن محله إذا كان المثلي مقبوضا للشريكين، أما إذا كان غير مقبوض فلا، كذا في "الحموي" عن "الشلبي". (فتح) وهي في غيره إلخ: أي المبادلة الظاهرة في غير المثلي للتفاوت، فلا يمكن أن يجعل كأنه أخذ عين حقه؛ لعدم المعادلة بينهما بيقين، وإذا كان كذلك، فليس لأحدهما أن يأخذ حظه في غيبة صاحبه. (فتح) ويجبر في متحد الجنس إلخ أي إذا طلب بعض الشركاء القسمة يجبر الآبي على القسمة في متحد الجنس، سواء كان في ذوات الأمثال أو لا؛ لأن فيه معنى المبادلة والمبادلة يجري فيها الجبر إذا تعلق بها حق الغير كالمديون يجبر على ملكه لإيفاء الدين. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٢٨٩/٨] لا في غيره إلخ: أي لا يجبر في غير متحد الجنس بيع كالغنم مع الإبل؛ لتعذر المعادلة باعتبار فحش التفاوت في المقاصد، ولو توافقوا جاز؛ لأن الحق لهم. (فتح)
And compulsion is applied in uniform genus when one of the two partners requests it, not otherwise. And it is recommended to appoint a divider whose sustenance is from the public treasury, so that he divides without fee; otherwise, a divider is appointed who divides for a fee, distributed equally per head, not according to the shares. So regarding 'he takes his share' etc.: this is a branch on the basis that division in fungible items is separation and distinguishing of his share, but its place is when the fungible item is possessed by the two partners; if it is not possessed, then no. Thus in al-Hamwi from al-Shalabi. (Fath). And 'it in other than it' etc.: i.e., exchange is apparent in non-fungible items due to disparity, so it cannot be considered as if he took the very essence of his right; because of the lack of equivalence between them with certainty. When this is the case, it is not permissible for one of them to take his share in the absence of his partner. (Fath). And 'compulsion is applied in uniform genus' etc.: i.e., if some partners request division, the refusing partner is compelled to divide if the genus is uniform, whether in fungible items or not; because in it there is the meaning of exchange, and compulsion applies to exchange when the right of another is attached to it, like the debtor is compelled to sell his property to pay a debt. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/289). 'Not in other than it' etc.: i.e., compulsion is not applied in non-uniform genus, like sheep with camels; because equivalence is impossible due to gross disparity in purposes. If they mutually agree, it is permissible because it is their right. (Fath).
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi rule on compulsion (jabr) in division. If the property to be divided is of a single genus (e.g., all wheat, all sheep), a partner who refuses to divide can be compelled by a judge upon the request of another partner. This is because division in such cases involves an exchange of equal values, and compulsion is analogous to forcing a debtor to sell property to satisfy a debt. However, if the property consists of different genera (e.g., a camel and a sheep), compulsion is not applied because the exchange would involve disparate values that cannot be fairly equated. In that case, division is only with mutual consent. The passage also clarifies the previous discussion on fungible items: a partner may take his share in the other's absence only if the item is already in the joint possession of both partners. If it is not in their possession (e.g., a debt owed to them), he cannot take it without the other's permission. This ruling is based on the principle that division in fungibles is separation (ifraz), but that separation requires actual possession.

Key Terms
الجبر (al-jabr): Compulsion applied by a judge to force a reluctant partner to participate in division.
متحد الجنس (muttaḥid al-jins): Property consisting of a single genus or category, e.g., all wheat or all sheep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case can a reluctant partner be compelled to divide joint property?
A) When the property is of multiple genera
B) When the property is of a single genus
C) When one partner is absent
D) When the property is non-fungible

Key Takeaway
Compulsion in division is wajib only when the property is of one genus; mixed genus requires mutual consent.
